Definition of al-Qadir and Motives
Defines al-qadir and explains the concept of motives (dawāʿī) in human action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-Qadir and how motives (dawāʿī) explain human action and inaction.

Core Idea
Capability is not absolute; it depends on motives.

This passage from al-Matālib al-ʿĀliya lays the groundwork for understanding divine and human agency. Before we can discuss God's power, we must first understand what it means to be a capable agent (al-qādir) in general. The author provides a precise definition and then unpacks the concept of motives (dawāʿī) that drive action or refraining.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's precise style.
The passage starts with 'المقدمات الأولي'.
2
Identify the definition of al-qadir
Find the definition given by the author. Notice that it links capability to possibility of acting or not acting.
The definition uses 'يصح منه أن يفعل تارة، وأن لا يفعل أخرى'.
3
Understand the three cognitive states
The author mentions three states: knowledge (علم), conjecture (ظن), and belief (اعتقاد). These give rise to motives.
These are the sources of decisive inclination or aversion.
4
Trace the path from motive to action
Follow the causal chain: cognitive state → decisive inclination/aversion → action/inaction (if limbs are sound).
The condition of sound limbs is crucial.
5
Grasp the meaning of al-dāʿī
The passage concludes that this whole cognitive process is what is meant by motive.
Motive is not just a feeling but a cognitive judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمات الأولي في حد القادر : أجود ما قيل فيه : أنه الذي يصح منه أن يفعل تارة ، وأن لا يفعل أخرى ، بحسب الدواعي المختلفة . وتفسير الدواعي : هو أن الإنسان إذا علم أو ظن ، أو اعتقد، أن له في الفعل الفلاني مصلحة راجحة . فعند حصول أحد هذه الثلاثة ، يحصل في قلبه ميل جازم إلى الفعل ( فإن كانت أعضاؤه سليمة فإن عند حصول ذلك الميل في قلبه يصدر عنه ذلك الفعل ((١) وأما إن علم ، أو ظن ، أو اعتقد أن له في الفعل الفلاني مفسدة راجحة ، فعند حصول هذا العلم ، أو الاعتقاد ، أو الظن ، يحصل في قلبه نفرة جازمة عن ذلك الفعل، فإن كانت أعضاؤه سليمة ، ترتب على حصول تلك النفرة ، مع سلامة الأعضاء : الترك. وهذا هو المراد بالداعي
First introductions: On the definition of al-Qadir. The best thing said about it is: that it is he from whom it is possible to do at one time and not to do at another, according to different motives (dawāʿī). And the explanation of motives (dawāʿī) is: that when a person knows, or thinks, or believes that there is a predominant benefit in a certain action, then upon the occurrence of one of these three, a decisive inclination occurs in his heart towards the action. Then if his limbs are sound, upon the occurrence of that inclination in his heart, that action issues from him. But if he knows, or thinks, or believes that there is a predominant harm in that action, then upon the occurrence of this knowledge, or belief, or thinking, there occurs in his heart a decisive aversion from that action. Then if his limbs are sound, upon the occurrence of that aversion together with soundness of limbs, there follows: leaving. And this is what is meant by al-dāʿī (the motive).
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'al-qadir' (the capable/powerful agent) and then explains the concept of 'al-dawāʿī' (motives) that underlie human action. The passage begins by stating the best definition of al-qadir: it is the one from whom it is possible to perform an action at one time and to refrain from it at another, depending on different motives. This definition emphasizes that capability is not absolute but relative to motives. Then it explains what motives are: when a person has knowledge, strong conjecture (ẓann), or belief that a certain action contains a predominant benefit, one of these three cognitive states gives rise to a decisive inclination (mayl jāzim) in the heart towards the action. If the limbs are sound, the action follows from that inclination. Conversely, if the person knows, thinks, or believes that the action contains a predominant harm, this cognitive state causes a decisive aversion (nufra jāzima) from the action, leading to leaving it (tark) if the limbs are sound. The passage concludes that this cognitive state—whether it leads to inclination or aversion—is what is meant by 'al-dāʿī' (the motive). The argument follows the order: definition of qadir, then definition of motive, then explanation of how motive produces action or inaction.

Key Terms
قادر
داعي
ميل جازم
نفرة جازمة
مصلحة راجحة
مفسدة راجحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that qadir means absolute power to do anything regardless of motives.
Correction: The definition ties capability to the presence of motives: it is the one from whom it is possible to act or not act according to motives.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the best definition of al-qadir?
A) He who always acts.
B) He from whom it is possible to act or not act according to different motives.
C) He who has complete power over everything.
D) He who acts without any motive.

Key Takeaway
The passage establishes that a capable agent is one who can act or refrain based on motives, which are cognitive states producing inclination or aversion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself procrastinating or avoiding a difficult task, do you honestly examine the motives (dawāʿī) in your heart? Are you aware of the beliefs or assumptions that create a decisive aversion? What would it take to replace that aversion with an inclination toward the beneficial action?
?
Think of a sinful habit you struggle with. According to this lesson, your actions follow from a cognitive state—a belief that there is a predominant benefit in that sin. What specific false belief (ʿilm, ẓann, or iʿtiqād) is driving that inclination? Will you confess that lie to Allah and seek to replace it with the truth revealed in His Book?
?
If you truly believe that your capability (qudra) as a human is tied to your motives, then every action you do or don't do is a mirror of your heart's orientation. How can you ignore the state of your heart when you claim to desire righteousness? What concrete step will you take today to cultivate a decisive inclination toward an act of obedience you've been avoiding?

The Four Possibilities on Power and Motive
Presents four positions on how power and motive together produce action, defends the first view, and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four possible causal relations between power and motive in producing an action, and why the author holds that the sum of both is the effective cause.

Core Idea
Power and motive together cause action; each alone is insufficient.

This passage presents the second investigation of the discussion on causality. It outlines four logical possibilities for how power (qudra) and motive (dāʿī) interact to produce an action. After enumerating them, the author argues for the first view—that the combination of both is the effective cause—and defends it against an objection that might arise from a definitional misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle and the four possibilities
Begin by reading the opening principle: when motive calls, action necessarily occurs. Then read the four enumerated possibilities carefully. Notice the precise phrasing for each.
The Arabic uses 'أحدها', 'ثانيها', etc. for the four.
2
Identify the author's preferred view and its evidence
After the enumeration, the author states 'الأقرب أن يقال...' and provides evidence. Identify the claim and the reasoning that power alone and motive alone are insufficient, but together they produce the effect.
3
Study the objection and the author's answer
Read the objection that if power is defined as capacity to effect, then saying power alone is unsuitable is contradictory. Then see how the author redefines power to resolve the contradiction.
This shows a key definitional move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني : إنه إذا دعاه الداعي إلى الفعل حصل الفعل لا محالة . ثم ههنا احتمالات. أحدها : أن المؤثر في حصول الفعل هو مجموع القدرة مع الداعي . وثانيها : أن المؤثر فيه هو القدرة بشرط الداعي . وثالثها : أن الداعي علة تامة ، تصير قدرة القادر علّة تامة الحصول الفعل . ولهذا يقال : العملة الغائية علة تامة لعلة العملة الفاعلية. ورابعها : إن حصول القدرة ، والداعي سبب الحصول الاستعداد التام ، لخروم ذلك الفعل من العدم إلى الوجود . فأما نفس ذلك الحصول والحدوث، فمن الأسباب العلوية . فهذه الاحتمالات الأربعة لا بد من بيانها : ونقول : الأقرب أن يقال : المؤثر في الفعل هو مجموع القدرة مع الداعي . والدليل عليه : أن القدرة وحدها غير صالحة للتأثير - على ما سب بيانه ، عند تقرير أنه عند عدم الداعي يمتنع الفعل - ومعلوم أيضاً : أن الداعي وحده غير صالح للتأثير . وأما عند اجتماعهما ، فإنه يحصل الأثر . فعلمنا أن المؤثر هو المجموع . فإن قالوا : قولكم القدرة وحدها غير صالحة للتأثير ، قول ) باطل لأن القدرة عبارة عما يكون له صلاحية التأثير ، فقولكم : القدرة وحدها غير صالحة للتأثير قول ) (۲) بأن القدرة ليست مؤثرة. وهذا متناقض والجواب : إنا نعني بالقدرة كون الأعضاء بحالة متى انضم الداعي إليها حصل الفعل . وإذا كان المراد بالقدرة ذلك . فقد زال السؤال .
The second investigation: When the motive (al-dāʿī) calls to the action, the action occurs necessarily. Then there are probabilities (iḥtimālāt) here. One of them: that the effect (al-muʾaththir) in the occurrence of the action is the sum of power (al-qudra) along with the motive. Second: that the effect in it is power conditioned by the motive. Third: that the motive is a complete cause (ʿilla tāmma) which makes the power of the capable agent a complete cause for the occurrence of the action. For this reason it is said: the final cause is a complete cause for the cause of the efficient cause. Fourth: that the occurrence of power and motive is a cause for the acquisition of complete preparedness (istiʿdād tāmm) for the emergence of that action from non-existence to existence. As for the actual occurrence and happening, it is from the higher causes (al-asbāb al-ʿulwiyya). These four probabilities must be explained. And we say: the closest (al-aqrab) is to say that the effect in the action is the sum of power along with the motive. And the evidence for it: that power alone is not suitable for effect, as previously explained in the establishment that when the motive is absent the action is impossible; and it is also known that the motive alone is not suitable for effect. But when they are combined, the effect occurs. So we know that the effect is the sum. Then if they say: your statement 'power alone is not suitable for effect' is false, because power is an expression for that which has the capacity (ṣalāḥiyya) for effect, so your statement 'power alone is not suitable for effect' is a statement that power is not effective, and this is contradictory. And the answer: we mean by power the state of the limbs such that when the motive is added to it, the action occurs. And when the meaning of power is that, then the question is removed.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is the second investigation from the earlier discussion. It begins by stating a key principle: when the motive calls to an action, the action necessarily occurs. Then it presents four possible positions (iḥtimālāt) regarding the causal role of power (qudra) and motive (dāʿī) in producing the action. The four are: (1) The effect is the sum (majmūʿ) of power and motive. (2) The effect is power conditioned (bi-sharṭ) by the motive. (3) The motive is a complete cause that makes the power of the agent a complete cause for the action; thus the final cause (motiv) is a complete cause for the efficient cause (the agent's power). (4) Power and motive together cause complete preparedness (istiʿdād tāmm) for the action, but the actual occurrence is from higher causes (al-asbāb al-ʿulwiyya, possibly celestial or divine causes). After listing these four, the author states that the closest (al-aqrab) view is the first: the effect is the sum of power and motive. The evidence is that power alone is insufficient for effect (as previously established by showing that without motive action is impossible), and motive alone is also insufficient. But when they combine, the effect occurs. Hence the effect is the sum. Then an objection is raised: if power is defined as having the capacity to effect, then saying it is alone not suitable for effect is contradictory because it implies power is not effective. The author answers: we define power not as inherent effectiveness but as the state of the limbs such that when the motive is added, the action occurs. So defined, the objection dissolves. The passage follows the order: principle, four possibilities, author's preferred view with evidence, objection, and answer.

Key Terms
داعي
قدرة
مؤثر
علة تامة
استعداد تام
أسباب علوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If power is defined as having capacity to effect, then it must be sufficient alone to produce action.
Correction: Power is defined here as the state of limbs ready for action when motive is present; it is not inherently effective by itself.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the author's preferred view on how power and motive produce action?
A) Power alone suffices.
B) The motive alone suffices.
C) The effect is the sum of power and motive.
D) Power and motive cause complete preparedness, but the action is from higher causes.

Key Takeaway
The effective cause of action is the combination of power and motive; each alone is insufficient, and this definition of power as a conditional capacity resolves the objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you tend to rely on your own power (ability) alone without cultivating the right motives (dāʿī) for your actions? How does this lesson challenge you to ensure both are aligned for righteous deeds?
?
When you fail to act upon a good intention, which of the two—power or motive—is more often missing? What prevents you from combining them to produce the action you know you should do?
?
The author defines power as a conditional capacity that only becomes effective when joined with motive. If this is true, then excuses that 'I have no power' are often masks for lacking true motive. Where in your life have you used 'I can't' to avoid 'I won't'?

The Four Possibilities for What Produces an Action
Enumeration of four possibilities for the cause of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four distinct views on what combination of factors brings about an action.

Core Idea
An action requires both power and motive, but how they combine is a matter of debate.

The author begins this investigation by listing four possible ways to understand the relationship between power (al-qudra) and motive (al-da'i) in causing an action. These theories range from viewing the combination of both as the cause to seeing the actual coming-into-being as from higher causes. This lesson will help you grasp each of these four positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration of the four possibilities
Read the Arabic passage listing the four possibilities once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get a general sense.
The possibilities are numbered one through four.
2
Identify the key difference between possibility 1 and 2
Notice that possibility 1 says the effect is the combination of power and motive, while possibility 2 says the effect is power conditioned by motive. Understand the subtle distinction: in possibility 2, power is still the primary effect but with an added condition.
3
Understand the third possibility's reference to final cause
Possibility 3 introduces the idea of the motive as a complete cause (علة تامة) that makes power a complete cause. The author notes that this is why the final cause is said to be complete cause for the cause of the efficient cause.
This is a deeper philosophical concept from Aristotelian causation.
4
Note the fourth possibility's distinction between preparedness and actual occurrence
Possibility 4 separates the preparation (complete preparedness) caused by power and motive from the actual coming-into-being, which is attributed to higher causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني: إنه إذا دعاه الداعي إلى الفعل حصل الفعل لا محالة. ثم ههنا احتمالات. أحدها: أن المؤثر في حصول الفعل هو مجموع القدرة مع الداعي. وثانيها: أن المؤثر فيه هو القدرة بشرط الداعي. وثالثها: أن الداعي علة تامة، تصير قدرة القادر علّة تامة لحصول الفعل. ولهذا يقال: العلة الغائية علة تامة لعلة العلة الفاعلية. ورابعها: إن حصول القدرة، والداعي سبب لحصول الاستعداد التام، لخروج ذلك الفعل من العدم إلى الوجود. فأما نفس ذلك الحصول والحدوث، فمن الأسباب العلوية. فهذه الاحتمالات الأربعة لا بد من بيانها: ونقول: الأقرب أن يقال: المؤثر في الفعل هو مجموع القدرة مع الداعي. والدليل عليه: أن القدرة وحدها غير صالحة للتأثير - على ما سب بيانه، عند تقرير أنه عند عدم الداعي يمتنع الفعل - ومعلوم أيضاً: أن الداعي وحده غير صالح للتأثير. وأما عند اجتماعهما، فإنه يحصل الأثر. فعلمنا أن المؤثر هو المجموع. فإن قالوا: قولكم القدرة وحدها غير صالحة للتأثير، قول باطل لأن القدرة عبارة عما يكون له صلاحية التأثير، فقولكم: القدرة وحدها غير صالحة للتأثير قول بأن القدرة ليست مؤثرة. وهذا متناقض. والجواب: إنا نعني بالقدرة كون الأعضاء بحالة متى انضم الداعي إليها حصل الفعل. وإذا كان المراد بالقدرة ذلك فقد زال السؤال. فإن قيل: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: المؤثر هو القدرة إلا أن انضمام الداعي إليها شرط لصدور ذلك الأثر عن القدرة؟ والفرق بين أجزاء العلة وبين ما يكون شرطاً لتأثير العلة في المعلول: معلوم. والجواب: إنا لا نعني بالمصدر والمؤثر إلا مجموع الأمور التي لا بد من حصولها حتى تصير مصدراً لذلك الأثر. وعلى هذا التقدير فلا فرق عندنا في القيود الوجودية بين أن يقال: إنها أجزاء العلة وبين أن يقال إنها شرائط لصدور الأثر عن العلة. واعلم أن بهذا القدر الذي ذكرناه لا يظهر أن الحق من تلك الاحتمالات الأربعة: ماذا؟
Second Investigation: When the motive calls him to act, the action necessarily occurs. Then here there are possibilities. First: The effect in producing the action is the combination of power (al-qudra) and the motive. Second: The effect is power conditioned by the motive. Third: The motive is a complete cause, making the agent's power a complete cause for the occurrence of the action. Hence it is said: the final cause is a complete cause for the cause of the efficient cause. Fourth: The occurrence of power and motive is the cause of the complete preparedness for the emergence of that action from non-existence to existence. As for that very occurrence and coming-to-be, it is from the higher causes. So these four possibilities must be explained. We say: The closest is to say: The effect in the action is the combination of power with the motive. The proof is: Power alone is not fit for effect—as previously explained when establishing that in the absence of motive action is impossible—and also it is known that motive alone is not fit for effect. But when they combine, the effect occurs. So we know that the effect is the combination. If they say: Your statement 'power alone is not fit for effect' is false because power is defined as that which has the capacity for effect, so your statement that power alone is not fit for effect is a statement that power is not effective. This is contradictory. The answer: We mean by power the state of the limbs such that when the motive attaches to them the action occurs. When power is meant thus, the question is removed. If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that the effect is power, but the attachment of the motive to it is a condition for the issuance of that effect from power? The difference between parts of a cause and what is a condition for the effect of the cause on the effect is known. The answer: We do not mean by source and effect except the totality of matters that must be obtained until they become a source for that effect. On this estimation, there is no difference for us among existential restrictions between saying they are parts of the cause and saying they are conditions for the issuance of the effect from the cause. Know that with this amount we have mentioned, it is not apparent which of those four possibilities is true?
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed investigation into the cause of action. The author asserts that when a motive (da'i) is present, the action inevitably occurs. He then lists four theories: (1) The combination of power (qudra) and motive is the effect. (2) Power is the effect but conditioned by the motive. (3) The motive is the complete cause, making power also a complete cause. (4) Power and motive together cause complete preparedness, while the actual coming-into-being is from higher causes. The author argues the first is closest: neither alone suffices, but together they produce action. He addresses an objection: if power is defined as that which can effect, then saying power alone cannot effect contradicts its definition. He responds by redefining power as a state of the limbs that, when motive joins, results in action. Then another objection: why not say power is the effect but motive is a condition? He answers that he does not distinguish between parts of a cause and conditions; all existential requisites are equally necessary. He concludes that this discussion does not yet reveal which of the four is actually true.

Key Terms
القدرة
الداعي
العلة التامة
الاستعداد التام
الأسباب العلوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Each possibility can be combined arbitrarily or that the fourth does not require power and motive.
Correction: The passage clearly distinguishes four distinct theories; each has a specific logical structure. The fourth theory still requires power and motive to produce preparedness, but the actual existence comes from higher causes.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth possibility, what produces the actual coming-into-being of an action?
A) The combination of power and motive
B) The power alone
C) The higher causes
D) The complete preparedness

Key Takeaway
There are four distinct views on how power and motive produce an action, ranging from direct combination to preparation by higher causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now understand that an action requires both power and motive. Think of a sin you committed despite knowing it was wrong. Was your motive truly absent, or did you suppress your conscience? What does this lesson force you to admit about the state of your heart before that action, and what will you do differently the next time a similar motive arises?
?
The fourth possibility states that the actual coming-to-be of an action is from higher causes. Does this make you feel less accountable for your actions? Search your soul: do you ever use divine decree as an excuse to avoid responsibility? How does this lesson compel you to examine that tendency and to own your actions fully today?
?
If the motive can be a complete cause (third possibility), then your inner desires are not neutral – they can be the driving force of your actions. What secret motive have you been nurturing that you know leads to disobedience? Will you uproot it this week, and what specific step will you take?

The Author's Proof for the Combined Effect
Author's argument for the combination of power and motive as the cause of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that the combination of power and motive is the direct cause of action.

Core Idea
Neither power alone nor motive alone produces action; only together do they create the effect.

After listing the four possibilities, the author states that the closest to truth is the first: the combination of power and motive. He supports this with a simple but powerful proof: each alone is insufficient, but together they produce the action. This lesson examines that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's preferred view and proof
Read the Arabic passage from 'ونقول: الأقرب أن يقال' to 'فعلمنا أن المؤثر هو المجموع'. Understand the claim and the structure of the proof.
2
Understand why power alone is insufficient
The author claims power alone is not fit for effect, citing the earlier conclusion that without motive, action is impossible.
This was established earlier: 'عند عدم الداعي يمتنع الفعل'.
3
Understand why motive alone is insufficient
It is also known that motive alone cannot produce action without power.
4
See how their combination necessarily results in the action
When both are present, the effect occurs. This observation leads to the conclusion that the combination is the cause.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني: إنه إذا دعاه الداعي إلى الفعل حصل الفعل لا محالة. ثم ههنا احتمالات. أحدها: أن المؤثر في حصول الفعل هو مجموع القدرة مع الداعي. وثانيها: أن المؤثر فيه هو القدرة بشرط الداعي. وثالثها: أن الداعي علة تامة، تصير قدرة القادر علّة تامة لحصول الفعل. ولهذا يقال: العلة الغائية علة تامة لعلة العلة الفاعلية. ورابعها: إن حصول القدرة، والداعي سبب لحصول الاستعداد التام، لخروج ذلك الفعل من العدم إلى الوجود. فأما نفس ذلك الحصول والحدوث، فمن الأسباب العلوية. فهذه الاحتمالات الأربعة لا بد من بيانها: ونقول: الأقرب أن يقال: المؤثر في الفعل هو مجموع القدرة مع الداعي. والدليل عليه: أن القدرة وحدها غير صالحة للتأثير - على ما سب بيانه، عند تقرير أنه عند عدم الداعي يمتنع الفعل - ومعلوم أيضاً: أن الداعي وحده غير صالح للتأثير. وأما عند اجتماعهما، فإنه يحصل الأثر. فعلمنا أن المؤثر هو المجموع. فإن قالوا: قولكم القدرة وحدها غير صالحة للتأثير، قول باطل لأن القدرة عبارة عما يكون له صلاحية التأثير، فقولكم: القدرة وحدها غير صالحة للتأثير قول بأن القدرة ليست مؤثرة. وهذا متناقض. والجواب: إنا نعني بالقدرة كون الأعضاء بحالة متى انضم الداعي إليها حصل الفعل. وإذا كان المراد بالقدرة ذلك فقد زال السؤال. فإن قيل: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: المؤثر هو القدرة إلا أن انضمام الداعي إليها شرط لصدور ذلك الأثر عن القدرة؟ والفرق بين أجزاء العلة وبين ما يكون شرطاً لتأثير العلة في المعلول: معلوم. والجواب: إنا لا نعني بالمصدر والمؤثر إلا مجموع الأمور التي لا بد من حصولها حتى تصير مصدراً لذلك الأثر. وعلى هذا التقدير فلا فرق عندنا في القيود الوجودية بين أن يقال: إنها أجزاء العلة وبين أن يقال إنها شرائط لصدور الأثر عن العلة. واعلم أن بهذا القدر الذي ذكرناه لا يظهر أن الحق من تلك الاحتمالات الأربعة: ماذا؟
Second Investigation: When the motive calls him to act, the action necessarily occurs. Then here there are possibilities. First: The effect in producing the action is the combination of power (al-qudra) and the motive. Second: The effect is power conditioned by the motive. Third: The motive is a complete cause, making the agent's power a complete cause for the occurrence of the action. Hence it is said: the final cause is a complete cause for the cause of the efficient cause. Fourth: The occurrence of power and motive is the cause of the complete preparedness for the emergence of that action from non-existence to existence. As for that very occurrence and coming-to-be, it is from the higher causes. So these four possibilities must be explained. We say: The closest is to say: The effect in the action is the combination of power with the motive. The proof is: Power alone is not fit for effect—as previously explained when establishing that in the absence of motive action is impossible—and also it is known that motive alone is not fit for effect. But when they combine, the effect occurs. So we know that the effect is the combination. If they say: Your statement 'power alone is not fit for effect' is false because power is defined as that which has the capacity for effect, so your statement that power alone is not fit for effect is a statement that power is not effective. This is contradictory. The answer: We mean by power the state of the limbs such that when the motive attaches to them the action occurs. When power is meant thus, the question is removed. If it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that the effect is power, but the attachment of the motive to it is a condition for the issuance of that effect from power? The difference between parts of a cause and what is a condition for the effect of the cause on the effect is known. The answer: We do not mean by source and effect except the totality of matters that must be obtained until they become a source for that effect. On this estimation, there is no difference for us among existential restrictions between saying they are parts of the cause and saying they are conditions for the issuance of the effect from the cause. Know that with this amount we have mentioned, it is not apparent which of those four possibilities is true?
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed investigation into the cause of action. The author asserts that when a motive (da'i) is present, the action inevitably occurs. He then lists four theories: (1) The combination of power (qudra) and motive is the effect. (2) Power is the effect but conditioned by the motive. (3) The motive is the complete cause, making power also a complete cause. (4) Power and motive together cause complete preparedness, while the actual coming-into-being is from higher causes. The author argues the first is closest: neither alone suffices, but together they produce action. He addresses an objection: if power is defined as that which can effect, then saying power alone cannot effect contradicts its definition. He responds by redefining power as a state of the limbs that, when motive joins, results in action. Then another objection: why not say power is the effect but motive is a condition? He answers that he does not distinguish between parts of a cause and conditions; all existential requisites are equally necessary. He concludes that this discussion does not yet reveal which of the four is actually true.

Key Terms
القدرة
الداعي
المؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That power alone is sufficient to produce an action, as some might think power is the sole cause.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that power alone is not fit for effect, citing the impossibility of action without motive.

Checkpoint
What is the author's conclusion about the cause of an action?
A) Power alone is the cause
B) Motive alone is the cause
C) The combination of power and motive is the cause
D) The cause is unknown

Key Takeaway
The combination of power and motive is the necessary and sufficient cause for an action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know you have the ability (power) to pray on time, yet you often delay. What does this lesson reveal about what is missing in your heart? Will you ask Allah today to grant you both the ability and the sincere motive to fulfill this act?
?
Think of a sin you struggle to leave. Is it because you lack the willpower or because your desire for it is still strong? According to this passage, neither power nor motive alone suffices. Which one must you work on first? What concrete step will you take right now to weaken the false motive?
?
If you truly believe that only when power and motive combine does action occur, then how should you change your dua when you ask Allah for help in obeying Him? Will you now ask for both strength and a love for obedience? Commit to a specific dua tonight.

Defending the Combined Effect Against Objections
Objections and responses regarding the combined effect view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends his view by addressing two key objections.

Core Idea
The author redefines power and cause to clarify that existential requirements are inseparable from the cause.

The author's claim that power alone cannot produce action invites an objection: if power is by definition effective, saying it is ineffective is contradictory. The author responds by redefining power. A second objection asks why we cannot distinguish between cause and condition; the author answers that in this context, there is no practical difference. This lesson walks through those objections and responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection and the author's response
Read the Arabic from 'فإن قالوا' to the end of that response. Understand the objection and how the author redefines power.
2
Understand the first objection
The objector says that if power is defined as that which can effect, then saying power alone cannot effect is contradictory.
3
Understand the author's answer to the first objection
The author redefines power as a state of the limbs that only results in action when motive is joined. This avoids the contradiction.
4
Read the second objection and the author's response
Read from 'فإن قيل' to the end. Understand why the distinction between cause part and condition is not relevant here.
5
Reflect on the concluding remark
The author says that with this discussion, it is not yet apparent which of the four possibilities is correct.

Objection: Is the Agent Really the Cause?
The author presents a philosophical objection about the source of the effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical objection that the effect of power and motive may come from a separate cause such as the Active Intellect, and the requirement to examine it.

Core Idea
The real cause might be the Active Intellect, not the agent's power and motive.

In this passage, the author raises a crucial objection from the philosophers: when a person has both the power and the motive to act, does the action truly come from that person, or does it emanate from a separate source—the Active Intellect? This objection challenges our everyday understanding of causality and forces us to examine the nature of agency. The author presents it neutrally, urging us to reflect and decide whether it is true or false.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the objection's structure
Read the passage and note that it begins with 'One might say' (لقائل أن يقول), signaling an objection from an opposing view.
The author is not stating his own view here.
2
Understand the philosophical view
Identify the two claims: 1) power and motive create complete preparedness (الاستعداد التام), 2) the effect then emanates from the Active Intellect (العقل الفعال).
This is the view of the philosophers (الفلاسفة).
3
Note the author's call for examination
The author concludes that this possibility must be contemplated to determine its truth or falsehood. This sets up the need for a response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثالث: لقائل أن يقول: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: أن عند حصول مجموع القدرة والداعي يصدر الأثر عن سبب منفصل، كما يقول الفلاسفة: من أن مجموع القدرة والداعي يوجب الاستعداد التام لحدوث ذلك الأثر، وعند حصول هذا الاستعداد التام يفيض الأثر عن العقل الفعال؟ ويجب أن يتأمل في هذا الاحتمال ليعلم أنه حق أو باطل
Third Investigation: One might say: Why is it not permissible to say that when the combination of power and motive is obtained, the effect issues from a separate cause, as the philosophers say: that the combination of power and motive necessitates complete preparedness for the occurrence of that effect, and when this complete preparedness is obtained, the effect emanates from the Active Intellect? One must contemplate this possibility to know whether it is true or false.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This short passage presents a third investigation: an objection from the philosophers. They propose that the combination of power and motive does not directly produce the action; rather, it creates a complete preparedness (isti'dad tam), and then the actual effect emanates from a separate cause—the Active Intellect (al-`aql al-fa``al). The author simply presents this view and says it must be examined to determine if it is true or false. He does not give an answer here, leaving it for further consideration.

Key Terms

العقل الفعال
الفيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author believes the Active Intellect is the real cause.
Correction: The author is merely presenting an objection from the philosophers; he does not endorse it.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers mentioned, what does the combination of power and motive cause?
A) The action itself.
B) Complete preparedness.
C) The will.
D) The motive.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers propose that the effect comes from the Active Intellect via complete preparedness, not from the agent's power and motive directly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision to act, do you truly believe that you are the one causing the action, or do you feel that something else (your circumstances, your nature, fate) is the real cause? How does this objection challenge your sense of personal responsibility before Allah?
?
If the effect of your actions ultimately comes from a separate cause beyond your control, does that diminish your accountability? How must you adjust your reliance on Allah while still acting as a responsible agent?
?
Contemplate the phrase 'complete preparedness'. Are you actively preparing yourself to receive divine guidance and support, or do you passively wait for effects to emanate? What one concrete step can you take today to better prepare your heart for the effects of faith?

No Real Distinction Between Parts and Conditions of a Cause
Author clarifies his view on cause and conditions, stating no distinction between parts and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's view that, within his definition, the difference between parts of a cause and conditions for its effect is merely terminological.

Core Idea
Cause, parts, and conditions all refer to the same set of necessary prerequisites.

The author tackles a subtle but important point in causal theory: Are the parts of a cause distinct from its conditions? Having introduced his definition of the cause as the totality of necessary matters, he argues that the distinction collapses. This passage sets the stage for evaluating four specific possibilities about causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to how the author defines his terms.
2
Identify the author's definitions
Notice what the author means by 'source' (مصدر) and 'effector' (مؤثر): the totality of matters necessary for the effect.
3
Understand the equivalence claim
On this definition, calling something a 'part' of the cause or a 'condition' for its effect makes no practical difference. Both are simply necessary prerequisites.
4
Reflect on the open question
The author notes that this general equivalence does not automatically resolve which of four specific possibilities is correct. This sets up a further investigation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفرق بين أجزاء العلة وبين ما يكن شرطاً لتأثير العلة في المعلول : معلوم . والجواب : إنا لا نعني بالمصدر والمؤثر إلا مجموع الأمور التي لا بد من حصولها حتى تصير مصدراً لذلك الأثر . وعلى هذا التقدير فلا فرق عندنا في القيود الوجودية بين أن يقال : إنها أجزاء العلة وبين أن يقال إنها شرائط لصدور الأثر عن العلة . واعلم أن بهذا القدر الذي ذكرناه لا يظهر أن الحق من تلك الاحتمالات الأربعة : ماذا ؟
And the difference between the parts of the cause and what is a condition for the effect of the cause upon the effect: it is known. The answer: We do not mean by 'source' and 'effector' except the totality of matters whose attainment is necessary for it to become a source for that effect. And upon this estimation, there is no distinction for us in the existential restrictions between saying they are parts of the cause and saying they are conditions for the emission of the effect from the cause. And know that with this extent that we have mentioned, it does not appear which of those four possibilities is correct: what is it?
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage clarifies the author's stance on the relationship between cause, its parts, and conditions. It begins by stating that the distinction between parts of a cause and conditions for its effect is well-known. The author then defines his terms: 'source' (مصدر) and 'effector' (مؤثر) refer to the entire set of necessary prerequisites for producing an effect. On this definition, there is no practical difference between calling certain existential restrictions 'parts' of the cause or 'conditions' for the cause's operation—the meaning is the same. However, the author notes that this general explanation alone does not indicate which of four specific possibilities (presumably discussed earlier or later) is the truth. This serves as a bridge to the subsequent detailed investigations.

Key Terms
العلة
المعلول
المصدر
المؤثر
القيود الوجودية
الشرائط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Parts of a cause and conditions are fundamentally different kinds of thing.
Correction: The author argues that since he defines the cause as the total set of necessary prerequisites, calling something a part or a condition makes no difference in meaning; all are equally necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is meant by 'source' (مصدر) and 'effector' (مؤثر)?
A) The primary cause alone
B) The totality of matters necessary for the effect to occur
C) The conditions for the cause's effect
D) The parts of the cause

Key Takeaway
The author's unified definition of the cause dissolves the distinction between its parts and conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often attribute outcomes in your life to a single cause—did you get that job because of your interview, or because of the training, the connection, the timing? After this lesson, will you pause to recognize that all prerequisites are equally essential? This week, write down one event and list every condition that had to be present for it to happen. Do you still feel comfortable calling some 'parts' and others 'conditions'?
?
The author says that even after unifying parts and conditions, the correct causal hypothesis is still unknown. Are you willing to live with that uncertainty, or do you prefer false certainty? Today, identify one belief you hold about why something happened in your life and honestly ask: 'Could there be another possibility I haven't considered?' Commit to remaining open until you have examined all four possibilities.
?
You claim to believe that God is the ultimate cause of all that occurs, but do you act as if secondary causes are independent? When something goes wrong, do you automatically blame the immediate cause without reflecting on the total set of conditions Allah set in place? This lesson demands that you see every event as emerging from a complete set of God's appointed prerequisites. Will you adjust your response to setbacks accordingly? Practice by reciting 'Qaddarallahu wa ma sha'a fa'ala' and truly meaning it.

The Possibility of Effect from a Separate Cause
Author presents a proposal that effect may come from a separate cause when power and motive combine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that an effect can originate from a separate cause (active intellect) when power and motive combine, and why this claim requires careful examination.

Core Idea
Effects may not come directly from the agent; they could flow from an external source when conditions are right.

This passage introduces a key philosophical objection to the standard kalam view of causality. The philosophers suggest that when power and motive are present, the effect arises not from the agent himself but from a separate cause—the active intellect. This is a subtle claim that the author insists must be scrutinised carefully.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposal
Read the passage and locate the central question: why can't the effect come from a separate cause when power and motive combine?
The author frames this as a third research topic.
2
Understand the philosophers' mechanism
Note the chain: power + motive → complete preparedness → effect flows from active intellect. This is the causal model being proposed.
3
Observe the author's stance
The author does not immediately accept or reject the idea but calls for careful reflection to determine whether it is true or false.
This dialectical approach is typical of kalam.

Source Text

البحث الثالث : لقائل أن يقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : أن عند حصول مجموع القدرة والداعي يصدر الأثر عن سبب منفصل ، كما يقول الفلاسفة : من أن مجموع القدرة والداعي يوجب الاستعداد التام لحدوث ذلك الأثر ، وعند حصول هذا الاستعداد التام يغيض الأثر عن العقل الفعال ؟ ويجب أن يتأمل في هذا الاحتمال ليعلم أنه حق أو باطل
The third research: A speaker may say: Why is it not permissible to say that when the combination of power (qudra) and motive (da'ī) occurs, the effect (athar) issues from a separate cause (sabab munfaṣil), just as the philosophers say: that the combination of power and motive necessitates the complete preparedness (isti'dād tāmm) for the occurrence of that effect, and when this complete preparedness occurs, the effect flows (yaghīḍ?) from the active intellect (al-'aql al-fa''āl)? And one must reflect on this possibility to know whether it is true or false.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents a third research question: a proposal that when both power and motive are present, the effect could come from an external cause rather than from the agent himself. This is the position of the philosophers, who claim that the combination of power and motive produces a complete preparedness in the recipient, and from that preparedness the effect emanates from the active intellect. The author does not immediately accept or reject this; instead he says it must be examined carefully to determine its truth. This reflects the dialectical method of kalam: considering all possibilities.

Refuting Divine Promise and Threat as Direct Motivators
Refutes the idea that divine promise and threat directly motivate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that only knowledge of the benefits and harms in divine promise and threat—not the promise/threat themselves—motivates action.

Core Idea
Knowledge, not promise or threat, truly moves us to act.

We often think that God's promise of reward compels us to obey and His threat of punishment deters us from sin. But is it the promise itself that motivates, or our knowledge of it? In this passage, the author clarifies that the true motivator is our awareness of the benefits and harms contained in the promise and threat.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's claim
The author states that saying 'the promise calls to action' is metaphorical. Read the Arabic and translation carefully to see how he frames it.
Notice the phrase 'وهذا أيضاً مجاز' (This too is metaphorical).
2
Identify the real cause
The author shifts to 'بل الحق' (Rather, the truth is) and identifies knowledge as the real caller and turner. Compare the two parts.
3
Reflect on the implication
Understand why this distinction matters for theology and ethics: it shows that actions are not automatically caused by external factors but depend on cognition.
This is a core principle in kalam about causality and responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني من الأشياء التي يظن أنها داعية إلى الفعل وصارفة عنه : إنه يقال : إن وعد الله يحمل على الفعل ويدعو إليه ، ووعيده يزجر عن الفعل ويصرف عنه . وهذا أيضاً مجاز . بل الحق : أن الداعي إلى الفعل هو علم بما في ذلك الوعد من المنافع والصارف له عن الفعل هو علم بما في ذلك الوعيد من المضار النوع الثالث : الشك . فإنه يظن أنه إذا بقي الإنسان شاكاً في الشيء فإن كونه شاكاً يدعوه إلى البحث والطلب ، فصار كونه شاكاً داعياً له إلى الفعل . والجواب : إن الشاك في الشيء متردد فيه على التسوية والإقدام على الفعل ترجيح الجانب الفعل والإستواء يناقض الترجيح . وأحد الضدين لا يكون سبباً للضد الثاني . أما قوله : إن كونه شاكاً قد يحمله على الطلب . قلنا : الداعي هناك إلى الطلب هو العلم والاعتقاد ، وذلك لأنه إذا لم يعلم حال
The second type of things that are thought to be callers to action and turners away from it: It is said that God's promise impels to action and calls to it, and His threat deters from action and turns away from it. This too is metaphorical. Rather, the truth is: the caller to action is knowledge of the benefits in that promise, and the turner away from action is knowledge of the harms in that threat. The third type: Doubt. For it is thought that if a person remains doubtful about a thing, his being doubtful calls him to investigation and seeking, so his being doubtful becomes a caller to action. The answer: The doubtful person is hesitant about the thing with equivalence, and advancing to action is a preponderance of the side of action, while equivalence contradicts preponderance. One of two opposites cannot be a cause for the second opposite. As for his statement that his being doubtful may impel him to seeking, we say: the caller there to seeking is knowledge and belief, because if he does not know the state
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of things mistakenly thought to influence action. The second type is divine promise and threat. The author states that it is metaphorical to say that the promise or threat itself calls or deters; rather, it is the agent's knowledge of the benefits in the promise and the harms in the threat that truly motivate or deter. The third type is doubt. Some think that doubt calls a person to investigation and seeking, thus motivating action. The author refutes this by noting that doubt involves hesitation and equivalence (taswiya), whereas action requires preponderance (tarjih). Equivalence contradicts preponderance, so doubt cannot be the cause of action. If doubt does sometimes lead to seeking, the real caller is knowledge and belief—specifically the knowledge that one does not know the state, which is actually a form of awareness.

Misconception: God's promise and threat themselves directly compel action.
Correction: The author explains that the direct cause is knowledge of the benefits and harms, not the promise/threat themselves.

Checkpoint

A) God's promise
B) Knowledge of the benefits in the promise
C) Fear of punishment
D) Hope for reward

Key Takeaway
The true motivator is not the external promise or threat but the agent's knowledge of its content.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship, are you driven more by the hope of reward or the fear of punishment, or by a deep knowledge that transforms your heart? What would change if knowledge alone became your primary motive?
?

Refuting Doubt as a Motivator for Inquiry
Refutes the idea that doubt itself motivates inquiry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why doubt cannot be a cause for action due to the logical contradiction between doubt (equivalence) and action (preponderance).

Core Idea
Doubt hesitates; action decides. They cannot be cause and effect.

Some people argue that doubt naturally leads to investigation and action—after all, when we are unsure, we seek answers. However, the author presents a logical refutation: doubt involves complete equivalence, while action requires a preference. How can two opposites be causally linked? This lesson explores that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
Read the author's description of the claim: being doubtful calls to investigation. Note the phrase 'إذا بقي الإنسان شاكاً'.
2
Grasp the logical refutation
The author introduces two key terms: التسوية (equivalence) and الترجيح (preponderance). See how he shows they are opposites and cannot be causally linked.
This is a subtle but powerful argument using the principle of opposition.
3
Consider the counterargument
The author then addresses a possible objection: if doubt seems to lead to seeking, it is actually knowledge (of ignorance) that motivates. Understand this refinement.
Notice the shift: الداعي هناك إلى الطلب هو العلم والاعتقاد.

The Second and Third Proofs: Knowledge, Not Purpose, Drives Action
Two proofs against purpose as cause, establishing knowledge as cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two arguments demonstrating that an agent's knowledge of an act's benefit, not the benefit itself, is what causes the act.

Core Idea
What comes after cannot cause what comes before.

This passage presents two logical proofs that the benefit or goodness of an action is not the real cause of the agent performing it. The author argues that since the benefit occurs after the action, it cannot be the cause; rather, the agent's knowledge of the benefit is what motivates the action. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise, logical style.
2
Identify the two proofs
The passage contains the second and third proofs. The second uses temporal precedence (the purpose comes after the act). The third uses circularity (if purpose were cause, it would lead to a loop).
3
Trace the logic of the second proof
The agent's being an agent precedes the purpose. What precedes cannot be caused by what is subsequent. Therefore, purpose is not the cause.
Think of building a house: the benefit of shelter occurs only after completion.
4
Trace the logic of the third proof
The purpose's occurrence is caused by the agent's agency. If purpose itself caused agency, there would be circularity. That is impossible.
Circularity means A causes B and B causes A, which is logically invalid.
5
Conclude: knowledge is the real cause
From both proofs, the author concludes that the true cause of agency is the agent's knowledge that the act contains goodness and benefit.
This refutes the idea that benefit itself is the motivator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إن الفاعل إذا فعل لأجل غرض . فذلك الغرض إنما يحصل في آخر ذلك الفعل . مثاله : أن من يبني بيتاً لدفع الحر والبرد ، فهذا المقصود إنما يحصل بعد دخول البيت ، بكماله في الوجود . ولهذا قيل : إن أول الفكر آخر العمل . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : كون الفاعل فاعلاً لذلك الفعل متقدم على دخول ذلك الغرض في الوجود، والمتقدم على الشيء يمتنع كونه معللاً بالمتأخر الثالث : إن حصول ذلك الغرض معلل يكنون الفاعل فاعلاً لذلك الشيء الذي هو السبب حصول ذلك النفع ، فلو كان ذلك الغرض علة لتلك الفاعلية لزم الدور ، وهو محال فيثبت بهذه الوجوه : أن كون الفعل منفعة وحسناً ، ليس علة لكون فاعلاً له البتة . وإنما المؤثر في الفاعلية هو علم الفاعل باشتمال ذلك الفعل على ذلك الوجه من الحسن والمنفعة .
And the second: When an agent acts for a purpose, that purpose only occurs at the end of that act. Example: he who builds a house to repel heat and cold — this purpose only occurs after entering the house, upon its complete existence. Hence it is said: the beginning of thought is the end of action. If this is established, we say: The agent's being an agent of that act precedes the entry of that purpose into existence, and what precedes a thing cannot be caused by what is subsequent. The third: The occurrence of that purpose is caused by the agent's being an agent of that thing which is the cause of obtaining that benefit. So if that purpose were the cause of that agency, circularity would follow, which is impossible. So it is established by these arguments that the act's being a benefit and good is not at all a cause for being an agent of it. Rather, the effecter in agency is the agent's knowledge that the act includes that aspect of goodness and benefit.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs (the second and third in a series) to refute the idea that the benefit or goodness of an act is what makes an agent perform it. The author first states the second proof: when an agent acts for a purpose, that purpose is only achieved at the end of the act. For example, building a house to repel heat and cold — the purpose is only realized after the house is completed. It is said that the first thought is the last action. Therefore, the agent's being an agent of that act is prior to the existence of the purpose, and something prior cannot be caused by something posterior. The third proof: The occurrence of the purpose is caused by the agent's being an agent of the act that leads to that benefit. So if the purpose were the cause of the agency, this would lead to circularity, which is impossible. The conclusion from both proofs: the act's being a benefit or good is not a cause for the agent's performing it. Instead, the true cause is the agent's knowledge that the act contains that aspect of goodness and benefit.

Key Terms
غرض (purpose)
علم (knowledge)
منفعة (benefit)
حسن (goodness)
دور (circularity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The purpose (benefit/goodness) itself is what motivates the agent to act.
Correction: The author shows that the purpose occurs after the act, so it cannot be the cause. The real cause is the agent's prior knowledge that the act will lead to that purpose.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, why would it be impossible for the purpose to be the cause of agency?
A) Because the purpose is not real
B) Because it would lead to circularity
C) Because the purpose is too far in the future
D) Because the agent does not know the purpose

Key Takeaway
The agent's knowledge of an act's benefit, not the benefit itself, is what causes the agent to perform the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship, is it the anticipated reward that drives you, or is it your knowledge of Allah's command? How does this distinction change the sincerity of your action?
?
Reflect on a recent good deed you did. Was it motivated solely by the benefit you knew would come, or by the knowledge of its goodness in itself? What does your answer reveal about your understanding of agency?
?
If your actions are caused by your knowledge, how can you increase your knowledge to improve your actions? What specific step will you take today to acquire beneficial knowledge?

Divine Promise and Threat as Figurative Motivators
The author presents and refutes the view that divine promise and threat directly motivate action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine promise and threat are considered figurative motivators, and that the true driver of action is knowledge of the benefits and harms they contain.

Core Idea
God's promise and threat do not directly move us; our knowledge of their benefits and harms does.

This lesson examines a common assumption in theological ethics: that God's promise of reward and threat of punishment directly cause us to act. The author challenges this, arguing that these are only metaphorical expressions. As you read, notice how the author distinguishes between the external divine speech and the internal cognitive state that truly motivates or deters. This distinction is crucial for understanding the role of knowledge in moral psychology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the two positions presented.
Pay attention to the word 'مجاز' (metaphorical).
2
Contrast the two views

3
Reflect on the implication

This passage presents the second type of things mistakenly thought to motivate or deter action: divine promise and threat. The author states the objection: it is claimed that God's promise encourages action and His threat discourages it. However, the author asserts that this is only a metaphor (مجاز). The true reality is that what motivates action is knowledge (علم) of the benefits within the promise, and what deters action is knowledge of the harms within the threat. Thus, the promise and threat themselves are not direct causes; rather, the agent's knowledge of the associated benefit or harm is what drives action.

Doubt Does Not Motivate Inquiry; Knowledge Does
Sanusi refutes that doubt motivates inquiry, arguing knowledge of harm does.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why doubt, as a state of equipoise, cannot cause action, and how knowledge of potential harm actually motivates inquiry.

Core Idea
Doubt is equilibrium; it cannot tip the scale toward action.

The author examines the common claim that doubt drives us to seek knowledge. He argues that doubt is a state of perfect hesitation (equipoise), which cannot logically produce a preference for action. Instead, it is the knowledge of potential harm or benefit that truly moves us to investigate. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation. Let the author's own argument come through.
Pay attention to the logical structure: objection, answer, then deeper explanation.
2
Understand the objection
What is the common belief about doubt? That it motivates inquiry. The passage states this clearly.
This is the view the author is refuting.
3
Grasp the refutation
The author points out that doubt implies equipoise (تسوية), while acting requires preference (ترجيح). One opposite cannot cause the other.
Equipoise and preference are opposites, so doubt cannot produce action.
4
See the real motivator
The author clarifies that the true motivator is knowledge (علم) of potential harm and the benefit of knowing. The person knows that inquiry helps avoid harm, so that knowledge impels him.
Knowledge of benefit, not doubt, is the cause.
5
Reflect on the argument
Consider how this refines our understanding of human motivation and causality in Kalam. Doubt is not a motive; knowledge is.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث : الشك . فإنه يظن أنه إذا بقي الإنسان شاكاً في الشيء فإن كونه شاكاً يدعوه إلى البحث والطلب ، فصار كونه شاكاً داعياً له إلى الفعل . والجواب : إن الشاك في الشيء متردد فيه على التسوية والإقدام على الفعل ترجيح الجانب الفعل والإستواء يناقض الترجيح . وأحد الضدين لا يكون سبباً للضد الثاني . أما قوله : إن كونه شاكاً قد يحمله على الطلب . قلنا : الداعي هناك إلى الطلب هو العلم والاعتقاد ، وذلك لأنه إذا لم يعلم حال الشيء جوز فيه كونه سبباً للضرر ، والعلم حاصل بأنه لا يمكنه دفع هذا الضرر المحتمل ، إلا بأن يعرف حال ذلك الشيء . فهذا الإنسان عالم بأن تحصيل العلم بحال تلك الواقعة يفيده الاحتراز عن ذلك الضرر المحتمل . وهذا نفع عظيم . فالعلم باشتمال ذلك الطلب على هذا النفع هو الذي صار حاملا له على هذا الطلب .
The third type: doubt. It is thought that if a person remains doubtful about a thing, his being doubtful calls him to investigation and seeking, so his being doubtful becomes a motivator for him to action. The answer: The doubtful person is in a state of equipoise about the matter, and undertaking the action is a preference for the side of action, while equipoise contradicts preference. And one of two opposites cannot be a cause of the other opposite. As for his saying that his being doubtful may impel him to seek (investigation): We say: the motivator there to seeking is knowledge and belief, because when he does not know the state of the thing, he considers it possible that it may be a cause of harm, and he knows that he cannot repel this potential harm except by knowing the state of that thing. So this person knows that obtaining knowledge of the state of that incident benefits him in guarding against that potential harm. And this is a great benefit. So knowledge that this seeking includes this benefit is what became the impeller for him to this seeking.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage addresses the third type: doubt. The objection claims that a person's doubt about something drives him to investigate and act. The author refutes this: the doubtful person is in a state of equal hesitation (تسوية), while acting requires a preference for one side. Equality contradicts preference. One opposite cannot be the cause of the other. As for the claim that doubt leads to seeking knowledge, the author explains that what really motivates the seeker is knowledge or belief about potential harm. The person knows he cannot avoid possible harm without knowing the thing's true state. Thus, he seeks knowledge because he knows that obtaining it brings the benefit of avoiding harm. So the motivator is knowledge of the benefit in seeking, not doubt itself.

Key Terms
شك (doubt)
تسوية (equipoise)
ترجيح (preference)
علم (knowledge)
اعتقاد (belief)
ضرر (harm)
نفع (benefit)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Doubt itself motivates one to seek knowledge and act.
Correction: Doubt is equipoise and cannot produce preference. The real motivator is knowledge of potential harm and the benefit of knowing.

Checkpoint
Why can't doubt be the cause of action according to the author?
A) Doubt is too weak
B) Doubt is a state of equilibrium, which cannot cause a preference
C) Doubt always leads to inaction
D) Doubt is a form of knowledge

Key Takeaway
Doubt, as a state of equilibrium, cannot logically cause action; it is the knowledge of potential harm and benefit that truly motivates inquiry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you delay seeking knowledge about a religious matter that you are unsure about, is it really because you are doubtful, or is it because you have not fully internalized the harm of remaining ignorant? How would your actions change if you truly believed ignorance could lead to harm?
?
Think of a time you claimed you were 'researching' or 'seeking clarity' but were actually just procrastinating. What was the real motivator—or lack of it? Based on this lesson, what knowledge were you failing to recognize?
?
Sanusi says the true motivator is knowledge of potential harm and benefit. Is there an area of your faith where you have not sought knowledge because you have not believed that ignorance could be harmful? What would it take for you to genuinely feel that urgency?

Sight and Hearing: Indirect Motivators
The author argues that sight and hearing only motivate through knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sight and hearing are not direct causes of action, but rather operate through the knowledge they generate.

Core Idea
Perception does not move us; knowledge of benefit or harm does.

We often think that seeing something or hearing a sound directly makes us act or refrain. But the author argues that sensory experiences only motivate because they produce knowledge. This lesson examines that causal chain and clarifies the real driver of human action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objection presented and the author's answer.
The passage is short but dense. Pay attention to the logical sequence.
2
Identify the objection
Find where the author presents the objection: that sight and hearing seem to directly motivate or deter action.
The phrase 'وهذا يوهم' signals the objection.
3
Understand the answer
See how the author refutes the objection by explaining that sight/hearing produce knowledge, and that knowledge (of benefit or harm) is what actually motivates.
The answer begins with 'والجواب'.
4
Trace the causal chain
Map out the logic: sight/hearing → knowledge/ belief → action or refrain. This is the key insight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع : الرؤية والسماع . وذلك لأنا إذا رأينا شيئاً ، أو سمعنا صوتاً ، فقد تصير رؤية ذلك الشيء حاملة لنا على فعل ، أو على ترك . وكذا القول في السماع ، وهذا يوهم أن للرؤية والسماع أثر في الدعاء إلى الفعل أو في الصرف عن الفعل . والجواب : إنا إذا رأينا الشيء فإنه يحصل العلم بحاله ، فإذا علمنا من حال كونه نفعاً زائداً ، دعانا ذلك العلم إلى الفعل ، وإن علمنا من حاله كونه ضرراً زائداً ، صرفنا ذلك العلم عن الفعل . فالرؤية والسماع إنما صارتا داعيتين إلى الفعل تارة وصارفتين عنه بواسطة إفادة العلم والاعتقاد .
The fourth type: sight and hearing. For when we see something, or hear a sound, the sight of that thing may become a bearer for us to act or to refrain. Similarly for hearing. This gives the impression that sight and hearing have an effect in motivating or deterring action. The answer: When we see the thing, knowledge of its state occurs. So if we know from its state that it is an additional benefit, that knowledge calls us to action; and if we know from its state that it is an additional harm, that knowledge turns us away from action. So sight and hearing become motivators to action sometimes and deterrents from it only by virtue of producing knowledge and belief.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth type: sight and hearing. The objection is that seeing something or hearing a sound can motivate or deter us directly. The author's answer: when we see something, we acquire knowledge of its state. If we know that it is beneficial, that knowledge motivates us; if harmful, it deters us. Thus, sight and hearing are not direct causes; they only bring about knowledge and belief, which are the actual motivators or deterrents.

نفع (benefit)

Misconception: Sensory perception (sight/hearing) directly causes action or inaction.
Correction: Perception only causes action indirectly by producing knowledge of benefit or harm, which is the actual motivator.

Checkpoint

A) The sight itself
B) The knowledge of benefit or harm
C) The hearing
D) The object seen

Key Takeaway
Sight and hearing are not independent causes of action; they only work by providing knowledge and belief about benefit or harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel moved to act or refrain based on something you saw or heard, pause. Is it the sight itself driving you, or the judgment you made about its benefit or harm? Which of your actions today were motivated by a quick, unexamined impression rather than genuine knowledge? What would change if you paused to verify the knowledge behind your impulses?
?
Think of a time you were deterred from something because you believed it harmful. Was that belief based on true knowledge or just a superficial impression? How many of your fears about this world are actually based on flawed perceptions rather than sound knowledge? Will you commit to seeking true knowledge before letting your senses dictate your actions?
?
The author says sight and hearing only produce knowledge and belief, which then cause action. If that is true, then every action you take based on what you see or hear is ultimately a reflection of what you truly know and believe about benefit and harm. So here is the hard question: Do your actions reveal that your beliefs about what is truly beneficial and harmful are aligned with the Qur'an and Sunnah, or with the fleeting judgments of this world? What is one belief you need to correct today so that your actions follow?

Knowledge is the Real Motivator: Two Views on Will and Desire
The author presents two views on will and desire, concluding that knowledge is the real cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author argues that knowledge, not will or desire, is the true cause of action and aversion.

Core Idea
Knowledge, not will, is the real driver of action.

This passage addresses the fifth type of objection regarding will and desire. The author presents two scholarly responses that both ultimately ground motivation in knowledge. As you read, notice how each view either identifies will with knowledge of benefit or makes knowledge the cause of will. This analysis shows that emotional inclinations are not independent motivators.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage breaks off mid-sentence—this does not affect the core argument.
2
Identify the two views
Find the first view that reduces will and aversion entirely to knowledge. Then find the second view that distinguishes them but makes knowledge the cause.
3
Understand how both lead to knowledge
In both views, knowledge of benefit or harm is the real cause of inclination or repulsion. Ask yourself: does this make will superfluous?
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters for theology: if God's will is not an independent attribute but identical to or caused by His knowledge, what does that imply?
This connects to broader debates about divine attributes.

The answer: Some people claim that will is nothing but knowledge that the act contains an additional benefit, and aversion is nothing but knowledge that the act contains an additional harm. And others concede that will and aversion are different from these two knowledges, but they claim that when knowledge occurs that the act contains an additional benefit, inclination to the act and will to obtain that thing occur. And when knowledge occurs that the act contains a preponderant harm, repulsion and aversion occur. So knowledge that it is beneficial causes inclination, and knowledge that it is harmful causes aversion. On this basis, the real motivator and deterrent is this knowledge. And as for aversion...

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth type: will and desire. The objection is that will motivates and aversion deters. The author gives two views in response. The first view reduces will and aversion entirely to knowledge: will is knowledge of benefit, aversion is knowledge of harm. The second view distinguishes them but sees knowledge as the cause that produces will and aversion. In both views, the actual cause of action or restraint is knowledge. The text breaks off mid-sentence but the point is clear: knowledge is the underlying motivator, not the emotional states themselves.

The Fourth Type: Vision and Hearing as Motivators
The author discusses vision and hearing as motivators and refutes the objection that they directly cause action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that vision and hearing do not directly cause action but do so through the knowledge they produce about benefit or harm.

Core Idea
Senses are channels; knowledge is the true motivator.

We often feel that seeing something directly makes us act or refrain, but the author invites us to look deeper. This passage presents an apparent objection and then clarifies the real mechanism: it is the knowledge gained from sense perception that moves us. Let's see how he dismantles the surface impression.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own flow come through before analyzing.
Pay attention to how the objection is set up — it seems plausible.
2
Identify the objection
The passage first states that seeing or hearing seems to directly carry us to action or restraint. This is the surface impression that needs correction.
The author uses 'يوهم' (gives the impression) to signal this is not the final truth.
3
Understand the author's answer
Notice how the author answers: he traces the effect back to knowledge ('العلم') that arises from perception. It is that knowledge of benefit or harm that actually motivates.
The phrase 'بواسطة إفادة العلم والاعتقاد' is key — it shows the mediating role of knowledge.
4
Reflect on the role of knowledge
Ask yourself: if you saw a food and it looked nutritious (benefit), you would eat. If it looked spoiled (harm), you would avoid. The sight alone didn't do it; your knowledge of benefit/harm did.
This connects to the broader theme of causality in action.
5
Test your understanding
Try the checkpoint quiz to ensure you can distinguish the direct cause (knowledge) from the indirect cause (senses).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع : الرؤية والسماع . وذلك لأنا إذا رأينا شيئاً ، أو سمعنا صوتاً ، فقد تصير رؤية ذلك الشيء حاملة لنا على فعل ، أو على ترك . وكذا القول في السماع ، وهذا يوهم أن للرؤية والسماع أثر في الدعاء إلى الفعل أو في الصرف عن الفعل . والجواب : إنا إذا رأينا الشيء فإنه يحصل العلم بحاله ، فإذا علمنا من حال كونه نفعاً زائداً ، دعانا ذلك العلم إلى الفعل ، وإن علمنا من حاله كـونه ضرراً زائداً ، صرفنا ذلك العلم عن الفعل . فالرؤية والسماع . إنما صارتا داعيتين إلى الفعل تارة ) وصارفتين عنه بواسطة إفادة العلم والاعتقاد )
The fourth type: vision and hearing. That is because when we see something, or hear a sound, then seeing that thing may become a carrier for us to act, or to refrain. Similarly for hearing. This gives the impression that vision and hearing have an effect in calling to action or in turning away from action. The answer: When we see a thing, knowledge of its state occurs. So if we know from its state that it is a beneficial benefit, that knowledge calls us to action. And if we know from its state that it is a harmful harm, that knowledge turns us away from action. So vision and hearing only become motivators to action sometimes and turners-away from it by means of providing knowledge and belief.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage discusses the fourth type of motivation: vision and hearing. The author first states the apparent objection: when we see or hear something, it seems that the sense perception itself directly causes us to act or refrain. This gives the impression that vision and hearing have an intrinsic effect on action. Then the author provides the answer: in reality, it is not the sense perception itself, but the knowledge that results from it. When we see something, we gain knowledge of its state—whether it is beneficial or harmful. That knowledge then motivates us to act or refrain. Thus, vision and hearing are merely intermediaries; the true direct cause of motivation is the acquired knowledge or belief about benefit and harm.
