Will and Aversion: Dependent on Knowledge of Benefit and Harm
The author discusses will and aversion as motivators, refutes their independence, and provides an example from everyday experience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that will and aversion are not independent motivators but are secondary to knowledge of benefit and harm in the causal chain of action.

Core Idea
Will and aversion do not motivate directly; they follow from knowledge of benefit or harm.

Why do we will something? Is the will itself the cause of action, or is it merely a response to something deeper? In this passage, the author examines the relationship between will, aversion, and knowledge. He argues that it is our belief about benefit and harm that truly moves us, while will and aversion are secondary effects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two views
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the passage begins with a claim about will and aversion, then presents two opposing views on their relationship to knowledge.
The first view reduces will to knowledge; the second grants they are distinct but caused by knowledge.
2
Analyze the example of the man visiting a friend
In the example, a man going to visit a friend recollects an important matter at home. Look at how his movement depends on his belief about the relative benefits of going vs. returning.
3
Trace the causal chain
The example shows that when beliefs are equal, no movement occurs; when one belief outweighs the other, action follows. This demonstrates that knowledge (belief) is the true motivator, not will or aversion independently.
This supports the second view.
4
Reflect on the implications for causality
Consider how this argument fits into the broader discussion of causality. The author is refuting the idea that will and aversion are independent causes, locating causality in knowledge instead.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الخامس : أن الإرادة داعية إلى الفعل (۱) والكراهية صارفة عنه ، وكذلك الشهوة داعية إلى الفعل ، والنفرة صارفة عنه والجواب : من الناس من زعم أنه لا معنى للإرادة إلا العلم باشتمال الفعل على النفع الزائد، ولا معنى للكراهية إلا العلم باشتمال الفعل على (٢) الضرر الزائد . ومنهم من سلم كون الإرادة والكراهية مغايرتين لهذين العملين ، إلا أنه زعم أن عند حصول العلم بكون الفصل مشتملا على النفع الزائد يحصل الميل إلى الفعل ، وإرادة تحصيل ذلك الشيء . وعند العلم باشتمال ذلك الفعل على مضرة راجحة تحصل النفرة والكراهية . فالعلم بكونه نافعاً . يوجب حصول الميل، والعلم بكونه ضاراً يوجب حصول النفرة عنه . وعلى هذا التقدير فالداعي والصارف في الحقيقة هو هذا العلم . وأما الكراهية والإرادة فهما تابعان لذلك العلم ، والذي يدل على ذلك : أن الإنسان إذا خرج من بيته لزيارة صديق له ، ثم في أثناء الطريق وقع في قلبه : أن له مهماً في البيت ، يوجب عليه الرجوع من ذلك الطريق إلى البيت ( فهاهنا إن حصل اعتقاد أن المنفعة في الذهاب إلى الصديق تعادل المنفعة الحاصلة من الرجوع إلى البيت (۱) ) بقي ذلك الشخص في مكانه بحيث لا يتحرك البتة ، ثم إذا وقع في خاطره إن إحدى المنفعتين راجحة على الأخرى تحرك إلى ذلك الجانب . وهذا يدل على أن الميل والإرادة تابعين لهذه الاعتقادات والله أعلم
The fifth type: that the will is a motivator to action and aversion is a turner-away from it, and likewise desire is a motivator to action and repulsion is a turner-away from it. The answer: Some people claim that there is no meaning to will except knowledge that the action contains beneficial benefit, and no meaning to aversion except knowledge that the action contains harmful harm. And among them are those who affirm that will and aversion are different from these two acts, but they claim that when knowledge occurs that the action contains beneficial benefit, inclination to action and will to obtain that thing occur. And when knowledge that the action contains preponderant harm occurs, repulsion and aversion occur. So knowledge that it is beneficial necessitates the occurrence of inclination, and knowledge that it is harmful necessitates the occurrence of repulsion. On this estimation, the motivator and the turner-away in reality is this knowledge. As for aversion and will, they are secondary to that knowledge. And what indicates that is: that a person who leaves his house to visit a friend, then in the middle of the road it occurs to his heart that he has an important matter at home that obliges him to return from that road to the house. Here, if a belief occurs that the benefit in going to the friend equals the benefit from returning home, that person remains in his place without moving at all. Then if it occurs to his mind that one of the two benefits is preponderant over the other, he moves towards that side. This indicates that inclination and will are secondary to these beliefs. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage discusses the fifth type of motivation: will and aversion. The author first presents the initial claim: will motivates action, aversion prevents it, similarly desire and repulsion. Then the answer is given in two parts. First, some scholars reduce will and aversion to mere knowledge of benefit or harm—they are not distinct. Second, others grant that will and aversion are distinct from knowledge, but they are caused by knowledge: when knowledge of benefit arises, inclination and will follow; when knowledge of harm arises, repulsion and aversion follow. Thus, the true motivator is knowledge, and will and aversion are secondary. The author supports this with an example: a person going to visit a friend, but then recollects an important matter at home. If the perceived benefits of continuing and returning are equal, he remains unmoved. Only when he believes one benefit outweighs the other does he act. This shows that movement depends on belief, not on will independently. The author concludes with 'And Allah knows best.'

Key Terms
الإرادة
الكراهية
الشهوة
النفرة
العلم
الاعتقاد
الميل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Will and aversion are independent causes of action that operate without prior knowledge or belief.
Correction: The author argues that will and aversion are not independent; they are secondary to knowledge of benefit or harm. The example shows that movement depends on belief, not on will directly.

Checkpoint
What does the example of the man going to visit a friend demonstrate?
A) That will is the primary cause of action
B) That external circumstances directly cause action
C) That inclination and will follow from beliefs about benefit
D) That aversion is stronger than desire

Key Takeaway
Inclination and will are secondary to beliefs about benefit and harm; knowledge is the true motivator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent decision you made where you felt a strong desire or aversion. Can you trace that feeling back to a specific belief about benefit or harm? What does this tell you about the real driver of your actions?
?
The passage argues that your movements are governed by your beliefs. Are there beliefs you hold about your daily affairs that might be incorrect, leading you to harmful actions? Will you take time today to examine one such belief?
?
If your will is secondary to knowledge, then the quality of your knowledge determines the quality of your choices. Are you actively seeking correct knowledge—especially about spiritual matters—or are you relying on assumptions? Commit to one step to improve your knowledge this week.

Motive and Deterrent Explained
Author explains how pleasure leads to motive and pain leads to deterrent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the pursuit of pleasure and avoidance of pain generate motives and deterrents for action according to the author.

Core Idea
Pleasure drives motive, pain drives deterrent — the engine of action.

The author now applies the previous definition of pleasure and pain to explain the origin of motives (dawā‘ī) and deterrents (ṣawārif). He shows that when we conceive a relationship between an action and pleasure or pain, that conception itself makes the action desired or undesired. This establishes the psychological basis for voluntary action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once without analyzing. Notice the conditional 'when we conceive' structure.
This passage builds on the previous discussion of pleasure and pain.
2
Identify the key terms
Locate the terms for 'motive' (al-da'i) and 'deterrent' (al-sarif). Understand how they are defined in relation to desire for attainment or non-existence.
The terms 'mawlub al-husul' and 'mawlub al-adam' are crucial.
3
Understand the causal link
See how the author connects the perception of an action's consequence (pleasure or pain) to its desirability. The action becomes desired (motive) if it entails pleasure, undesired (deterrent) if it entails pain.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this causal link matters for theology: it explains how humans are motivated to act, which is foundational for discussions of free will and moral responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : لما كان حصول اللذة والسرور أمراً مطلوباً بالذات فإذا تصورنا في أمر من الأمور ، كونه مستلزماً لحصول اللذة والسرور ، صار مطلوب الحصول . وذلك هو الداعي إلى الفعل . وإذا تصورنا في أمر من الأمور كونه مستلزماً للألم والغم ، صار مطلوب العدم ، وذلك هو الصارف عن الفعل
When you know this, we say: Since the attainment of pleasure and joy is desired by itself, when we conceive in something that it entails the attainment of pleasure and joy, it becomes desired to be obtained. That is the motive to the action. And when we conceive in something that it entails pain and grief, it becomes desired to be non-existent, and that is the deterrent from the action.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage builds on the previous definitions. It explains how the intrinsic desirability of pleasure translates into a motive for action. If we perceive that a certain action or object will necessarily lead to pleasure or joy, then that action becomes desired—it is the motive (al-da'i) to perform the action. Conversely, if we perceive that something leads to pain or grief, it becomes desired not to exist, i.e., we avoid it—that is the deterrent (al-sarif) from the action. The author is establishing a psychological-causal link between the evaluation of ends and the initiation of action.

Key Terms
الداعي
الصارف
مطلوب الحصول
مطلوب العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think motive is only about pleasure, but the author includes both motive and deterrent based on pain.
Correction: The author clarifies that both motive and deterrent stem from the same principle: pleasure leads to desire for attainment (motive), pain leads to desire for non-existence (deterrent). They are two sides of the same coin.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what makes an action become 'desired to be obtained'?
A) It is always painful.
B) It is conceived as leading to pleasure or joy.
C) It is intrinsically good.
D) It is commanded by reason.

Key Takeaway
The desirability of an action is derived from its perceived connection to pleasure or pain, which defines motive and deterrent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent action you did. Was it driven by seeking pleasure or avoiding pain? Which one? Can you trace the entailment in your mind? Did you conceive the action as leading to pleasure or pain? Write down the thought process as the author describes.

When you avoid a sin, is it because you fear the pain of punishment or because you desire the pleasure of Allah's pleasure? The author says deterrent is based on pain. How does your deterrent align with this? Does your motive for obedience stem from pleasure or just from avoiding punishment? Be honest.
?
If pleasure and pain are the engines of all action according to this passage, then what place does love of Allah have? Is love of Allah a pleasure? Or does it transcend this framework? Examine your own heart: are your actions toward Allah motivated by seeking the pleasure of His love or by the pain of His displeasure? Which one is stronger in you today?

Harm vs. Loss of Benefit: The Core Distinction
Enumeration and refutation of a counterargument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why harm is more intensely disliked than loss of benefit according to rational judgment.

Core Idea
Harm is inherently more repugnant than loss of benefit.

This passage opens the discussion on the classification of evils. The author asserts a fundamental rational principle: harm is more disliked than loss of benefit. He then addresses a counterargument that tries to equate the two, and refutes it by insisting on comparing equal quantities.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial claim
Read the first sentence where the author states that harm is stronger than loss of benefit without doubt.
2
Examine the counterargument
Notice that some scholars claim loss of benefit is equivalent to harm, and they provide a proof from transactions.
They use examples of people bearing slight harm to avoid losing great benefit.
3
Understand the refutation
The author criticizes the proof by arguing that we must compare when quantities are equal. When they are equal, harm is clearly more disliked.
This refutation relies on self-evident intellectual judgment.
4
Conclude the first discussion
The conclusion is that harm is more intensely disliked than loss of benefit when all else is equal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب البحث هاهنا من وجوه : البحث الأول : لا شك أن الضرر أقوى من زوال النفع . لان من المعلوم بالضرورة أن حصول الضرر أقوى في كونه مكروهاً من أن يبقى الإنسان خالياً عن النفع والضرر . وقال بعضهم : إن كانت المنافع حاضرة ، فضويتها بمنزلة الضرر . والدليل عليه : أنه يجري حال تفويت المنافع في المعاملات مجرى الضرر . وذلك لأن الرجل يحترز بتحمل المضرة اليسيرة من فوت المنفعة الكثيرة كما يحترز بتحمل المضرة القليلة من تحمل المضرة الكثيرة ، كما يتحمل تفويت النفع القليل لئلا يلزم تفويت النفع الكثير. وهذا الكلام ضعيف لأنا إذا أردنا أن نعرف أن أي النوعين أقوى من الثاني وجب أن يعتبر حالها عند الاستواء في الكمية . ففي هذه الحالة أيهما كان أكمل كمية كان ذلك أقوى . ومن المعلوم بالضرورة أنه إذا كان مقدار الضرر ومقدار المنفعة الفائتة متساويين في الكمية والقوة ، فإن بقاء ذلك المقدار من الضرر أشد كراهية عند العقل من تفويت ذلك القدر من المنفعة . بقي الكلام في أن تفويت المنفعة اشد كراهية أم دفع دافع الضرر ؟ الأقرب أن الثاني أقوى . لأن دفع دافع الضرر يقتضي اتقاء الضرر ، وقد ثبت أن الضرر أشد نفرة من تفويت المنفعة فلزم القطع بأن دفع دافع الضرر أشد نفرة من تفويت المنفعة
And discussion is required here from several aspects: The first discussion: There is no doubt that harm is stronger than loss of benefit. For it is self-evident that the occurrence of harm is stronger in being disliked than that a person remains devoid of benefit and harm. And some said: If benefits are present, then their loss is equivalent to harm. The proof for it: that the situation of losing benefits in transactions runs the course of harm. That is because a man avoids by bearing a slight harm the loss of a great benefit, just as he avoids by bearing a slight harm the bearing of a great harm, and just as he bears the loss of a small benefit so as not to necessitate the loss of a great benefit. But this statement is weak, because when we want to know which of the two types is stronger than the other, we must consider their case when they are equal in quantity. In that case, whichever is greater in quantity is stronger. And it is self-evident that if the amount of harm and the amount of lost benefit are equal in quantity and intensity, then the persistence of that amount of harm is more intensely disliked by the intellect than the loss of that amount of benefit. The remaining discussion is: Is loss of benefit more disliked, or repelling the repeller of harm? The closest is that the second is stronger. Because repelling the repeller of harm entails warding off harm, and it has been established that harm is more repugnant than loss of benefit. So it necessarily follows that repelling the repeller of harm is more repugnant than loss of benefit.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument comparing the intensity of dislike for different types of evils. It is the first of several discussions (wajh) on the classification. The author asserts that harm (al-darar) is more disliked than loss of benefit (zawal al-naf'), based on self-evident knowledge: experiencing harm is worse than being without benefit. Then he presents a counterclaim: some scholars argue that loss of existing benefits is equivalent to harm, because people in transactions avoid losing a great benefit even at the cost of a slight harm. They reason that people willingly bear a small harm to avoid losing a big benefit, just as they bear a small harm to avoid a big harm. The author refutes this by insisting on comparison under equal conditions. When the quantity and intensity of harm and lost benefit are equal, harm is more disliked. Therefore, the analogy fails. The discussion concludes with a comparison between loss of benefit and repelling the repeller of harm: the latter is more disliked because it involves warding off harm, and harm is worse than loss of benefit. Thus, the order of dislike from strongest to weakest is: harm, then repelling the repeller of harm, then loss of benefit.

Key Terms
الضرر
زوال النفع
الكمية
القوة
الكراهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Loss of benefit is as bad as harm because people avoid it strongly.
Correction: People avoid loss of benefit only when the benefit is large relative to the harm; when quantities are equal, harm is more disliked.

Checkpoint
How does the author decide which type of dislike is stronger?
A) By examining common legal transactions.
B) By considering self-evident preference when quantities are equal.
C) By asking people's opinions.
D) By referring to religious texts.

Key Takeaway
Harm is inherently more repugnant to the intellect than loss of benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When in your life have you complained about a loss of benefit as if it were a harm? How does this lesson change how you evaluate that complaint? Are you willing to recalibrate your gratitude and patience accordingly?
?
You instinctively avoid small harms to preserve great benefits, but you also bear great harms to avoid small harms. According to this lesson, which reaction is more rational? Look at your own recent decisions—have you confused the two? Will you now distinguish them with greater clarity?
?
If the intellect finds harm more repugnant than loss of benefit, why do you often treat them equally in your emotional reactions? What would it cost you to let this truth reshape how you respond to every day losses and pains? Make a concrete commitment to test this distinction in one situation tomorrow.

Loss of Benefit vs. Repelling the Repeller of Harm
Comparative analysis and conclusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why repelling the repeller of harm is more disliked than loss of benefit.

Core Idea
Preventing harm is more urgent than securing benefit.

Having established that harm itself is worse than loss of benefit, the author now compares two related concepts: losing a benefit versus repelling something that would cause harm. He argues that repelling the repeller of harm is even more disliked because it involves warding off harm, which is the worst type.

Guided Path
1
Read the new comparison question
The author asks: Is loss of benefit more disliked, or repelling the repeller of harm?
This is a separate discussion from the first.
2
Understand the term 'repelling the repeller of harm'
This means preventing something that would cause harm from occurring. It is a proactive measure.
3
Follow the reasoning for preference
Since repelling the repeller of harm prevents harm itself, and harm is more repugnant than loss of benefit, the repelling action inherits that stronger dislike.
The author calls this the closest opinion.
4
Confirm the final order
The strongest dislike is for harm itself, then repelling its repeller, then loss of benefit.

The Nature of Whims and Steadfastness in Action
Explains why a person of whims lacks steadfastness due to changing motives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a person of whims (badawāt) lacks steadfastness in action, and how changes in motives affect actions according to kalam causality.

Core Idea
Whims are not random; they arise from changing perceptions of benefit and harm.

The author explains a common human experience: fickleness. He defines 'whims' (badawāt) and gives the causal mechanism behind it. He then broadens the discussion to the limits of human intellect in grasping all aspects of benefit and harm.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the key terms and the flow of the argument.
The passage begins with 'على الترك' which may be a continuation; focus on the main argument.
2
Understand the definition of 'dhū badawāt'
Identify how the author defines a person of whims: someone whose perception of an action's goodness or corruption shifts over time.
The term 'بدوات' refers to passing notions or fleeting opinions.
3
Grasp the causal link
The passage states that actions are caused by ability (qudra) together with motives (dawa'i). When motives change, the action must change. This explains why a person of whims lacks steadfastness.
This is a key principle in kalam: actions are not arbitrary but based on motives.
4
Reflect on the epistemological point
The author adds that the aspects of benefit and harm are countless. The more the intellect comprehends, the more perplexity; the less, the less steadfastness. Consider how this relates to human decision-making.
This shows the author's awareness of the limits of reason in practical judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على الترك. والناس يقولون لصاحب هذه الخواطر المختلفة إنه إنسان ذو بدوات، وأنه ليس له في شيء من الأفعال ثبات. ومعنى كونه ذو بداوات: إنه تارة يبدو له كون الفعل راجح الصلاح، وتارة أخرى يبدو له كون الفعل راجع الفساد. وإنما كان مثل هذا الإنسان قليل الثبات، لأنه لما كان الفعل معللا بالقدر مع الدواعي، فعند تغير الدواعي يجب تغير الفعل. فالإنسان الذي يكون ذا بداوات، لا بد وأن يكون له في شيء من الأفعال ثبات. واعلم: أن الجهات المعتبرة بحسب المصالح والمفاسد كثيرة خارجة عن الضبط، وكلما كان وقوف العقل عليها أكثر كانت الحيرة والاضطراب أكثر، وكلما كان وقوف العقل عليها أقل كان البقاء والثبات على الطريق الواحد أقل.
On omission. People say of the one who has these varying thoughts that he is a person of whims (badawat), and that he has no steadfastness (thabat) in any of his actions. The meaning of his being a person of whims is that sometimes it appears to him that the action is predominantly good, and at other times it appears to him that the action is predominantly corrupt. This person is only of little steadfastness because when the action is caused by ability (qudra) together with motives (dawa'i), then when the motives change the action must change. So the person who has whims must necessarily have no steadfastness in any of his actions. Know that the aspects considered regarding benefits and harms are many beyond enumeration, and the more the intellect comprehends them, the greater the perplexity and confusion, and the less the intellect comprehends them, the less the remaining and steadfastness on a single path.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of whims (badawat) and its relation to steadfastness in action. The author begins by describing a person who has varying thoughts (khawatir mukhtalifa) and is called 'dhū badawāt' – one who is fickle, often changing his mind. The meaning is clarified: such a person sometimes sees an action as predominantly beneficial, and at other times as predominantly harmful. The reason for this instability is that actions are caused by ability (qudra) combined with motives (dawa'i); when motives change, the action must change accordingly. Therefore, a person of whims cannot have steadfastness in any action. The author then adds a broader epistemological point: the aspects of benefit and harm are numerous and cannot be fully grasped. The more the intellect comprehends them, the more perplexity and instability increase; the less it comprehends, the less steadfastness on one path. Thus, the passage links human fickleness to the complexity of evaluating consequences.

Key Terms
بدوات
دوافع
ثبات
قدرة
دواعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think whims are simply random or arbitrary.
Correction: The author grounds whims in the change of motives based on perceived benefit or harm, not randomness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does a person of whims (dhū badawāt) lack steadfastness in actions?
A) Because he is naturally indecisive.
B) Because actions are caused by ability and motives, and when motives change, actions change.
C) Because he does not have ability to act.
D) Because benefits and harms are always equal.

Key Takeaway
A person of whims lacks steadfastness because his actions are driven by fluctuating perceptions of benefit and harm, causing his motives to change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What is one concrete step you can take today to stabilize your motives by relying on a deeper principle rather than momentary convenience?

The Effect of Belief in Harmfulness on Omission
Proves that belief in harmfulness has no effect on omission; only lack of belief in benefit causes omission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that belief in harmfulness does not cause omission, and that omission is merely the absence of a belief in benefit.

Core Idea
Belief in harmfulness does not cause omission – only the absence of belief in benefit leaves action in non-existence.

This passage presents the third branch of an argument about causality in human action. The author has already established that ability (qudra) and motive (da'iya) have no effect on omission. Now he turns to the role of belief in harmfulness. He will argue that this belief likewise has no causal role; omission is simply the original state of non-existence that persists when there is no belief in benefit. This challenges the intuition that a belief that something is harmful directly causes us to omit it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the author builds his case step by step.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Define 'omission' as the author does
The author defines omission as 'the thing remaining in its original non-existence'. Understand this as a key move: omission is not a positive act but the absence of existence.
This definition is crucial to the argument.
3
Trace the two cases
The author considers two scenarios: 1) belief in harmfulness, 2) no belief in preponderance either way. In both, the result is omission. This shows that belief in harmfulness adds nothing.
4
Understand the causal principle
The author uses the principle that 'the cause of non-existence is the absence of the cause'. Non-existence does not need a positive cause; it happens automatically when the cause of existence is absent.
This principle is central to the whole argument.
5
Connect to the bigger picture
The passage concludes that ability and motive also have no effect on omission. The only thing the agent produces is the existence of an action. Non-existence is nothing more than the lack of a cause.
This reinforces the overall doctrine that only positive acts are attributable to the agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الثالث: وهو أنا قد ذكرنا أنه إذا كان الفعل راجح المفسدة في اعتقاد الفاعل لزم الترك، وإذا لم يحصل أيضاً فيه اعتقاد حصول الصلاح يجب الترك. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: إنه لا معنى للترك إلا بقاء الشيء على العدم الأصلي، فإن كان على تقدير أن يحصل اعتقاد رجحان المفسدة لم يحصل إلا الترك، وعلى تقدير عدم اعتقاد الرجحان، لا في الفعل ولا في الترك، لم يحصل إلا الترك. فعند هذا يظهر أنه ليس لاعتقاد كونه راجح المفسدة أثر البتة، بل إن حصل اعتقاد أنه راجح الفعل حصل الفعل، وإن لم يحصل هذا الاعتقاد بقي الفعل على عدمه الأصلي، بناء على أن علة العدم هي عدم العلة، فحينئذ لا يكون لاعتقاد أن هذا الفعل راجح المفسدة أثر في الترك البتة. فهذا يدل على أن اعتقاد كونه راجح المفسدة لا أثر له البتة في الترك. وكنا قد دللنا في أول هذا الباب على أن القدرة لا أثر لها البتة في الترك، فقد ظهر بالبحث الذي ذكرناه: أن القدرة لا أثر لها البتة في الترك، وأن الداعية لا أثر لها البتة في الترك. وذلك يقرر ما ذكرناه من أن المستند إلى القادر الفاعل ليس إلا وجود الفعل. فأما عدمه فلا تأثير للقدرة فيه، ولا تأثير للداعي فيه. والله أعلم.
The third branch: that we have mentioned that if the action is predominantly harmful in the belief of the agent, omission (tark) is necessary; and if also he does not have a belief that benefit will occur, omission is necessary. If you understand this, we say: omission means nothing other than the thing remaining in its original non-existence (al-'adam al-asli). For if it is assumed that a belief of predominant harmfulness occurs, nothing results except omission; and if it is assumed that there is no belief of preponderance, neither in the action nor in omission, nothing results except omission. On this basis, it becomes clear that the belief that it is predominantly harmful has no effect at all, rather if a belief that the action is preponderant occurs, the action occurs; and if this belief does not occur, the action remains in its original non-existence, based on the principle that the cause of non-existence is the absence of the cause. Therefore, the belief that this action is predominantly harmful has no effect at all on omission. This indicates that the belief that it is predominantly harmful has no effect at all on omission. And we have already proven at the beginning of this chapter that ability (qudra) has no effect at all on omission. So it has become clear through our inquiry that ability has no effect at all on omission, and that the motive (da'iya) has no effect at all on omission. And that confirms what we mentioned: that the basis for the capable agent is only the existence of the action. As for its non-existence, ability has no effect on it, nor does the motive have any effect on it. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents a structured argument (designated as 'the third branch') about the role of belief in harmfulness (mafsada) in producing omission (tark). The author restates his premise: if the agent believes an action is predominantly harmful, omission follows; also if he has no belief in benefit, omission follows. The key move is to define omission as 'remaining in original non-existence' – it is not a positive act but the absence of existence. The argument then proceeds through cases: whether or not one believes in harmfulness, the result is always omission (either due to that belief or due to absence of benefit belief). Therefore, belief in harmfulness has no distinctive causal effect; it is the absence of belief in benefit that leaves the action in non-existence. The author uses the causal principle that non-existence requires only the absence of its cause, not a positive cause. He reinforces this with prior conclusions that ability (qudra) and motive (da'iya) also have no effect on omission. The ultimate conclusion is that the agent's capacity only produces the existence of the action; non-existence involves neither ability nor motive.

What is the author's definition of 'omission' (tark) in this passage?

Key Takeaway
Omission is not caused by belief in harmfulness; it is simply the default state of non-existence that persists when there is no belief in benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a specific good deed you have been consistently avoiding. According to this lesson, your omission is not caused by any fear of harm or inconvenience, but by the absence of a heartfelt belief in its benefit. What would it take for you to genuinely believe that the reward from Allah outweighs your perceived risk? Write down one concrete step you will take today to cultivate that belief – a short supplication, a moment of reflection, or a small action – and commit to it.
?
This lesson reveals that your failures to act are not passive events you endure, but defaults you allow because you lack conviction. If omission is simply the absence of belief in benefit, then every area of stagnation in your life – be it prayer, charity, or repentance – points to a deficit in your trust in Allah's promise. Identify one such area and ask yourself: Do I truly believe that Allah's reward is real and immediate? Pray earnestly tonight for a certainty that moves you to act.
?
Consider the excuses you often make for not doing good: fear of people's criticism, fear of losing time, fear of failure. The lesson insists these fears have no causal role whatsoever. They are not the reason you omit; the real reason is that your belief in the benefit from Allah is weak. In the next 24 hours, challenge yourself to act on a good deed you have been postponing, and observe how your mind invokes these fears. Then, forcefully replace that fear with a declaration of divine benefit. How does this shift change your experience?

The Four Aspects Proving Infinite Ranks of Interests and Harms
The author presents four aspects (7-10) proving ranks of interests and harms are infinite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses four aspects (7-10) to demonstrate that the ranks of benefits and harms are infinite and relative, not fixed.

Core Idea
Interests and harms are not absolute but vary infinitely by mixture, person, and context.

In this passage, the author continues his argument for the infinity of the ranks of benefits and harms by presenting four additional aspects. These aspects build on the earlier idea that the more aspects of benefit a thing contains, the more valuable it is, and if those aspects are not fixed, then the ranks are not fixed. These four aspects show how mixing, distance, persistence, and personal perception all contribute to the infinite variety of benefit and harm.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then read the literal English translation. Try to get a sense of the author's flow of argument.
The passage contains four numbered aspects plus a conclusion.
2
Identify the general principle
Notice the opening statement: 'The more it encompasses aspects of benefits, the more pleasant and preferable it is.' This principle sets up the reasoning for the aspects that follow.
3
Understand each aspect
Read each aspect (7-10) one by one. For each, ask: what factor does it introduce that makes benefit and harm relative? For example, aspect 7 focuses on mixing of events, aspect 8 on comparison, aspect 9 on personal disposition, aspect 10 on individual taste and means.
The aspects are independent but all support the same conclusion.
4
Connect to the conclusion
After the four aspects, read the concluding sentence: 'Thus it is established by these ten aspects that the ranks of benefits and harms are disordered and infinite.' Reflect on how each aspect contributes to showing that benefit and harm are not fixed.
5
Reflect on implications
Consider the practical implication: if benefit and harm are relative, then judgments about what is beneficial or harmful must take into account context, person, and means.
This connects to broader discussions in kalam about divine wisdom and human actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلما كان اشتماله على وجوه المصالح أكثر ، كان الذ وآثر . وإذا كانت تلك الجهات غير مضبوطة كانت مراتب المصالح والمفاسد غير مضبوطة .
الوجه السابع : أن حوادث هذا العالم ليست بسيطة بل بعضها يمتزج بالبعض ، فيتولد عند ذلك الامتزاج أحوال أخرى . ثم من المعلوم أن مراتب الامتزاجات غير متناهية، فوجب أن تكون مراتب المصالح والمفاسد غير متناهية .
الوجه الثامن : إن البعد خير من النسبة ، لكن يعارضه أن النسبة العظيمة العالية تكون خيراً من البعد القليل .
الوجه التاسع : أن المداومة على العمل قد تفيد في حق بعضهم الحب الشديد والميل العظيم ، وقد تفيد في حق غيرهم الملالة والسامة . وأحوال الخلق في هاتين الحالتين مضطربة متفاوتة .
الوجه العاشر : أن الشيء قد يكون لذيذاً عند إنسان ، ومكروهاً عند غيره . وقد يكون لذيذاً إذا أمكنه تحصيله بطريق معين ، ويكون مكروهاً إذا كان لا يمكنه تحصيله إلا بطريق آخر. وقد يكون عند إنسان آخر بالعكس منه .
فيثبت بهذه الوجوه العشرة ، أن مراتب المصالح والمفاسد مضطربة وغير متناهية .
The more it encompasses aspects of benefits, the more pleasant and preferable it is. And if those aspects are not fixed, then the ranks of benefits and harms are not fixed.
The seventh aspect: that the events of this world are not simple, but some mix with others, so from that mixing other states arise. Then it is known that the ranks of mixtures are infinite, so it is necessary that the ranks of benefits and harms are infinite.
The eighth aspect: that distance is better than relation, but it is opposed by the fact that a great and high relation is better than a small distance.
The ninth aspect: that persistence in an action may produce in some intense love and great inclination, and may produce in others boredom and weariness. The states of creatures in these two cases are disordered and varying.
The tenth aspect: that a thing may be pleasurable for one person, and disliked by another. And it may be pleasurable if he can obtain it by a certain path, and disliked if he cannot obtain it except by another path. And for another person it may be the opposite.
Thus it is established by these ten aspects that the ranks of benefits and harms are disordered and infinite.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents four numbered aspects (7-10) that demonstrate the infinite variety and disorderliness of interests (masalih) and harms (mafasid). The author first states a general principle: the more a thing encompasses various aspects of benefit, the more valuable it is, and if those aspects are not fixed, the ranks of benefits and harms are not fixed. Then he lists each aspect. Aspect seven: events of the world are not simple; they mix, producing new states, and the grades of mixtures are infinite, so the grades of interests and harms must be infinite. Aspect eight: distance (al-bu'd) is better than relation (al-nisba), but a great and high relation may be better than a small distance, showing relativity. Aspect nine: persistence in an action can cause intense love or boredom depending on the person, and people's states vary. Aspect ten: the same thing can be pleasurable for one person and disliked by another, and can be pleasurable if obtained one way but disliked if obtained another, and the opposite holds for another person. The conclusion: these ten aspects (including the first six referenced but not detailed here) establish that the ranks of interests and harms are disordered and infinite.

مفاسد (mafasid)
مراتب (maratib)
امتزاجات (imtizajat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that interests and harms are fixed and absolute, the same for everyone in all circumstances.
Correction: This passage demonstrates that interests and harms are relative to mixtures, comparisons, personal dispositions, and means of acquisition, and therefore their ranks are infinite and disordered.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The ranks of interests and harms are not fixed but infinite and disordered, varying according to mixtures, relations, personal dispositions, and means of acquisition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an action you habitually perform in worship. Have you ever felt it becoming stale or burdensome? According to aspect 9, that very action may produce boredom in you now. Are you willing to vary your practices to rekindle love, or do you persist mechanically out of routine?
?
Aspect 10 reveals that the same thing can be pleasurable for you and disliked by another. Consider a disagreement you've had about a permissible matter. Instead of condemning the other's choice, can you humbly accept that their 'harm' may be real for them?
?
The ranks of interests are infinite. Yet you often settle for immediate, easy pleasures. What is one 'high interest' you have been avoiding because of difficulty? Will you take a concrete step today to pursue it, trusting that its benefit outweighs the cost?

Motivations and Deterrents as Beliefs: The Source of Infinite Action Diversity
The author explains that motivations are beliefs about interests, leading to infinite action diversity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that motivations and deterrents are beliefs about interests, and how the diversity of souls and constant transitions of thoughts lead to infinite diversity of actions.

Core Idea
Actions vary infinitely because motivations are beliefs, not fixed interests.

The author builds on the previous discussion about infinite interests and harms, now turning to the subjective nature of motivation. He argues that motivations and deterrents are not objective interests but beliefs about them. This key point, combined with the diversity of rational souls and the constant flux of thoughts, explains the vast diversity of human actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the phrase 'الدواعي والصوارف هي اعتقاد حصول المصالح الراجحة أو المفاسد الراجحة'.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage starts by stating that motivations and deterrents are not interests themselves, but beliefs about obtaining dominant interests or harms. This is the foundational idea.
3
Trace the argument for infinite diversity
The author gives two reasons: (1) transitions of thoughts and imaginations are not fixed, and (2) rational souls differ in essence. Combine these, and the possibilities become infinite.
Notice how the conclusion about actions is derived: states of limbs are subordinate to thoughts and souls.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that it is unlikely for two humans to agree on one way of actions and statements, and even one human cannot stay on one way. Reflect on why this is significant for understanding human behavior.
This also applies to animals, though the text is ambiguous.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ههنا كلام آخر . وهو : أنا قد ذكرنا أن الدواعي والصوارف ليست هي تلك المصالح والمفاسد في أنفها ، بل الدواعي والصوارف هي اعتقاد حصول المصالح الراجحة أو المفاسد الراجحة . ومعلوم أن انتقالات الأفكار والخيالات غير مضبوطة . وأيضاً : ثبت أن النفوس الناطقة مختلفة بالماهية ، ولها أحوال مختلفة بحسب مقتضيات تلك الماهيات . فإذا اعتبرنا مجموع هذه الوجوه بسيطة وفلكية ، صارت غير متناهية. ولما عرفت أن أحوال الجوارح تابعة لتصورات الأفكار والنفوس ، لا جرم اختلفت الأفعال جداً ، حتى قلنا : إنه يبعد أن يتوافق إنسانان على طريقة واحدة من الأفعال والأقوال . بل يبعد أن يستمر إنسان واحد على طريقة واحدة في أفعال الجوارح ، وفي أفعال القلوب . بل لا يزال أن ينتقل من تصور إلى تصور آخر . فيلزم أن ينتقل من ميل إلى ميل آخر ، فيلزم أن ينتقل من فعل إلى فعل آخر . وهذا هو السبب في الأفعال المختلفة عن الحيوانات . وبالله التوفيق .
Then there is another discussion. And it is: we have mentioned that motivations and deterrents are not those interests and harms themselves, rather motivations and deterrents are the belief in obtaining dominant interests or dominant harms. And it is known that the transitions of thoughts and imaginations are not fixed. Also: it is established that rational souls differ in essence, and they have different states according to the requirements of those essences. So if we consider the totality of these aspects, simple and celestial, they become infinite. And since it is known that the states of the limbs are subordinate to the conceptions of thoughts and souls, necessarily actions differ greatly, to the point that we say: it is unlikely that two humans agree on one way of actions and statements. Rather, it is unlikely that one human continues on one way in the actions of the limbs and in the actions of the hearts. Rather, he continually transitions from one conception to another conception, so it necessitates transitioning from one inclination to another inclination, so it necessitates transitioning from one action to another action. And this is the cause of the different actions in animals. And with God is success.

Commentary
This passage presents a new discussion that builds on the previous conclusion about the infinity of interests and harms. The author states that motivations (dawa'i) and deterrents (sawarif) are not the interests and harms themselves, but rather the belief (i'tiqad) in obtaining dominant interests or dominant harms. He then notes that transitions of thoughts and imaginations are not fixed. Additionally, rational souls (nufus natiqa) differ in their essences (mahiyya), leading to different states. Considering all these aspects (simple and celestial), they become infinite. Since the states of the limbs (jawahir) are subordinate to the conceptions (tasaawwurat) of thoughts and souls, actions vary greatly. The author concludes that it is unlikely for two humans to agree on one way of actions and statements, and even one human cannot continue on one way; he constantly transitions from one conception to another, which leads to transitions in inclinations and actions. This is identified as the cause of the diverse actions in animals (or from animals). The passage ends with a traditional phrase invoking God's success.

Why Human Actions Are So Diverse
Author explains causal chain from conception to action leading to diversity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the diversity of human actions is causally linked to the diversity of conceptions, according to the author.

Core Idea
Human actions differ because our conceptions and inclinations are constantly changing.

The author presents a psychological explanation for why humans act so differently from one another—and why even a single person rarely acts consistently. He traces the cause back to the ever-changing conceptions in our minds, which shift our inclinations and ultimately our actions. This passage lays out a causal chain that explains the observable diversity in human behavior.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm and claims come through.
Notice how the author uses a strong statement: 'it is unlikely'.
2
Identify the causal chain
The passage presents a sequence: conception → inclination → action. Find each step in the text and note the Arabic words for each.
The key words are: تصور, ميل, فعل.
3
Reflect on the claims about individuals
The author says it's unlikely for two people to agree on one method, and unlikely for one person to stay consistent. Why does he think that? Connect to the causal chain.
This shows the deep-seated nature of diversity.
4
Consider the broader implication
The passage ends by stating this as the reason for diverse actions in animals. Think about what makes humans different from animals in this regard.
The chain explains both inter-human and intra-human diversity.

لتصورات الأفكار والنفوس ، لا جرم اختلفت الأفعال جداً ، حتى قلنا : إنه يبعد أن يتوافق إنسانان على طريقة واحدة من الأفعال والأقوال . بل يبعد أن يستمر إنسان واحد على طريقة واحدة في أفعال الجوارح ، وفي أفعال القلوب . بل لا يزال أن ينتقل من تصور إلى تصور آخر . فيلزم أن ينتقل من ميل إلى ميل آخر ، فيلزم أن ينتقل من فعل إلى فعل آخر . وهذا هو السبب في الأفعال المختلفة عن الحيوانات . وبالله التوفيق .
Due to the conceptions of thoughts and souls, without doubt actions differ greatly, so much so that we said: It is unlikely that two humans agree on one method of actions and statements. Rather, it is unlikely that one human continues on one method in the actions of the limbs and in the actions of the hearts. So it follows that he moves from one inclination to another inclination, so it follows that he moves from one action to another action. And this is the reason for the diverse actions from animals. Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage presents a psychological explanation for the diversity of human actions. The author begins by stating that because thoughts and souls have diverse conceptions (تصورات), actions necessarily differ greatly. He then makes a strong claim: it is improbable for two people to agree on a single way of acting and speaking. Even more striking, it is improbable for a single individual to remain consistent in one method, whether in physical actions (actions of the limbs) or internal actions (actions of the heart). The reason is that a person continuously moves from one conception to another, which leads to a change in inclination (ميل), and finally to a change in action. This constant flux is presented as the underlying cause for the variety of actions seen in humans, distinguishing them from animals. The passage ends with a prayer for divine assistance.

Key Takeaway
The diversity of human actions is not random but arises from the continuous change of conceptions and inclinations.

Can Actions Be Purely Beneficial?
Examination of whether an action can be purely beneficial, distinguishing physical vs. spiritual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why purely spiritual actions can be entirely beneficial while physical actions always carry some harm.

Core Idea
Spiritual actions are pure benefit; physical actions always involve some harm.

In this passage, the author considers whether any action can be completely beneficial. After presenting a skeptical view that even beneficial actions cause fatigue, he shows that this only applies to physical actions. Spiritual actions, being non-physical, are entirely free from harm. This distinction explains why spiritual pleasures are superior.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the question posed and the structure of argument.
The author first reports an objection, then gives his own view.
2
Identify the two types of actions
Find where the author distinguishes physical actions (أفعال الجوارح) and spiritual actions (أفعال نفسانية روحانية). Note the characteristics of each.
3
Understand the argument against pure benefit
The objection says that even a single repeated action causes fatigue, which is a harm. But if benefits outweigh harms, the fatigue is unnoticed. The author agrees for physical actions.
4
See the author's distinction
The author argues that physical actions involve movement and heat, leading to weakness. Spiritual actions involve no bodily movement, so they are harm-free.
5
Reflect on the implication for spiritual pleasures
The passage concludes that this is one reason spiritual pleasures are superior to bodily pleasures.
Think about how this relates to religious practices like prayer or contemplation.

الفصل الخامس : بقية الكلام في هذا الباب. الفرع الأول : إنه هل يعقل وجود فعل يكون نفعاً من كل الوجوه ؟ قال بعضهم : إن هذا كالمتعذر. لأن الإنسان إذا واظب على الإتيان بنوع واحد من العمل ، ظهر فيه التعب والإعياء . ولولا أنه حصل بالفعل الأول تنوعاً من أنواع التعب . لما ظهر التعب الشديد عند المداومة على العمل : إلا أنه لما كانت المنافع المتولدة من ذلك العمل أكثر من المضار المتولدة منه ، صار ذلك التعب القليل مخففاً غير مشعور به. وأقول : هذا الكلام حق في أفعال الجوارح، لأنها لا تنفك عن حركة لتلك الأعضاء . والحركة والسخونة توجب الموجب للضعف . وأما الأفعال النفسانية الروحانية المحضة فهي منافع خالية عن جهات الضرر بالكلية. وهذا هو أحد الأسباب الموجبة لفضل اللذات الروحانية على اللذات الجسدائية .
Chapter Five: The remainder of the discussion in this section. The first branch: Is it conceivable that there exists an action that is beneficial from all aspects? Some said: This is impossible. Because if a person persists in performing one type of work, fatigue and exhaustion appear in him. And were it not that by the first action a variety of fatigue had occurred, severe fatigue would not appear upon continuation of the work. However, since the benefits generated from that work are more than the harms generated from it, that little fatigue becomes mitigated and unnoticed. And I say: This statement is true regarding physical actions (afʿāl al-jawāriḥ), because they are not free from movement of those limbs. And movement and heat necessitate what necessitates weakness. As for purely spiritual-psychic actions (al-afʿāl al-nafsāniyya al-rūḥāniyya al-maḥḍa), they are benefits devoid of aspects of harm entirely. And this is one of the reasons necessitating the superiority of spiritual pleasures over bodily pleasures.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage addresses the question of whether an action can be purely beneficial. The author first reports the view of some who deem it impossible, arguing that even a single repeated action causes fatigue, which is a harm. However, they concede that if the benefits outweigh the harms, the fatigue becomes negligible. The author then gives his own opinion: this argument holds only for physical actions (أفعال الجوارح), which necessarily involve movement and heat leading to weakness. But purely spiritual actions (الأفعال النفسانية الروحانية المحضة) are completely free from any aspect of harm. Therefore, they can be purely beneficial. This distinction serves to explain the superiority of spiritual pleasures over physical ones.

Misconception: All actions, including spiritual ones, involve some harm (e.g., fatigue or distraction).
Correction: The author argues that purely spiritual actions, being non-physical, involve no bodily movement and thus are entirely free from harm. They are pure benefits.

B) They are done with good intention
C) They are commanded by God
D) They are not real actions

Author refutes purely harmful action possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why kalam scholars argue that no action can be purely harmful, based on the principle that every agent acts for some good.

Core Idea
No action is purely harmful because every agent seeks some benefit.

This lesson examines a debated question in Islamic theology: can an action be entirely harmful? The author states that the correct view is that this is impossible. Although the passage is incomplete, the reasoning points to the nature of agency and purpose.

Can an Action Be Purely Beneficial? Distinguishing Bodily from Spiritual Acts
Author distinguishes bodily and spiritual actions to argue pure benefit possible for the latter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why bodily actions always involve some harm, but spiritual actions can be purely beneficial, and how this explains the superiority of spiritual pleasures.

Core Idea
Spiritual actions can be purely beneficial, free from the fatigue inherent in bodily actions.

This passage tackles a fundamental question: can any action be completely beneficial without any downside? An objector argues that all actions cause fatigue, so none is purely beneficial. However, the author distinguishes bodily actions from spiritual acts, showing that spiritual actions are devoid of harm. This distinction also sheds light on why spiritual pleasures surpass bodily ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the flow from objection to response.
The passage begins with the objection before the author's answer.
2
Identify the objection
The objector claims pure benefit is impossible because all actions involve fatigue. Understand this reasoning.
The objector uses the example of persisting in one type of work.
3
Note the distinction
The author concedes for bodily actions but introduces spiritual actions as a separate category. Identify the characteristics of each.
Bodily actions involve movement and heat; spiritual actions are free from them.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Spiritual actions are pure benefits, and this explains why spiritual pleasures are superior. Consider why this matters for theology.
This connects to broader themes of the superiority of the spiritual over the physical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقية الكلام في هذا الباب. الفرع الأول : إنه هل يعقل وجود فعل يكون نفعاً من كل الوجوه ؟ قال بعضهم : إن هذا كالمتعلر. لأن الإنسان إذا واظب على الإتيان بنوع واحد من العمل ، ظهر فيه التعب والإعياء . ولولا أنه حصل بالفعل الأول تنوعاً من أنواع التعب . لما ظهر التعب الشديد عند المداومة على العمل : إلا أنه لما كانت المنافع المتولدة من ذلك العمل أكثر من المضار المتولدة منه ، صار ذلك التعب القليل مخففاً غير مشعور به. وأقول : هذا الكلام حق في أفعال الجوارح، لأنها لا تنفك عن حركة لتلك الأعضاء . والحركة والسخونة توجب الموجب للضعف . وأما الأفعال النفسانية الروحانية المحضة فهي منافع خالية عن جهات الضرر بالكلية. وهذا هو أحد الأسباب الموجبة لفضل اللذات الروحانية على اللذات الجسدائية . واختلفوا أيضاً : في أنه هل يعقل وجود فعل يكون ضرراً من كل الوجوه ؟ والحق : أنه باطل . لأن كل فعل . صدر عن فاعل ، وكان فاعله مريداً له ، ويكون حصوله لذيذاً عنده ، لا يكون ذلك الفعل شراً من كل الوجوه.
Remainder of the discussion in this chapter. The first branch: Whether it is rationally conceivable for an action to be beneficial from every aspect? Some said: This is like the impossible. Because if a person persists in performing one type of work, fatigue and exhaustion appear in him. And were it not that the first act produced a kind of fatigue, severe fatigue would not appear upon persisting in work. However, since the benefits generated from that work are more than the harms generated from it, that little fatigue becomes lightened and not perceived. I say: This statement is true regarding bodily actions, because they are not free from movement of those limbs. And movement and heat necessitate what necessitates weakness. As for purely spiritual and psychical actions, they are benefits devoid of any aspect of harm entirely. And this is one of the causes necessitating the superiority of spiritual pleasures over bodily pleasures. And they also differed: Whether it is rationally conceivable for an action to be harmful from every aspect? The truth: It is invalid. Because every action that proceeds from an agent, and its agent wills it, and its occurrence is pleasurable to him, that action is not evil from every aspect.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage addresses two related issues about the nature of actions. First, it asks whether an action can be purely beneficial (نفعاً من كل الوجوه). An objector argues that all actions involve fatigue, so no action is purely beneficial. The author responds by distinguishing bodily actions (أفعال الجوارح) from purely spiritual/psychical actions (الأفعال النفسانية الروحانية المحضة). Bodily actions always involve movement and heat, which cause fatigue, but spiritual actions are free from such harm. This explains why spiritual pleasures are superior. Second, it asks whether an action can be purely harmful (ضرراً من كل الوجوه). The author asserts this is impossible because any action that an agent willingly performs and finds pleasurable cannot be entirely evil. Thus, every willed action has some benefit for the agent.

أفعال نفسانية روحانية

Correction: The author shows that spiritual actions, unlike bodily actions, can be purely beneficial because they lack physical fatigue.

C) Because they are commanded by God.
D) Because they are spiritual in nature.

Spiritual actions can be purely beneficial because they are free from the physical fatigue inherent in bodily actions, explaining the superiority of spiritual pleasures.

Can an Action Be Purely Harmful? The Impossibility of Pure Evil
Author argues pure harm impossible because willing agents find pleasure in their actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why no action performed by a willing agent can be purely harmful, because the agent's will and pleasure in the action entail some benefit.

Core Idea
No willed action is purely harmful; the agent's pleasure in it makes it not entirely evil.

Having established that spiritual actions can be purely beneficial, the author turns to the opposite question: can an action be purely harmful? Some might think so, but the author argues it is impossible. Every action performed by a willing agent who finds pleasure in it cannot be completely evil. This lesson explores that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation

This is a short but powerful argument.

The author says 'The truth: It is invalid.'
3
Understand the proof
The proof is that any action performed willingly by an agent who finds it pleasurable cannot be entirely evil. The agent's will and pleasure indicate some benefit.
Consider why pleasure implies benefit.
4
Consider the implication
This means every willed action has at least some good from the agent's perspective. Reflect on how this aligns with broader theological principles.
This resonates with the idea that evil is not absolute.

Multiple Benefits and Motivations in One Action
Explains the three types of motivations and how multiple can combine in one action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of motivation (دواعي) and how a single action can contain multiple independent motives from these categories.

Core Idea
A single action can be motivated by multiple independent reasons, each sufficient on its own.

This lesson explores a key principle in understanding causality and motivation: a single action can serve many purposes and be driven by many reasons. The author classifies motivations into three fundamental types and shows how they can coexist in one action, each independently sufficient to call to action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three categories
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three types of motivations listed: benefit, repelling harm, and repelling what repels benefit.
These three categories are the foundation for the entire discussion.
2
Observe how multiple aspects can exist within each category
The text explains that a single action can be a benefit from many aspects, or repel many types of harm, etc. Each aspect is an independent motivation.
Think of examples like an action that brings multiple benefits (health, wealth, knowledge).
3
Understand the conclusion: each aspect is independently sufficient
The author concludes that if an action has multiple beneficial aspects or repels multiple harms, each aspect alone would be enough to motivate the action. This shows that motivations can combine without conflict.
This principle is important for understanding how multiple causes can converge on one effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الثاني : إن الفعل الواحد قد يكون مشتملا على جهات كثيرة من المنافع ، ويكون مشتملا على جهات كثيرة من الدواعي . مثاله : إنا بينا أن الجهات الداعية إلى الفعل ثلاثة : أحدها : كونه نفعاً . والثاني : كونه دافعاً للضرر . والثالث : كونه دافعاً لدافع النفع . فإذا حصل في الفعل الواحد أحد هذه الجهات الثلاثة ، فقد حصل فيه أمور ثلاثة كل واحد منها مستقل بالدعاء إلى الفعل . وأيضاً : فالفعل الواحد يمكن أن يكون نفعاً من جهات كثيرة ، بحيث يكون العلم بكل واحد منها جهة مستقلة بالدعاء إلى الفعل وكذلك يمكن أن يكون دافعاً لأنواع كثيرة من المضار بحيث يكون كل واحد منها مستقلا بالدعاء إلى الفعل. وأيضاً : فالفعل الواحد يمكن أن يكون دافعاً لأشياء كثيرة يكون كل واحد منها سبباً مستقلاً بتفويت المنفعة. فإذا قدرنا حصول فعل واحد ، وكان نفعاً من جهات كثيرة ، وكان دافعاً لأنواع كثيرة من الضرر ، وكان أيضاً دافعاً لأقسام كثيرة من الأحوال الموجبة لفوات النفع ، كان كل واحد من هذه الوجوه : وجهاً ، لو انفرد لكان مستقلاً بالدعاء إلى الفعل. فهذا بيان كيفية اجتماع الوجوه الكثيرة من الدواعي في الفعل الواحد.
The second branch: A single action may contain many aspects of benefits and many aspects of motivations. Its example: We have explained that the aspects that call to action are three: One: its being a benefit. Second: its being a repeller of harm. Third: its being a repeller of what repels benefit. So if in a single action one of these three aspects occurs, then there occur in it three things, each of which is independently sufficient to call to action. Also, a single action can be a benefit from many aspects, such that knowledge of each one is an independent aspect calling to action. Similarly, it can be a repeller of many types of harms, such that each one is independent in calling to action. Also, a single action can be a repeller of many things, each of which is an independent cause of losing benefit. So if we suppose a single action occurs, and it is a benefit from many aspects, and it is a repeller of many types of harm, and also a repeller of many kinds of states that necessitate the loss of benefit, then each of these aspects would be an aspect that, if it were alone, would be independent in calling to action. This is an explanation of how many aspects of motivations come together in a single action.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage classifies the types of motivations (دواعي) that call an agent to action. The author identifies three basic categories: (1) The action is a benefit (نفع) in itself; (2) It repels harm (دافع للضرر); (3) It repels something that prevents benefit (دافع لدافع النفع). A single action can contain multiple aspects from these categories, and each aspect can independently motivate the action. Moreover, within each category, there can be multiple specific instances (e.g., many types of harm being repelled), each also independently sufficient to motivate. The author concludes that when all these motivations converge in one action, each aspect would alone be sufficient to call to the action, showing how multiple independent motivations can coexist.

Key Terms
جهات الداعية
نفع
دافع للضرر
دافع لدافع النفع
دواعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Multiple Motivations: Supportive or Contradictory?
Classifies multiple motivations and raises a causal problem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how multiple motivations for an action can be classified as either mutually supportive or mutually contradictory, and the problem that arises when each motivation alone is sufficient to cause the action.

Core Idea
When multiple motivations converge, they either work together or against each other.

In this passage, the author begins the third branch of the discussion on motivations, focusing on how multiple aspects of motivations interact within a single action. He divides them into two broad categories: those that are compatible and those that are contradictory. He then raises a problem about the first category that challenges our intuitive understanding of causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Note the terms used for 'supportive' and 'contradictory'.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage explicitly states two categories: متعاضدة متوافقة and متنافية متعاندة. Understand that these are the two possible relationships.
3
Understand the problem with the first category
The problem is that if each motivation alone is sufficient to tip the scale, then when they combine, how does the tipping occur? Does each one independently cause the action?
This is a classical problem of causal overdetermination.
4
Reflect on the issue
Think about why this is a problem for causal reasoning. It challenges the principle of causal sufficiency.

Commentary
This passage begins the discussion of how multiple motivations interact in a single action. Multiple Motives in a Single Action
Razi explains how a single action can contain multiple motives and benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single action can have multiple independent motives according to Razi's threefold classification.

Core Idea
One action can be motivated by many independent benefits and repelling of harms.

Razi explores a fascinating question: can a single act be driven by more than one motive? He argues that not only can an action have multiple motives, but each motive can itself be independent and sufficient. This passage lays out the three basic types of motives and shows how they can multiply within one action. Understanding this helps us appreciate the complexity of moral and theological reasoning about actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice how Razi structures his argument: a general claim, then an example, then elaboration.
Pay attention to the three categories he mentions.
2
Identify the three core motives
Razi lists three motive-giving aspects: benefit, repelling harm, and repelling what repels benefit. Understand each one.
These are the building blocks for multiple motives.
3
Understand how each can multiply
Razi explains that each of these three categories can contain many sub-aspects. For example, an action can be beneficial in many ways, not just one.
This shows that motives are not limited to one per category.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
Razi concludes that if an action has multiple aspects from each category, each aspect would be sufficient alone to motivate the action. This demonstrates the coexistence of independent motives.
This refutes the idea that motives are mutually exclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الثاني : إن الفعل الواحد قد يكون مشتملا على جهات كثيرة من المنافع ، ويكون مشتملا على جهات كثيرة من الدواعي . مثاله : إنا بينا أن الجهات الداعية إلى الفعل ثلاثة : أحدها : كونه نفعا . والثاني : كونه دافعا للضرر . والثالث : كونه دافعا لدافع النفع . فإذا حصل في الفعل الواحد أحد هذه الجهات الثلاثة ، فقد حصل فيه أمور ثلاثة كل واحد منها مستقل بالدعاء إلى الفعل . وأيضا : فالفعل الواحد يمكن أن يكون نفعا من جهات كثيرة ، بحيث يكون العلم بكل واحد منها جهة مستقلة بالدعاء إلى الفعل وكذلك يمكن أن يكون دافعا لأنواع كثيرة من المضار بحيث يكون كل واحد منها مستقلا بالدعاء إلى الفعل . وأيضا : فالفعل الواحد يمكن أن يكون دافعا لأشياء كثيرة يكون كل واحد منها سببا مستقلا بتفويت المنفعة . فإذا قدرنا حصول فعل واحد ، وكان نفعا من جهات كثيرة ، وكان دافعا لأنواع كثيرة من الضرر ، وكان أيضا دافعا لأقسام كثيرة من الأحوال الموجبة لفوات النفع ، كان كل واحد من هذه الوجوه : وجها ، لو انفرد لكان مستقلا بالدعاء إلى الفعل . فهذا بيان كيفية اجتماع الوجوه الكثيرة من الدواعي في الفعل الواحد
The second branch: A single action may contain many aspects of benefits, and may contain many aspects of motives. Example: We have shown that the motive-giving aspects of an action are three: one: its being beneficial; the second: its being a repeller of harm; the third: its being a repeller of what repels benefit. So if in a single action one of these three aspects occurs, then three things occur in it, each of which is independent in motivating the action. Also: a single action can be beneficial from many aspects, such that knowledge of each one is an independent aspect for motivating the action. Similarly, it can be a repeller of many types of harms, such that each one is independent in motivating the action. Also: a single action can be a repeller of many things, each of which is an independent cause for losing benefit. So if we suppose the occurrence of a single action, and it is beneficial from many aspects, and it is a repeller of many types of harm, and it is also a repeller of many kinds of states necessitating the loss of benefit, then each of these aspects would be an aspect that, if isolated, would be independent in motivating the action. This is an explanation of how many aspects of motives come together in a single action.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of how a single action can contain multiple motives. The author begins by stating that a single action can have many beneficial aspects and many motive-giving aspects. He then gives an example: he previously established that the motive-giving aspects of an action are three: being beneficial, repelling harm, and repelling what repels benefit. If a single action contains all three, then there are three independent motives. Moreover, each of these categories can itself have multiple sub-aspects: e.g., an action can be beneficial in many ways, repel many types of harm, and repel many causes of lost benefit. The author concludes that if an action has all these aspects, each aspect would be sufficient on its own to motivate the action. This demonstrates the possibility of multiple independent motives converging in one act.
