The Puzzle of Multiple Independent Motives
Razi presents a logical trilemma about multiple independent congruent motives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical trilemma Razi raises when multiple independent sufficient motives concur, and why each of the three possible outcomes is deemed impossible.

Core Idea
When two independent causes demand the same effect, reason crashes into paradox.

Razi now takes us deeper into the problem of congruent motives. He imagines a scenario where each motive alone would be enough to determine the action. When they combine, a logical trilemma emerges. This passage presents that trilemma and argues why every option leads to contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The passage presents three possibilities for how inclination (tarjīḥ) might occur when multiple independent motives combine: by all, by some, or by none. Find each one in the text.
3
Understand why each is rejected
For each possibility, follow Razi's argument for why it is impossible. Note the key logical principles he uses: the effect with a complete cause is necessary, and that necessity prevents attribution to another cause.
Pay attention to the phrase 'يلزم أن يكون مستندا إلى كل واحد منهما حال كونه منقطعا عن كل واحد منهما'.
4
Reflect on the strength of the objection
Razi concludes that this is a 'strong objection' (سؤال قوي). Consider why this problem is significant for the discussion of motives and divine action.
This challenge is not resolved here; it is left as a puzzle for further investigation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الثالث : إنه إذا حصل في الفعل جهات كثيرة في الدواعي ، فتلك الجهات إما أن تكون متعاضدة متوافقة ، وإما أن تكون متنافية متعاندة . أما القسم الأول : فمثاله ما ذكرناه . وفيه أشكال ، وهو أنه إذا كان كل واحد من تلك الجهات بحيث يكون اعتقاد حصوله سببا مستقلا بالدعاء إلى الفعل ، وبترجح جانب الفعل . فعند اجتماعهما إما أن يقع ذلك الترجيح بكل واحد منها ، أو يقع ببعضها دون البعض ، أو لا يقع بشيء منها . والأول باطل . لأن الفعل إنما احتاج إلى المرجح لكونه في نفسه ممكن الوجود والعدم، لكن المعلول مع العلة التامة ومع السبب المستقل يكون واجب الحصول . وإذا كان واجبا فوجوبه يمنعه من الانتساب إلى الغير فيكون ذلك الأثر السبب المستقل يمنعه من الاستناد إلى الثاني، وكونه مع الثاني يمنعه من الاستناد إلى الأول ، فلو وجد مع العلتين المستقلتين ، يلزم أن يكون مستندا إلى كل واحد منهما حال كونه منقطعا عن كل واحد منهما . وذلك محال . وأما القول بوقوع ذلك المعلول بأحد تلك العلل المستقلة ، فهذا أيضا محال ، لأنه يقتضي ترجيح أحد طرفي الجائز على الآخر لا المرجح وهو محال . وأما القول بامتناع وقوعه بشيء منها ، فهو أيضا محال ، لأنه يلزم منه ، أن القادر حال ما حصلت دواعيه إلى الفعل وتأكدت ولم يحصل لها مانع فإنه يمتنع الفعل عليه وذلك محال . فهذا سؤال قوي في هذا الموضع
The third branch: When there occur in an action many aspects of motives, those aspects are either mutually supportive and congruent, or mutually exclusive and opposed. As for the first division: its example is what we have mentioned. And in it there is a problem, namely that if each of those aspects is such that the belief in its occurrence is an independent cause for motivating the action and for inclining the side of the action, then when they come together, either that inclination occurs by each one of them, or it occurs by some of them without others, or it does not occur by any of them. The first is invalid because the action only required the inclining factor due to its being in itself possible of existence and non-existence, but the effect with the complete cause and with the independent cause is necessarily obtained. And if it is necessary, its necessity prevents it from being attributed to another. So that effect from the independent cause prevents it from being attributed to the second, and its being with the second prevents it from being attributed to the first. So if it exists with two independent causes, it would necessarily be dependent on each one of them while being disconnected from each one of them, which is impossible. As for the statement that the effect occurs by one of those independent causes, this too is impossible, because it would necessitate tipping one of the two sides of the possible without the tipper, which is impossible. As for the statement that it is impossible for it to occur by any of them, that too is impossible, because it would necessitate that the capable agent, when his motives to the action have occurred and become strong and no impediment has occurred to them, that the action would be impossible for him, which is impossible. So this is a strong objection in this place.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents a problem (شكال) arising from the first type of congruent motives. The author sets up the scenario: multiple independent motives each sufficient to incline the agent to act. When they combine, we must consider how the inclination (ترجيح) occurs: either by all of them, by some, or by none. Each option is shown to be impossible. (1) If by all, then two independent causes would both be the cause of the same effect, which is impossible because each cause would attribute the effect to itself while excluding the other. (2) If by some, then action would occur without sufficient cause (مرجح) since other equally strong causes exist but are not chosen arbitrarily. (3) If by none, then even with strong motives and no impediments, the action becomes impossible, which contradicts the notion of capability. Therefore, the problem is left as a strong objection, indicating a paradox that requires resolution.

Key Terms
دواعي متعاضدة
علة تامة
سبب مستقل
ترجيح
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is a direct refutation of free will and shows that multiple motives cannot exist.
Correction: Razi is not denying free will; he is highlighting a logical difficulty that arises when we assume each motive is an independent sufficient cause. The objection is left unresolved, indicating a deep puzzle rather than a simple refutation.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three possibilities Razi considers for how inclination occurs when multiple independent motives combine?
A) The inclination occurs by each motive independently
B) The inclination occurs by the weakest motive
C) The inclination occurs by some motives and not others
D) The inclination does not occur by any motives

Key Takeaway
When multiple independent sufficient motives concur, all logical possibilities lead to contradiction, revealing a deep objection that requires further analysis.

Conflicting Motives: The Strongest Prevails
Razi states the rule for conflicting motives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi resolves the case of conflicting motives: the strongest determines the outcome, and when equal they cancel.

Core Idea
When motives conflict, the strongest wins; if equal, nothing happens.

In this passage, Razi addresses a common scenario in causal reasoning: what happens when motives oppose each other? He provides a straightforward rule that applies to conflicting tendencies. This builds on his earlier classification of motives, now focusing on the second type where they are mutually exclusive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the concise formulation of the rule.
The passage is short but dense with meaning.
2
Identify the two conditions
The passage presents two distinct cases: when the motives differ in strength, and when they are equal. Notice how each case leads to a different outcome.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this rule is important for causal reasoning. How does it help determine the effective cause in a conflict?
This connects to the broader theory of causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو ما إذا كانت الدواعي متنافية ومتنازعة . فنقول : قد ذكرنا فيها تقدم أن أيها أقوى ، وأيها أضعف ؟ وعند اجتماعها يكون التأثير للأقوى . وإن تساوت في القوة تعارضت وتساقطت ويبقى ما كان على ما كان

Commentary
This passage gives the rule for the second type of motives: conflicting motives. The author states that when motives are mutually exclusive, the stronger one determines the action. If they are perfectly balanced in strength, they negate each other, and the action remains in its original state (i.e., it does not occur unless another factor intervenes). This provides a clear resolution for the case of opposing tendencies.

The Fourth Branch: Timing of Motives Relative to Action
Razi questions whether motives must precede or coincide with action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the timing of motives relative to action depends on the causal model: whether the combination of power and motive is an effective cause or merely a preparatory cause.

Core Idea
The timing of motives is conditional: it hinges on how we understand causality.

In this passage, Razi poses a deceptively simple question: do motives occur before the action, or at the same time? But the answer is not straightforward — it depends entirely on whether we view the pair of power (قدرة) and motive (داع) as an effective cause (علة مؤثرة) or just a preparatory cause (علة معدة). Let's explore this crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the conditional structure: Razi sets up two possible scenarios.
The passage is incomplete, but the core idea is clear.
2
Identify the two causal models
Look for the terms 'علة مؤثرة' and 'علة معدة'. Understand that the first is an effective cause that directly produces the action, while the second merely prepares the way for another cause.
This distinction is central to kalam discussions of agency.
3
Trace the implication for timing
Razi's argument is that if the combination is an effective cause, the motive must coincide with the action; if it is preparatory, the effective cause is something else, and timing differs.
The passage cuts off, but the direction is clear.
4
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you grasp the conditional relationship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الرابع : هذه العلوم والاعتقادات والظنون التي سميناها بالدواعي ، يجب حصولها قبل حصول الفعل ، أو حال حدوثه وحصوله ؟ وهذا البحث مفرع على أن مجموع القدرة مع الداعي علة مؤثرة في وجود الفعل ، إن قلنا : ذلك المجموع علة معدة الحصول الفعل وأما العلة المؤثرة فهي موجود آخر . فإن قلنا : إنه علة مؤثرة وجب
The fourth branch: These cognitions, beliefs, and conjectures which we have called motives — must they occur before the occurrence of the action, or at the time of its occurrence? This inquiry is based on the idea that the totality of power together with the motive is an effective cause (علة مؤثرة) in the existence of the action. If we say that totality is a preparatory cause (علة معدة) for the occurrence of the action, and the effective cause is another existent. If we say that it is an effective cause, then it must be that...
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage raises a question about the temporal sequence of motives relative to action. The author sets up a conditional framework: the relationship depends on whether the combination of power (قدرة) and motive (داع) is considered an effective cause (علة مؤثرة) or merely a preparatory cause (علة معدة). If it is preparatory, then the effective cause is something else (presumably the agent's will). If it is effective, then the timing of the motive and action must necessarily coincide. The passage is incomplete, but it establishes the key terms of the debate.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that motives always precede the action in time, regardless of causal theory.
Correction: Razi shows that this is not necessarily true; it depends on whether the combination of power and motive is an effective cause or a preparatory cause. If it is effective, the motive must be simultaneous with the action.

Key Takeaway

Refutation of Three Possibilities for an Effect from Multiple Independent Causes
Refutation of three possibilities for an effect from multiple independent causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical refutation of three possibilities for an effect arising from multiple independent causes, and why only one cause can produce a given effect.

Core Idea
Multiple independent causes for one effect lead to logical contradiction.

This passage presents a powerful argument against the possibility of an effect being produced by more than one independent cause. Through examining three hypothetical scenarios, the author demonstrates that each leads to an impossibility, reinforcing the principle that a single effect requires a single, specific cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three possibilities
Read the first sentence of the Arabic passage and its translation. The author lists three possibilities for an effect in relation to multiple independent causes: from one of them, from some without others, or from none.
These are the three candidates to be refuted.
2
Understand the refutation of the first possibility
The first possibility (effect from one cause) is impossible because with a complete independent cause, the effect becomes necessary, and necessity prevents attribution to another cause. If two independent causes existed, the effect would have to be dependent on both while being cut off from each – a contradiction.
This shows that the effect cannot be from one cause if others are also present.
3
Understand the refutation of the second possibility
The second possibility (effect from some of the causes but not others) is impossible because it would require a preponderance without a preponderant cause, which is absurd. Choosing one cause over another demands a reason.
No cause can be favored without a preponderant.
4
Understand the refutation of the third possibility
The third possibility (effect from none of the causes) is impossible because it would imply that a powerful agent with strong motives and no obstacles cannot act, which is absurd. Thus, the effect must occur from one cause, but as shown earlier, that leads to contradiction.
All three logical alternatives fail.
5
Grasp the overall conclusion
The author concludes that this presents a strong question in theology, highlighting a deep problem about causality and divine action: if multiple independent causes are impossible, how can we understand divine attributes and actions?
This sets up a topic for further theological discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا واحد منها ، أو يقع ببعضها دون البعض ، أو لا يقع بشيء منها . والأول باطل . لأن الفعل إنما احتاج إلى المرجح لكونه في نفسه ممكن الوجود والعدم، لكن المعلول مع العلة التامة ومع السبب المستقل يكون واجب الحصول . وإذا كان واجباً قوجوبه يمنعه من الانتساب إلى الغير فيكون ذلك الأثر السبب المستقل يمنعه من الاستناد إلى الثاني، وكونه مع الثاني يمنعه من الاستناد إلى الأول ، فلو وجد مع العلتين المستقلتين ، يلزم أن يكون مستنداً إلى كل واحد منهما حال كونه منقطعاً عن كل واحد منهما . وذلك محال . وأما القول بوقوع ذلك المعلول بأحد تلك العلل المستقلة ، فهذا أيضاً محال ، لأنه يقتضي ترجيح أحد طرفي الجائز على الآخر لا المرجح وهو محال . وأما القول بامتناع وقوعه بشيء منها ، فهو أيضاً محال ، لأنه يلزم منه ، أن القادر حال ما حصلت دواعيه إلى الفعل وتأكدت ولم يحصل لها مانع فإنه يمتنع الفعل عليه وذلك محال . فهذا سؤال قوي في هذا الموضع
This [effect] is from one of them, or it occurs by some of them without others, or it does not occur by any of them. The first is false, because the act needed the preponderant cause due to its being in itself possible of existence and non-existence, but the effect with the complete cause and with the independent cause is necessary of occurrence. And when it is necessary, its necessity prevents it from being attributed to another; thus that effect, the independent cause, prevents it from being attributed to the second, and its being with the second prevents it from being attributed to the first. So if it existed with the two independent causes, it would be necessary that it be dependent on each one of them while being cut off from each one of them. And that is impossible. As for the statement that the effect occurs by one of those independent causes, this also is impossible, because it would require favoring one of the two sides of the contingent over the other without a preponderant, and that is impossible. As for the statement that it is impossible for it to occur by any of them, that also is impossible, because it would entail that the powerful agent, when his motives for the act have occurred and become strong and no obstacle occurs for him, the act is impossible for him – and that is impossible. So this is a strong question in this place.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage refutes three possibilities regarding an effect that could be produced by multiple independent causes. The author first identifies three alternatives: (1) the effect occurs from one of them, (2) it occurs from some of them but not others, or (3) it does not occur from any of them. He then argues each is false. 

(1) The first possibility is false because an act requires a preponderant cause (al-murajjih) due to its being contingent; but with a complete cause (al-'illah al-tammah) and an independent cause (al-sabab al-mustaqill), the effect becomes necessary. Necessity prevents it from being attributed to another cause, leading to a contradiction if there are two independent causes – each would prevent attribution to the other, yet the effect would have to be dependent on both simultaneously, which is impossible. 

(2) The second possibility – that the effect occurs by one of the independent causes – is also impossible because it would require a preponderance without a preponderant cause, which is absurd. 

(3) The third possibility – that the effect does not occur by any of them – is likewise impossible because it would imply that when a powerful agent has strong motives and no obstacles, the act is impossible, which is absurd. 

The author concludes that this presents a strong objection in this context, highlighting a deep theological problem regarding causality and divine action.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that multiple independent causes can coexist for one effect without contradiction.
Correction: The argument shows that each scenario leads to impossibility: either contradiction, arbitrary preference, or failure of agency.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What does that reveal about your sincerity? Ask yourself honestly: are your motives truly strong? If not, what is holding you back?
?
This logical argument concludes that only Allah can be the true independent cause. Does your heart truly rest in that fact, or do you still look to other 'independent' causes for security—wealth, health, relationships? What would it take for you to live as if only Allah is the independent cause? Make a concrete plan to shift one area of reliance today.

The Rule for Conflicting Motives: The Strongest Prevails
The author defines the rule for conflicting motives: stronger prevails, equals cancel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how conflicting motives are resolved in rational causality: the strongest prevails and equals cancel.

Core Idea
In conflicting motives, only the strongest acts; equals neutralize each other.

This passage explains a key principle in causality concerning multiple motives that push in opposite directions. When motives conflict, we cannot simply assume they both influence the outcome. Instead, reason dictates that the strongest motive takes effect, and if they are equal, they cancel out. This rule is foundational for understanding how actions are determined.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's direct statement sink in.
Notice the two distinct cases presented.
2
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two scenarios: when motives differ in strength, and when they are equal. Mark the key phrases: 'effect belongs to the strongest' and 'conflict and cancel out'.
3
Reflect on the cancellation principle
Consider why equal motives would cancel completely. This is a logical consequence: two equal opposing forces result in no net effect. Think of an example in daily life.
This principle appears often in causal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني : وهو ما إذا كانت الدواعي متنافية ومتنازعة . فنقول : قد ذكرنا فيها تقدم أن أيها أقوى ، وأيها أضعف ؟ وعند اجتماعها يكون التأثير للأقوى . وإن تساوت في القوة تعارضت وتساقطت ويبقى ما كان على ما كان
As for the second division: it is when the motives are mutually exclusive and conflicting. We say: we have mentioned earlier which of them is stronger and which is weaker. And when they come together, the effect belongs to the strongest. And if they are equal in strength, they conflict and cancel out, and what remains continues as before.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage addresses the second case of multiple motives (al-dawa'i): when they are mutually exclusive and conflicting. The author states that the stronger motive among them takes effect, which is a standard principle in causal reasoning. If the motives are equal in strength, they neutralize each other, and the situation remains unchanged (i.e., the act does not occur or the state continues as it was). This is a logical consequence: two equally strong opposing forces cancel out, resulting in no net effect.

Timing of the Motive: Efficient vs. Preparatory Cause
The author discusses whether motives must precede or coincide with the act, based on causal role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the classification of the motive-power combination as either an efficient cause or a preparatory cause determines whether the motive must precede the act or be simultaneous with it.

Core Idea
The temporal status of a motive depends on its causal role: efficient causes coexist with their effects, preparatory causes precede them.

This lesson explores a key debate in kalam about the timing of motives relative to actions. The author presents two possibilities based on causal classification. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping how theologians model agency and causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses conditional 'if we say' to present two positions.
2
Identify the two causal classifications
The passage presents two types of cause: efficient (mu'aththirah) and preparatory (mu'iddah). Find where each is defined and note how the temporal requirement differs.
3
Understand the logical consequence
For each classification, the author derives the temporal relation: efficient cause requires simultaneity, preparatory cause requires precedence. Ask yourself why this follows from the definition of each cause.
The author also warns that if a preparatory cause were not prior, an infinite regress would result.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
This analysis shows that the timing of motives is not simple but depends on a prior commitment to the nature of causal agency. Consider how this might apply to debates about divine vs. human action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الرابع : هذه العلوم والاعتقادات والظنون التي سميناها بالدواعي ، يجب حصولها قبل حصول الفعل ، أو حال حدوثه وحصوله ؟ وهذا البحث مفرع على أن مجموع القدرة مع الداعي علة مؤثرة في وجود الفعل ، إن قلنا : ذلك المجموع علة معدة الحصول الفعل وأما العلة المؤثرة فهي موجود آخر. فإن قلنا : إنه علة مؤثرة وجب حصول الداعي حال حدوث الفعل ، لما ثبت أن المؤثر يجب أن يكون موجوداً حال حصول الأثر . وإن قلنا : إنه علة معدة لا مؤثرة فحينئذ نقول : إنه يجب تقدم الداعي على الفعل ، لأن العلة المعدة يجب تقدمها على المعلول . وإلا لزم وقوع التسلسل في الأسباب والمسببات دفعة واحدة وذلك محال . والله ولي التوفيق .
The fourth branch: These sciences, beliefs, and conjectures which we have called motives (al-dawa'i): must they occur before the occurrence of the act, or at the time of its occurrence and happening? This research is based on the fact that the combination of power (al-qudrah) and the motive is an effective cause in the existence of the act. If we say: that combination is a preparatory cause for the occurrence of the act, and as for the effective cause, it is another existent. For if we say: it is an effective cause, then the motive must be present at the time of the act's occurrence, because it is established that the effect must be present at the time of the effect's occurrence. And if we say: it is a preparatory cause, not effective, then we say: the motive must precede the act, because the preparatory cause must precede its effect. Otherwise, a happening of infinite regress in causes and effects all at once would result, and that is impossible. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage addresses the fourth branch concerning the temporal relation between motives (sciences, beliefs, conjectures) and the act. The author frames the question: must these motives be prior to the act or simultaneous with it? The answer depends on the causal nature of the combination of power (qudrah) and motive. 

Two positions are considered: 
1. If the combination is an efficient cause (ʿillah muʾaththirah), then the motive must be present at the very moment the act occurs, because an efficient cause must coexist with its effect. 
2. If the combination is merely a preparatory cause (ʿillah muʿiddah), then the motive must precede the act, because a preparatory cause must be temporally prior to its effect. If it were not, an infinite regress of simultaneous causes and effects would follow, which is impossible (muḥāl). 

The passage ends with a pious invocation.

Key Terms

قدرة
علة مؤثرة
علة معدة
معلول
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motive must always precede the act.
Correction: The author shows that if the combination is an efficient cause, the motive is simultaneous with the act; only if it is a preparatory cause must it precede.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is the motive required to be simultaneous with the act?
A) When the combination is a preparatory cause
B) When the combination is an efficient cause
C) Always, regardless of classification
D) Never, it must always precede

Key Takeaway
The temporal relation of a motive to an action is determined by whether the motive-power combination is an efficient cause (simultaneous) or a preparatory cause (prior).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have the ability to do something good but hesitate, waiting for a stronger motive, are you treating your motive as a preparatory cause when it should be an efficient cause? What would change if you acted simultaneously with the motive you already have?

?

The motive must exist at the time of action
The author argues that the motive must be simultaneous if the cause is effective, but precedes if preparatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between an effective cause and a preparatory cause in relation to the motive for an action.

Core Idea
The motive's timing depends on whether the cause is effective or preparatory.

This short passage by the author addresses a key question in causal reasoning: when must the motive for an action exist? The answer hinges on distinguishing between two types of causes—effective and preparatory. Let's examine the argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the structure: a principle, a conditional, and a consequence.
The passage is concise but packed with logical reasoning.
2
Identify the two types of causes
The passage discusses two kinds of causes: effective (المؤثر) and preparatory (العلة المعدة). See how each relates to the timing of the motive.
The effective cause is simultaneous, the preparatory cause precedes.
3
Understand the consequence of ignoring the distinction
The author warns that failing to distinguish leads to an impossible sequence of causes and effects occurring all at once.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حصول الداعي حال حدوث الفعل ، لما ثبت أن المؤثر يجب أن يكون موجوداً حال حصول الأثر . وإن قلنا : إنه علة معدة لا مؤثرة فحينئذ نقول : إنه يجب تقدم الداعي على الفعل ، لأن العلة المعدة يجب تقدمها على المعلول . وإلا لزم وقوع التسلسل في الأسباب والمسببات دفعة واحدة وذلك محال . والله ولي التوفيق .
The occurrence of the motive at the time of the action, because it has been established that the effective cause must exist at the time of the effect. And if we say: it is a preparatory cause, not an effective one, then we say: the motive must precede the action, because the preparatory cause must precede its effect. Otherwise, a sequence of causes and effects occurring all at once would be necessary, and that is impossible. And God is the guide to success.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a proof concerning the temporal relationship between the motive (الداعي) and the action. The author states the established principle: the effective cause (المؤثر) must exist at the same time as its effect. Then he considers the alternative: if the cause is merely preparatory (علة معدة) and not truly effective, then the motive must precede the action, as preparatory causes always precede their effects. He concludes that failing to distinguish these cases would lead to an impossible infinite regress or simultaneous causation. The passage ends with a pious formula.

Key Terms
الداعي
المؤثر
العلة المعدة
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The motive must always precede the action.
Correction: The author shows that if the cause is effective, the motive must exist at the time of the action, not before.

Checkpoint

C) The motive would always precede the action.
D) The effective cause would not need to exist at the time of the effect.

Key Takeaway
The timing of the motive for an action depends on the nature of the cause: effective causes are simultaneous, preparatory causes precede the action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Does Action Depend on Motive? Three Positions
The author opens Chapter Six with the question and summarizes three positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central question of Chapter Six and the three distinct positions held by philosophers, al-Basri, and the majority of theologians.

Core Idea
Action's dependence on motive: philosophers say yes, most theologians say no.

This lesson introduces the sixth chapter’s core problem: does an action require a motive to occur? The author quickly maps the scholarly landscape, naming three groups with contrasting answers. As you read, notice how the question frames the entire chapter and sets up the proofs to follow.

Guided Path

The question is whether action depends on motive or not.

?
If action truly does not depend on motive, then an accidental sin and a deliberate one could be seen differently. Does this lesson challenge your sense of personal responsibility? Reflect on a moment you acted without a clear motive—was that action less 'yours'? What does that mean for your accountability before Allah?

First Proof that Action Does Not Depend on Motive
Al-Razi presents the first proof for the independence of action from motive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's first proof that a capable agent can act without a motive, and how he establishes it through two premises.

Core Idea
Action does not require a motive – capability alone suffices.

Al-Razi begins his case by presenting a logical proof that action can occur without a motive. His argument rests on two key premises: that the agent can exist without any beliefs, and that in that state action is still possible. Let's work through the proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the overall argument
Read the passage to see the conditional structure: if the capable agent can exist without beliefs and still act, then action does not require a motive.
The proof is a conditional argument with two premises.
2
Examine the first premise
Identify the two evidences that prove a capable agent can exist devoid of all beliefs: moving a finger without knowing its parts, and disagreement about types of actions.
The first premise is crucial for showing capability does not require belief.
3
Examine the second premise
Understand the proof that action is valid without beliefs via contradiction: if not, then the agent would be both capable and not capable at the same time.
This premise uses the definition of capability to force the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج القائلون بأن صدور الفعل عن القادر لا يتوقف على الدواعي:
الحجة الأولى: إن القادر يمكن وجوده خالياً من كل الاعتقادات، وهو في هذه الحالة يمكن صدور الفعل عنه، وذلك يدل على أن صدور الفعل عن القادر لا يتوقف على حصول الداعي. فيفتقر في تقرير هذا الدليل إلى إثبات أمرين:
الأول: إن القادر يمكن وجوده خالياً عن كل الاعتقادات. ويدل عليه وجهان:
الأول: إن الواحد منا قادر على تحريك إصبعه. ومعلوم أن إصبعه جسم مركب من أجزاء كثيرة. وتلك المسافة مركبة من أجزاء كثيرة. وهذا القادر غير عالم بعدد أجزاء تلك الإصبع، وبعدد أجزاء تلك المسافة، فيثبت أن القادر قد يكون قادراً على ما لا يعلمه ولا يعتقده ولا يظنه.
الثاني: إن الناس اختلفوا في أن الأجناس المقدورة للعباد كم هي؟ فلو لزم من كونه قادراً على الشيء كونه عالماً به، لزم أن يكون العلم بالأجناس المقدورة للعباد علماً ضرورياً، ولما لم يكن كذلك، ثبت أنا قد نقدر على أشياء مع أنا لا نعلمها.
الأمر الثاني: في بيان أنا عند الخلو عن هذه الاعتقادات يصح الفعل منا. والدليل عليه: أنه لو لم يصح ذلك لزم أن يكون القادر على الشيء غير قادر عليه، لأن المعنى من كونه قادراً، كونه بحيث يصح أن يصدر منه الفعل. فإذا فرضناه قادراً عند الخلو من هذه الاعتقادات، ثم قلنا: إنه لا يصح منه الفعل، لزم أن نقول: إنه حال كونه قادراً على الفعل ما كان قادراً على الفعل. وذلك متناقض.
Those who claim that the occurrence of an action from the capable agent does not depend on motives argued: The first proof: The capable agent can exist devoid of all beliefs, and in this state the action can occur from him, and this indicates that the occurrence of the action from the capable agent does not depend on the attainment of the motive. So establishing this proof requires proving two matters: The first: The capable agent can exist devoid of all beliefs. Two aspects indicate this: The first: One of us is capable of moving his finger. It is known that his finger is a body composed of many parts, and that distance is composed of many parts. This capable agent is not aware of the number of parts of that finger nor of the number of parts of that distance, so it is established that the capable agent may be capable of something he does not know, nor believe, nor opine. The second: People have disagreed about how many genera of acts are within the capacity of servants. If it were necessary that from being capable of something he is aware of it, then knowledge of the genera of acts within the capacity of servants would be necessary knowledge. Since it is not so, it is established that we may be capable of things while we do not know them. The second matter: Explaining that when we are devoid of these beliefs, action is valid from us. The proof of it: If that were not valid, then it would follow that the capable agent of something is not capable of it, because the meaning of being capable is that action can validly issue from him. If we suppose him capable when devoid of these beliefs, then we say that action is not valid from him, we would have to say that while being capable of the action, he was not capable of the action. That is contradictory.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the position that the occurrence of an action from a capable agent does not depend on the presence of a motive (داعي). The proof is structured as a conditional argument: if the capable agent can exist without any beliefs (اعتقادات) and still act, then action does not require a motive. To establish this, two premises must be proven. The first premise (الأمر الأول) is that the capable agent can indeed exist devoid of all beliefs. Two evidences are given for this: first, we can move our finger without knowing its exact composition, proving that capability can exist without detailed belief; second, the fact that people disagree about the number of action-types shows that knowledge of them is not necessary, otherwise it would be necessary knowledge, which it is not. The second premise (الأمر الثاني) is that in this state of being devoid of beliefs, action is still validly possible from the agent. The proof is that if it were not, then we would have a contradiction: the agent is capable yet action cannot issue from him, which contradicts the definition of capability (كونه بحيث يصح أن يصدر منه الفعل). Thus, the argument concludes that action does not depend on motive.

Key Terms
قادر
داعي
صدور الفعل
اعتقادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof denies that motives ever accompany actions or that they have any role.
Correction: The proof only argues that motives are not a necessary condition for action, not that they are irrelevant.

Checkpoint
What is the first premise of the first proof in this passage?
A) The capable agent cannot exist without beliefs.
B) The capable agent can exist devoid of all beliefs.
C) Action always requires a motive.
D) The capable agent is always aware of his actions.

Key Takeaway
The first proof establishes that a capable agent can act without a motive, using the possibility of action without belief and the contradiction if it were otherwise.

?
If actions do not require motives, then feelings like 'I don't feel like it' lose their power. In what area of your life are you waiting for a feeling before acting? How will you act on this truth starting now?

Proof from the Unmindful and Sleeping Person
Empirical examples of unmindful and sleeping persons acting without motives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how empirical examples of unmindful and sleeping persons demonstrate that actions can occur without prior motives.

Core Idea
Actions can occur without motives, as shown by everyday examples.

Sanusi now turns to everyday experience to argue that motives are not necessary for action. He describes cases of unmindful and sleeping individuals who perform voluntary actions without any conscious motive. These examples serve as empirical evidence against the claim that every action requires a prior motive.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's structure
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to see the overall structure: two cases (unmindful and sleeping), each with two sub-examples.
Notice that Sanusi uses the phrase 'its exposition has two aspects' for each case.
2
Examine the unmindful examples
Identify the two examples for the unmindful: moving a finger while mentally occupied, and an archer hitting an unintended target. Understand why each lacks a motive.
In the finger example, the person is heedless of the movement. In the archer example, the hitting is accidental.
3
Examine the sleeping examples
Identify the two examples for the sleeping: breathing (voluntary action) and moving/speaking without knowledge or belief.
Sleep prevents the presence of motives like belief or supposition.
4
Understand the conclusion
Connect the examples to the final claim: 'So here action has occurred without motive.' Reflect on how this supports the broader argument against necessary motives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: إن الساهي والنائم، قد يصدر الفعل عنهما مع عدم الدواعي. أما في حق الساهي: فتقريره من وجهين: الأول: إن الواحد منه قد يحرك إصبعه حال ما يكون مشغول القلب بعمل آخر، وفي تلك الحالة كان غافلاً عن تحريك ذلك الإصبع، مع أنه فعله. فههنا قد حصل الفعل من غير الداعي. الثاني: إن الرامي قد يرمي إلى شيء فيصيب شيئاً آخر. فتلك الإصابة الواقعة على سبيل الخطأ: فعله. مع أنه ما دعاه الداعي إلى ذلك الفعل، فيثبت أن الفعل قد يحصل بدون الداعي. وأما في حق النائم: فتقريره من وجهين: الأول: إن النائم يتنفس وهذا التنفس فعل اختياري، وهو قد حصل من غير الداعي، لأن النوم مانع من الاعتقاد أو الظن. الثاني: إن النائم قد ينتقل من أحد الجانبين إلى الثاني، وربما يتلفظ بالكلمات الكثيرة، مع أنه في تلك الساعة لم يحصل في قلبه شيء من العلوم والاعتقادات، لأن النوم مانع منها. فهاهنا قد حصل الفعل من غير الداعي.
The second proof: The unmindful and the sleeping person may perform action while lacking motives. Regarding the unmindful: its exposition has two aspects: first, one of them may move his finger while his heart is occupied with another work, and in that state he was heedless of moving that finger, yet he did it. So here action occurred without motive. Second: the archer may shoot at something and hit something else. That hitting occurring by mistake is his action, yet no motive called him to that action, so it is established that action may occur without motive. Regarding the sleeping: its exposition has two aspects: first, the sleeping person breathes, and this breathing is a voluntary action, and it occurs without motive because sleep prevents belief or supposition. Second: the sleeping person may move from one side to the other, and maybe utter many words, while at that moment nothing of knowledge or beliefs occurs in his heart because sleep prevents them. So here action has occurred without motive.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage presents empirical evidence for the occurrence of action without a prior motive. It is divided into two cases: the unmindful (sāhī) and the sleeping (nā'im). For the unmindful, two examples are given: first, moving a finger while mentally occupied elsewhere; second, an archer accidentally hitting a target. In both, the action is performed without an accompanying motive. For the sleeping person, again two examples: first, breathing—a voluntary action—occurs without belief or supposition due to sleep; second, changing position or speaking in sleep occurs without any knowledge or beliefs. The purpose is to demonstrate that actions can and do take place without the presence of a specific motive (dā'ī), which is a key point in the debate about the necessity of motives for action.

Key Terms
ساهي
نائم
داعي
فعل اختياري
غافل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All voluntary actions require a conscious motive.
Correction: Empirical examples of unmindful and sleeping persons show that voluntary actions can occur without any motive.

Checkpoint

A) Two
B) Four
C) Six
D) Eight

Key Takeaway
Empirical examples of unmindful and sleeping persons demonstrate that voluntary actions can occur without prior motives.

Proof from Skilled Artisans Acting Automatically
Skilled persons perform complex actions without attention or motive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how skilled automatic action challenges the necessity of motives for action.

Core Idea
Skill allows action without attention or motive.

This lesson presents a philosophical proof based on everyday experience: when a person becomes highly skilled at a craft, they can perform complex actions while being completely heedless of the details. This shows that motives or conscious attention are not always required for action, raising important questions about causality and agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the logical flow: skill leads to automaticity, then heedlessness, then the conclusion about motives.
2
Understand the skilled artisan scenario
The passage gives examples like a skilled writer or musician who performs complex actions without attending to each detail. Visualize these scenarios.
3
Identify the logical inference
If the person is heedless (ghāfil) of the parts, they cannot be knowing, believing, or even supposing them. Yet the action occurs. This is the key inference.
Contrast this with the common assumption that action requires conscious intention.
4
Grasp the conclusion about motives
The passage ends by stating that these actions occur while motives (dawā'ī) are not present. This shows that motives are not necessary for action.
Think about how this argument relates to broader discussions of causality and divine action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة: قالوا: الإنسان إذا صار ماهراً في حرفة معينة، مثل ما إذا صار ماهراً في صنعة الكتابة، أو في ضرب الطنبور، أو في غيرهما، فإنه قد يصير بحيث يكتب شيئاً كثيراً، أو يضرب الطنبور مدة معينة مع أنه يكون بقلبه غافلاً عن آحاد تلك الحروف، وعن آحاد تلك النقرات. وإذا كان غافلاً عنها يمتنع كونه عالماً بها، أو معتقداً لها، أو ظاناً بها. ففي هذه الصورة قد وجدت هذه الأفعال، مع أن الدواعي غير حاصلة فيها.
The third proof: They said: Man, when he becomes skilled in a particular craft, such as when he becomes skilled in the art of writing, or playing the tanbur, or other such, he may reach a state where he writes extensively or plays the tanbur for a certain period while his heart is heedless of the individual letters or individual strokes. If he is heedless of them, it is impossible for him to be knowledgeable of them, or believing in them, or supposing them. In this scenario, these actions have occurred while the motives are not present.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage uses the phenomenon of skill and habit to argue that actions can occur without motives. When a person becomes highly skilled at a craft like writing or playing an instrument, they can perform the action automatically, without attending to each individual component. The skilled writer is not conscious of each letter; the skilled musician is not aware of each stroke. Since he is heedless (ghāfil) of these details, he cannot be said to have knowledge, belief, or even a supposition about them. Nonetheless, the action occurs. This shows that motives (dawā'ī) are not necessary for the occurrence of action.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Complex actions always require conscious attention and deliberate intentionality.
Correction: Skilled performance shows that actions can be carried out automatically, without attention to details, and without motive.

The Proof from Choice Between Equals
The fourth proof: choosing between equals shows action without motive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from choice between equals demonstrates that action can occur without a specific motive.

Core Idea
Choice between identical options proves action can happen without any reason.

This lesson presents a famous theological argument: the case of a person choosing between two perfectly equal options. The argument shows that such a choice is made without any factor that would tip the scales, proving that actions can arise without a prior motive. This challenges the causal necessity of motives and supports the broader thesis that the will can act without a determining reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument's scenario
Pay attention to the examples: cups, loaves, and roads. Notice that the person has no preference between the options.
This is a classic Buridan's ass scenario.
2
Analyze the disjunction
The argument presents a logical disjunction: either the person prefers one option or does not. The second option leads to an absurd consequence.
Focus on why not choosing is impossible.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since the person must choose, and there is no factor that makes one option better, the choice happens without a motive.
This is the key point: action without a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة: قالوا: المخير بين شرب قدحين، وأكل رغيفين، والهارب إذا وصل إلى طريقين متساويين، فإنه يرجح أحدهما على الآخر لا مرجح. فههنا الفعل قد حصل من غير الداعي. وتمام التقرير: أنه عند حصول الاستواء بين القدحين والرغيفين، إما أن يرجح أحدهما على الآخر، أو لا يرجح. والثاني باطل، لأنه يلزم أن يقال: إن العطشان شدة العطش، لا يأخذ القدحين، بل يبقى ممتنعاً عنهما إلى أن يموت من العطش، مع كون القدحين حاضرين عنده من غير مانع، ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل فيثبت أنه لا بد وأن يأخذ أحدهما من غير مرجح. وإذا ثبت هذا فقد حصل الفعل، لا للدواعي. وهو المطلوب.
The fourth proof: They said: The one who is given a choice between drinking two cups and eating two loaves, and the fugitive when he reaches two equal roads, prefers one over the other without a preference. Here action has occurred without motive. The full exposition: When there is equivalence between the two cups and loaves, either he prefers one over the other or he does not prefer. The second is false, because it would imply that the thirsty person intensely thirsty does not take either cup, but remains abstaining from them until he dies of thirst, while the cups are present with him without obstacle, and it is known that this is false. So it is established that he must take one without a preferrer. And if this is established, then action has occurred, not for motives. That is what was sought.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents the famous argument from the problem of Buridan's ass. The scenario: a person is given two identical cups of drink or two loaves, or a fugitive reaches two equally good roads. They must choose one despite no preference (murajjih). The proof argues by disjunction: either the person prefers one over the other, or does not. The second option (no choice) is absurd because it would lead to starvation despite availability. Therefore, choice occurs without a prior motive. This shows that action can happen without a specific motive, supporting the broader thesis that motives are not necessary for action.

Key Terms
مخير
قدح
رغيف
طريق
مرجح
استواء
داعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every choice must have a reason or motive that makes one option better than the other.
Correction: This argument shows that choice can occur even when there is no such reason, as in the case of equal options.

Checkpoint
What is the absurd consequence of the person not choosing between the two equal cups?
A) He would drink both
B) He would die of thirst
C) He would become confused
D) He would ask for help

Key Takeaway
Action can occur without a motivating factor, as demonstrated by the choice between identical options.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a choice between two equally good options, do you ever delay because you lack a clear reason? This argument says inaction is absurd. What has your hesitation cost you spiritually?
?
The thirsty man dies if he doesn't choose. What area of your life—repentance, charity, or obedience—have you left undecided because you didn't 'feel motivated'? What will you choose today?
?
If action can happen without a motive, then your excuse 'I don't have the desire to worship' is invalid. Will you act without feeling, simply because Allah deserves your obedience?

The Sleeper's Action: Objection to the Necessity of Motive
This is the second objection against the necessity of a motive for action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the claim that every action requires a motive, as exemplified by the sleeper's actions.

Core Idea
Sleepers act without knowledge or belief, so motive is not always required.

The argument so far claimed that every action needs a motive (dā'ī). But what about a sleeping person who turns over or speaks? At that moment, he has no awareness, knowledge, or belief—so how can his action be motivated? This passage presents this objection and challenges the opponent to explain how action can occur without a motive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the key terms: نائم (sleeper), فعل (action), داعي (motive).
2
Identify the argument
The passage presents a counterexample: a sleeper acts without knowledge or belief. Trace the logical steps: sleeper acts → no knowledge (sleep prevents it) → no motive → action without motive.
The sleeper's movements and speech are given as examples.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider the challenge: if action can occur without a motive, then the claim that every action requires a motive is false. Ask yourself: what assumptions does this argument rely on? (e.g., that motive depends on knowledge or belief)
This objection targets the opponent's definition of motive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن النائم قد ينتقل من أحد الجانبين إلى الثاني ، وربما يتلفظ بالكلمات الكثيرة ، مع أنه في تلك الساعة لم يحصل في قلبه شيء من العلوم والاعتقادات ، لأن النوم مانع منها. فهاهنا قد حصل الفعل من غير الداعي
The second: indeed the sleeper may move from one side to the other, and perhaps utter many words, while at that moment nothing of knowledge or belief occurs in his heart, because sleep prevents them. So here the action has occurred without the motive.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection (hujja) against the claim that every action depends on a motive (da'ī). The objectors argue that a sleeping person can perform actions such as turning from side to side or speaking, while being completely unaware of what he is doing. Since sleep prevents the formation of knowledge ('ilm) or belief (i'tiqād) — which are prerequisites for a motive — the action must have occurred without any motive. Therefore, the existence of such actions proves that action can occur without a motive, contradicting the opposing view.

Key Terms
داعي
فعل
نائم
اعتقاد
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the sleeper's actions are still motivated by subconscious desires.
Correction: The argument assumes that motive requires conscious awareness and belief, which are absent during sleep. The objector's point is that if motive requires consciousness, then sleeper's actions are counterexamples.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, why does the sleeper's action occur without a motive?
A) Because the sleeper chooses to act deliberately.
B) Because sleep prevents awareness and belief, so no motive is present.
C) Because the sleeper is compelled to act by an external force.
D) Because the sleeper has a hidden motive that is not apparent.

Key Takeaway
The sleeper's actions provide a counterexample to the claim that every action requires a motive, challenging the opponent to refine their definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If actions can truly occur without a conscious motive, what does that mean for your accountability before Allah for actions done in heedlessness? Will you now seek to become more mindful of your every action?
?
The sleeper's actions are used as a philosophical objection. But beyond philosophy, examine your own 'sleep'—times when your heart is veiled from awareness. How can you awaken yourself so that your actions reflect genuine intention and knowledge?

The Third Objection: Actions Without Awareness
Third objection: skilled actions without conscious awareness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection to the necessity of motives for actions, specifically how a skilled craftsman's automatic actions challenge the link between awareness, motive, and action.

Core Idea
Skilled actions can occur without conscious awareness, challenging the need for a motive.

The third objection focuses on a familiar experience: a skilled person can perform a complex series of actions while mentally elsewhere. When writing or playing an instrument, we often execute each movement without thinking about it. This phenomenon seems to contradict the claim that every action requires a conscious motive for each part. Let's examine how the objectors articulate this challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Pay attention to the scenario: a skilled craftsman (like a writer or musician) acts while heedless of individual details.
The passage uses examples of writing and playing tambour.
2
Identify the key premises
Notice that the objectors claim being heedless (ghāfil) of particulars prevents knowledge or belief about them. This is crucial because knowledge/belief is needed for a motive.
Heedlessness is contrasted with awareness.
3
Trace the conclusion
The conclusion is that actions occur without motives in such cases. Ask yourself: does this contradict the earlier arguments that motives are necessary?
The objection targets the idea that every action requires a specific motive for each part.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Consider how this objection differs from the first two. It relies on observed human behavior rather than logical necessity. What might a defender of the necessity of motives say?
Think about the difference between a general motive (e.g., to write) and specific motives for each letter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : قالوا : الإنسان إذا صار ماهراً في حرفة معينة ، مثل ما إذا صار ماهراً في صنعة الكتابة ، أو في ضرب الطنبور ، أو في غيرهما ، فإنه قد يصير بحيث يكتب شيئاً كثيراً ، أو يضرب الطنبور مدة معينة مع أنه يكون بقلبه غافلاً عن آحاد تلك الحروف ، وعن آحاد تلك النقرات . وإذا كان غافلاً عنها يمتنع كونه عالماً بها ، أو معتقداً لها ، أو ظانا بها . ففي هذه الصورة قد وجدت هذه الأفعال ، مع أن الدواعي غير حاصلة فيها .
The third argument: they said: a person, when he becomes skilled in a particular craft, such as when he becomes skilled in the craft of writing or in playing the tambour, or in other things, then he may reach a state where he writes a lot, or plays the tambour for a certain period while his heart is heedless of the individual letters and the individual strokes. And when he is heedless of them, it is impossible that he has knowledge of them, or belief in them, or an opinion about them. So in this situation, these actions have occurred, while the motives are not present in them.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection. The objectors claim that a person highly skilled in a craft (e.g., writing or playing an instrument) can perform complex actions automatically, without attending to each detail. For instance, a skilled writer writes many letters while being mentally absent from each particular letter. Since he is unaware (ghāfil) of these details, he cannot have knowledge, belief, or even an opinion about them — all of which are prerequisites for a motive. Despite this lack of motive, the actions occur. Hence, actions can exist without motives.

The Fourth Argument: Choosing Between Equals Without a Motive
This is the fourth objection: a person presented with equal options still chooses one without a motive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that choice between equal options can occur without a motive and its implications for causality.

Core Idea
Choosing between equals without a motive proves that action can occur without a cause.

This lesson examines a classic objection to the principle of causality. The opponents present everyday scenarios where a person must choose between two perfectly identical options, yet still makes a choice. The argument claims that such a choice proves that actions can occur without a motive. Let's analyze the logic and its response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the phrase 'لا المرجع' — it may be an OCR error for 'لا بالمرجح'.
2
Identify the key example
The passage uses examples of a person choosing between two identical cups or loaves, and a fugitive at two equally long roads. Understand why these examples are chosen: they represent perfect equality.
3
Understand the dilemma
The argument presents a dilemma: either the person chooses one option or not. If not, absurd consequences follow (e.g., dying of thirst). If yes, the choice occurs without a preponderating motive. Grasp the logical structure.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that action can occur without a motive, challenging the idea that every action requires a cause. Consider how this connects to the broader debate on causality.
This argument is often used to defend free will or the possibility of choice without determining factors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : قالوا : المخير بين شرب قدحين ، وأكل رغيفين ، والهارب إذا وصل إلى طريقين متساويين ، فإنه يرجح أحدهما على الآخر ، لا المرجع. فههنا الفعل قد حصل من غير الداعي. وتمام التقرير : أنه عند حصول الاستواء بين القدحين والرغيفين ، إما أن يرجح أحدهما على الآخر ، أو لا يرجح. والثاني باطل ، لأنه يلزم أن يقال : إن العطشان شدة العطش ، لا يأخذ القدحين، بل يبقى ممتنعاً عنهما إلى أن يموت من العطش ، مع كون القدحين حاضرين عنده من غير مانع ، ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل فيثبت أنه لا بد وأن يأخذ أحدهما من غير مرجح ، وإذا ثبت هذا فقد حصل الفعل ، لا للدواعي . وهو المطلوب
The fourth argument: they said: the one who is given a choice between drinking two cups and eating two loaves, and the fugitive when he reaches two equally long roads, then he preponderates one over the other, not by a preponderator (?). So here the action has occurred without the motive. The full explanation: when equality occurs between the two cups and the two loaves, either he preponderates one over the other, or he does not. The second is false, because it would necessitate that the thirsty person, despite severe thirst, would not take the cups, but would remain abstaining from them until he dies of thirst, while the cups are present with him without any obstacle. And it is known that this is false. So it is established that he must take one of them without a preponderator. And when this is established, the action has occurred, not for motives. That is what is sought.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection. The objectors consider situations where a person faces two perfectly equal options: e.g., two identical cups of water or two roads equal in length. In such cases, the person must choose one despite no factor making one preferable. If he did not choose, he would die of thirst, which is absurd. Therefore, the choice occurs without any preponderating motive (tarjīḥ). Thus, action can exist without a motive. The phrase 'لا المرجع' (not by a preponderator) emphasizes that the selection is not based on any distinguishing factor.

Key Terms
تخيير
استواء
مرجح
داعي
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies free will.
Correction: The argument actually defends the possibility of choice without a determining motive, supporting free will.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, what would happen if the thirsty person did not choose one of the two equal cups?
A) He would eventually drink both.
B) He would die of thirst.
C) He would remain undecided forever.
D) A motive would eventually appear.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument uses the necessity of choice between equals to claim that actions can occur without a motive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Reflect on a time you made a choice without a clear reason. Did you attribute it to whim, or did you see the hand of Allah? How can this argument deepen your reliance on Him in moments of indecision?

The Fifth Argument: Slow Motion and Unconscious Acts
Sanusi presents the fifth argument using slow motion to prove action without awareness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the phenomenon of slow motion serves as evidence that actions can occur without conscious intent, according to two metaphysical frameworks.

Core Idea
Slow motion exposes action without awareness, challenging the necessity of motives.

This lesson examines the fifth objection raised against the theory of causation requiring conscious intent. The argument uses slow motion as a test case: whether framed in terms of indivisible atoms or a quality of motion, the agent is unaware of crucial components of their action. This shows that actions can occur without attending motives.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi presents two alternatives.
2
Identify the two frameworks
The passage describes two ways to interpret slow motion: one based on indivisible atoms (jawhar fard) and one based on a quality (kayfiyya) of motion. See how each leads to unawareness.
3
Follow the argument for each
For each framework, note what the agent produces and what they are unaware of. In the first, they produce rests without awareness; in the second, they produce motion and slowness without awareness.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Both cases show that action can occur without the agent attending to a motive. That challenges the view that all action requires a conscious motive.
This is the core of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن بطء الحركة إما أن يكون عبارة عن تحلل السكنات بناء على إثبات القول بالجوهر الفرد ؛ أو عبارة عن كيفية قائمة بالحركة بناء على نفي الجوهر الفرد. فإن كان الحق هو الأول . فالقوس الذي يأتي بالحركة يكون آتياً بتلك السكنات في أثناء تلك الحركات مع كونه غافلا عنها . فقد حصل الفعل لا للدواعي . وإن كان الحق هو الثاني ، كان الآتي بالحركة البطيئة آتياً بفعلين : أحدهما : نفس الحركة . والثاني : البطء القائم بتلك الحركة ، مع كونه غافلاً عن كونه آتياً بنوعين مختلفين من الفعل . فيكون آتياً بالفعل حال كونه غافلا عنه. وذلك يقتضي حصول الفعل من غير الداعي
The fifth argument: Slow motion is either an expression of the dissolution of rests (sakanāt) based on affirming the claim of the indivisible atom (jawhar al-fard), or an expression of a quality (kayfiyya) subsisting in the motion based on denying the indivisible atom. If the truth is the first, then the agent who produces the motion produces those rests during those motions while being heedless of them. So the action has occurred without motives. And if the truth is the second, then the producer of the slow motion produces two actions: one is the motion itself, and the second is the slowness subsisting in that motion, while being heedless of his producing two different types of action. So he produces the action while being heedless of it. And that entails the occurrence of action without the motive.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection, which appeals to the phenomenon of slow motion. Two theoretical frameworks are considered: (1) The view that posits indivisible atoms (jawhar fard), which explains slow motion as composed of discrete moments of rest (sakanāt) interspersed with motion. In this case, the agent who causes the motion is unaware of these individual rests, yet they occur—action without motive. (2) The view that denies the indivisible atom, explaining slowness as a quality (kayfiyya) supervening on motion. Here, the agent produces both the motion and its slowness while being unaware of producing two distinct types of action. Again, action occurs without conscious intent. Thus, under either metaphysical assumption, slow motion provides an example of action without a motive.

Key Terms
بطء الحركة
سكنات
جوهر فرد
كيفية
فعل
غافل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Agents are always fully aware of all aspects of their actions, including the components of slow motion.
Correction: This argument shows that in slow motion, the agent is unaware of the rests or the additional quality of slowness, proving action can occur without full awareness.

Checkpoint
In the fifth argument, how does the view of indivisible atoms (jawhar fard) explain slow motion?
A) As a continuous flow without breaks.
B) As a series of motions with interspersed rests.
C) As a quality added to the motion.
D) As a single, indivisible act.

Key Takeaway
Slow motion provides a concrete example where action occurs without the agent's conscious awareness of its components.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Think of a habitual action you perform daily—like walking, typing, or driving. Are you aware of every small movement it involves? How does Sanusi's argument about slow motion confront your assumption that you consciously intend every part of your actions? Will you now pay more attention to the unnoticed aspects of your deeds, or will you accept that much of what you do happens without your conscious motive?
?
The agent in the argument is 'heedless' (غافل) of producing rests or qualities. Can you recall a moment when you did something good or harmful without fully realizing it at the time? Does this possibility of unintentional action frighten or free you? How will you become more mindful of your automatic actions, knowing that they still count as your deeds?
?
If action can occur without a motive, then what justifies your claim that you are the sole author of your actions? This argument challenges the necessity of conscious motives—does it also challenge your sense of responsibility? The Sixth Argument: Motive Eliminates Free Will
This is the sixth objection: requiring a motive makes free choice indistinguishable from necessary causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the dependence of action on motive is argued to eliminate the distinction between free and necessary agents, and why this argument is considered false based on necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
Motive-dependence turns free choice into a myth.

This lesson presents the sixth and most fundamental objection against the Ash'ari theory of motive. The argument challenges the very possibility of free will by showing that if actions always follow motives, then human action becomes indistinguishable from natural necessity. Before we examine the counterarguments, we must grasp the logical force of this objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the objection, the explanation, the proof of falsehood, and the mention of responses.
Pay attention to the technical terms like 'موجب بالذات' and 'فاعل مختار'.
2
Understand the core logic
The argument shows that if the presence of motive necessitates action and its absence makes action impossible, then there is no room for capability (tamakkun) or choice (ikhtiyār).
This step is crucial: grasp the dichotomy of necessity and impossibility.
3
Grasp the disproof
The disproof relies on our intuitive knowledge (al-'ulūm al-ḍarūriyya) that voluntary human action is fundamentally different from natural causation like fire burning.
Think about the example of fire: it necessarily burns, but we know our actions are not like that.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Consider why this objection is powerful and why the proponents of the motive theory must provide strong answers.
The passage mentions that they have answered with clear and strong arguments, which will be studied later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : إن توقف الفعل على الداعي يوجب أن لا يبقى فرق بين الموجب بالذات وبين الفاعل المختار . وهذا باطل . بيان الأول : إنا سنبين أن عند حصول الداعية يجب الفعل وجوباً ضرورياً ، لا يمكن نقضه ، وعند عدم الداعية يمتنع وقوع الفعل ، ومعلوم أنه لا حال غير هاتين الحالتين ، فإذا كان الفعل في إحدى الحالتين واجباً ، وفي الحالة الثانية ممتنعاً. فحينئذ يلزم أن لا يحصل التمكن والاختيار في شيء من الأحوال البتة . فيثبت أن توقف الفعل على حصول الداعي، يقتضي أن لا يبقى فرق البتة بين الموجب بالذات وبين الفاعل المختار . وبيان أن هذا باطل : إنه من جملة العلوم الضرورية الفرق بين كون الإنسان متحركاً بقدرته واختياره وبين كون الحجر ناراً ، لا بطبعه ، وكون النار محرقة بطبعها ، فيثبت : أن القول بتوقف الفعل على الداعي : يفضي إلى الباطل المذكور ، فوجب أن يكون باطلاً . وأما القائلون بأن الفعل يتوقف على الداعي . فقد أجابوا عن هذه الدلائل بأجوبة واضحة قوية . وقالوا :
The sixth argument: The dependence of action upon the motive necessitates that no difference remains between the necessary agent by essence (al-mūjib bi al-dhāt) and the free agent (al-fā'īl al-mukhtār). And this is false. Explanation of the first: we will show that when the motive occurs, the action becomes necessary in a necessary way that cannot be broken, and when the motive is absent, the occurrence of the action is impossible. And it is known that there is no state other than these two states. So if the action is in one state necessary and in the second state impossible, then it follows that capability (tamakkun) and choice (ikhtiyār) occur in no state at all. So it is established that the dependence of action upon the occurrence of the motive entails that no difference at all remains between the necessary agent by essence and the free agent. And the demonstration that this is false: it is among the necessary sciences (al-'ulūm al-ḍarūriyya) the distinction between a human being moving by his power and choice and a stone falling (?), not by its nature, and fire burning by its nature. So it is established that the saying that action depends on the motive leads to the mentioned falsehood, so it must be false. And as for those who say that action depends on the motive, they have answered these proofs with clear and strong answers, and they said:
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth and most fundamental objection. The argument is: If an action necessarily occurs when a motive is present and is impossible when the motive is absent, then there are only two states: necessity and impossibility. In neither state is there genuine capability (tamakkun) or choice (ikhtiyār). This would collapse the distinction between a free agent (fā'īl mukhtār) and a necessary agent (mūjib bi al-dhāt), such as fire that necessarily burns. But our intuitive knowledge (ḍarūrī) tells us that there is a clear difference between voluntary human action and natural causation. Therefore, the claim that action depends on motive must be false. The passage concludes by noting that the proponents of the motive theory have given strong responses to these arguments, which will be presented next.

Key Terms
توقف
داعي
موجب بالذات
فاعل مختار
علوم ضرورية
تمكن
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motive-based action is compatible with free will.
Correction: The argument shows that if action is necessitated by motive, there is no genuine capability or choice, so it is not free.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth argument, what does the dependence of action on motive eliminate?
A) The possibility of action altogether.
B) The distinction between a free agent and a necessary agent.
C) The existence of the motive itself.
D) The need for a creator.

Key Takeaway
The dependence of action on motive leads to the problematic conclusion that free will is an illusion, but this is refuted by our intuitive knowledge of the difference between voluntary action and natural causation.
