الحجة الثالثة: إن قضية الإمكان والجواز قضية واحدة. فإن كان المفهوم من الجواز والإمكان محوجاً إلى المرجح، فليكن كذلك في كل المواضع، وإن لم يكن محوجاً إلى المؤثر، فليكن كذلك في كل المواضع. فأما القول بأنه محوج إلى المؤثر في موضع دون موضع، مع أنا لا نجد في العقل فرقاً بين الموضعين، فذلك مما لا يقبله العقل. إذا عرفت هذا، فنقول: وافقتمونا على أن الجوهر، لما جاز حصوله في هذا الحيز بدلاً عن ذلك، وفي ذاك بدلاً عن هذا، فإنه يمتنع رجحان أحدهما على الآخر، إلا المرجح. وأيضاً وافقتمونا على أن الممكن لما جاز وجوده، وجاز عدمه، فإنه يمتنع رجحان أحد الجانبين على الآخر إلا المرجح. فكذا هاهنا، لما كانت القدرة صالحة للفعل والترك، امتنع رجحان أحدهما على الآخر إلا المرجح. ضرورة أنه لا فرق بين هذه الصفة بأسرها في باب الجواز والإمكان. فإن قالوا: الفرق بين البابين أن كون الذات متحيزة، يقتضي صحة الحصول في كل الأحياز على البدل. وتوقيف التعيين على أمر منفصل لا يفضي إلى المحال. فلا جرم قلنا به. وأما في حق القادر، فإن القول بأن رجحان أحد الجانبين على الآخر يتوقف على أمر منفصل يفضي إلى المحال، فوجب أن لا نقول به. وبيان هذا الكلام إن كون القادر قادراً يقتضي صحة كل واحد من الضدين، على البدل. فلو حكمنا بتوقف التعيين على أمر منفصل، لكان عند حصول ذلك الأمر المنفصل يصير القادر موجباً. لأنا سنبين أن الفعل عند حدوث الداعية الجازمة، يصير واجب الوقوع، لكن القول بأن القادر حال كونه
The Third Proof: The principle of possibility (imkān) and permissibility (jawāz) is one principle. If the concept of permissibility and possibility necessitated the preponderator (al-murajjiḥ), then let it be so in all cases. And if it did not necessitate the effecter (al-muʾaththir), then let it be so in all cases. As for the statement that it necessitates the effecter in one case but not another, while we find no rational difference between the two cases, the intellect does not accept that. Once you know this, we say: You agree with us that for the substance (al-jawhar), since it is permissible for it to obtain in this location alternatively to that, and in that alternatively to this, then preponderance of one over the other is impossible except by a preponderator. Likewise, you agree with us that for the possible (al-mumkin), since its existence is permissible and its non-existence is permissible, then preponderance of one side over the other is impossible except by a preponderator. So too here, since capability (al-qudra) is fit for both act and omission, preponderance of one over the other is impossible except by a preponderator. Necessarily, there is no difference between all these cases in the category of permissibility and possibility. If they say: The difference between the two categories is that the essence being spatial (mutaḥayyiz) implies the validity of obtaining in all locations alternately. And suspending specification on a separate matter does not lead to impossibility; hence we say it. But for the capable agent, the statement that preponderance of one side over the other depends on a separate matter leads to impossibility, so we must not say it. The explanation of this statement is that the capable agent being capable implies the validity of each of the two opposites alternately. So if we judged that specification depends on a separate matter, then when that separate matter occurs, the capable agent would become necessitated (mūjib). For we will explain that the act, when the decisive motive (al-dāʿiya al-jāzima) occurs, becomes necessary of occurrence. But the statement that the capable agent when he is
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the necessity of a preponderator (المرجح) in the case of a capable agent. It is a classification and comparative argument. The author begins by stating that the principle of possibility (الإمكان) and permissibility (الجواز) is uniform. If permissibility necessitates a preponderator in one case, it must in all; if not, it never does. To claim a difference without rational basis is unacceptable. Then the author builds on agreed premises: you (opponents) agree that for a substance (الجوهر) to be in one location rather than another requires a preponderator, and for a possible being (الممكن) existence over non-existence requires a preponderator. Likewise, capability (القدرة) is equally related to act and omission; thus preponderance of one over the other requires a preponderator. Then an objection is raised: the difference is that for a spatial substance, suspending specification on an external factor does not lead to impossibility (like infinite regress), but for a capable agent, it would make the agent necessitated (موجب) when the motive arises, which contradicts free will. The response (though cut off at page end) begins to explain that the decisive motive does make the act necessary in occurrence, but the agent is still the agent even during that state—the argument likely continues that this does not destroy agency because the agent's capability remains. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the core argument is that the same logic applies uniformly to all cases of possibility, and the objection based on necessitation is dealt with by distinguishing the nature of the necessity from coercion.

الإمكان
الجواز
المرجح
القادر

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the principle of possibility is not uniform across different types of entities.
Correction: The author argues that there is no rational difference between cases like substance location, possible existence, and capability in terms of the need for a preponderator.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, what is the consequence of the uniformity of possibility?
A) A preponderator is needed only for spatial substances.
B) A preponderator is needed for all cases involving possibility.
C) A preponderator is never needed.
D) A preponderator is needed only for possible beings, not for capabilities.

Key Takeaway
The principle of possibility and permissibility is uniform, so if a preponderator is required in one case, it must be required in all similar cases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are your choices truly self-caused, or do they also depend on a preponderator beyond your conscious will? How will you reconcile this with your daily experience of decision-making?
?
The principle demands logical consistency: no exceptions without a reason. In your life, are there areas where you claim exceptions for yourself—like thinking you can change a habit by sheer will while requiring external causes for others? What would Sanusi say to you about the need for consistency?
?
Sanusi challenges his opponents to accept the same logic they apply to substances to their own capability. When you think about your faith, do you demand proof for some beliefs but not others? Will you commit to applying the same standard of reasoning to all of your convictions, even the uncomfortable ones?

The Capable Agent and the Preponderator: Answering the Objection
Sanusi presents and begins to refute the objection that a preponderator would make the agent necessitated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' objection that a preponderator for a capable agent leads to necessitation, and how Sanusi begins to refute it by distinguishing the decisive motive from coercion.

Core Idea
The objection that a preponderator turns the agent into a necessitator is refuted by the role of decisive motive.

Having argued for uniformity, Sanusi now addresses a key objection: that requiring a preponderator for a capable agent would lead to necessitation, destroying free will. He anticipates this and begins explaining why the decisive motive does not undermine agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection from 'فإن قالوا' to understand the opponents' claimed difference between spatial substance and capable agent.
The opponents argue that for a spatial substance, suspending specification does not lead to absurdity, but for a capable agent it does.
2
Understand the alleged absurdity
The opponents claim that if the agent's choice depends on an external factor, then when that factor occurs, the agent becomes a necessitator (موجب), which contradicts free will.
The key term is 'الداعية الجازمة' (decisive motive).
3
See the beginning of the response
Sanusi begins his reply by clarifying that the decisive motive makes the act necessary in occurrence, but the agent remains the agent.
The passage cuts off, but the argument likely continues to show that this necessity is compatible with agency.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the opponents' argument fails: the necessity from a decisive motive does not negate capability; rather, it is the actualization of choice.
This is a classic debate about determinism and free will.

الحجة الثالثة: إن قضية الإمكان والجواز قضية واحدة. فإن كان المفهوم من الجواز والإمكان محوجاً إلى المرجح، فليكن كذلك في كل المواضع، وإن لم يكن محوجاً إلى المؤثر، فليكن كذلك في كل المواضع. فأما القول بأنه محوج إلى المؤثر في موضع دون موضع، مع أنا لا نجد في العقل فرقاً بين الموضعين، فذلك مما لا يقبله العقل. إذا عرفت هذا، فنقول: وافقتمونا على أن الجوهر، لما جاز حصوله في هذا الحيز بدلاً عن ذلك، وفي ذاك بدلاً عن هذا، فإنه يمتنع رجحان أحدهما على الآخر، إلا المرجح. وأيضاً وافقتمونا على أن الممكن لما جاز وجوده، وجاز عدمه، فإنه يمتنع رجحان أحد الجانبين على الآخر إلا المرجح. فكذا هاهنا، لما كانت القدرة صالحة للفعل والترك، امتنع رجحان أحدهما على الآخر إلا المرجح. ضرورة أنه لا فرق بين هذه الصفة بأسرها في باب الجواز والإمكان. فإن قالوا: الفرق بين البابين أن كون الذات متحيزة، يقتضي صحة الحصول في كل الأحياز على البدل. وتوقيف التعيين على أمر منفصل لا يفضي إلى المحال. فلا جرم قلنا به. وأما في حق القادر، فإن القول بأن رجحان أحد الجانبين على الآخر يتوقف على أمر منفصل يفضي إلى المحال، فوجب أن لا نقول به. وبيان هذا الكلام إن كون القادر قادراً يقتضي صحة كل واحد من الضدين، على البدل. فلو حكمنا بتوقف التعيين على أمر منفصل، لكان عند حصول ذلك الأمر المنفصل يصير القادر موجباً. لأنا سنبين أن الفعل عند حدوث الداعية الجازمة، يصير واجب الوقوع، لكن القول بأن القادر حال كونه
The Third Proof: The principle of possibility (imkān) and permissibility (jawāz) is one principle. If the concept of permissibility and possibility necessitated the preponderator (al-murajjiḥ), then let it be so in all cases. And if it did not necessitate the effecter (al-muʾaththir), then let it be so in all cases. As for the statement that it necessitates the effecter in one case but not another, while we find no rational difference between the two cases, the intellect does not accept that. Once you know this, we say: You agree with us that for the substance (al-jawhar), since it is permissible for it to obtain in this location alternatively to that, and in that alternatively to this, then preponderance of one over the other is impossible except by a preponderator. Likewise, you agree with us that for the possible (al-mumkin), since its existence is permissible and its non-existence is permissible, then preponderance of one side over the other is impossible except by a preponderator. So too here, since capability (al-qudra) is fit for both act and omission, preponderance of one over the other is impossible except by a preponderator. Necessarily, there is no difference between all these cases in the category of permissibility and possibility. If they say: The difference between the two categories is that the essence being spatial (mutaḥayyiz) implies the validity of obtaining in all locations alternately. And suspending specification on a separate matter does not lead to impossibility; hence we say it. But for the capable agent, the statement that preponderance of one side over the other depends on a separate matter leads to impossibility, so we must not say it. The explanation of this statement is that the capable agent being capable implies the validity of each of the two opposites alternately. So if we judged that specification depends on a separate matter, then when that separate matter occurs, the capable agent would become necessitated (mūjib). For we will explain that the act, when the decisive motive (al-dāʿiya al-jāzima) occurs, becomes necessary of occurrence. But the statement that the capable agent when he is
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the necessity of a preponderator (المرجح) in the case of a capable agent. It is a classification and comparative argument. The author begins by stating that the principle of possibility (الإمكان) and permissibility (الجواز) is uniform. If permissibility necessitates a preponderator in one case, it must in all; if not, it never does. To claim a difference without rational basis is unacceptable. Then the author builds on agreed premises: you (opponents) agree that for a substance (الجوهر) to be in one location rather than another requires a preponderator, and for a possible being (الممكن) existence over non-existence requires a preponderator. Likewise, capability (القدرة) is equally related to act and omission; thus preponderance of one over the other requires a preponderator. Then an objection is raised: the difference is that for a spatial substance, suspending specification on an external factor does not lead to impossibility (like infinite regress), but for a capable agent, it would make the agent necessitated (موجب) when the motive arises, which contradicts free will. The response (though cut off at page end) begins to explain that the decisive motive does make the act necessary in occurrence, but the agent is still the agent even during that state—the argument likely continues that this does not destroy agency because the agent's capability remains. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the core argument is that the same logic applies uniformly to all cases of possibility, and the objection based on necessitation is dealt with by distinguishing the nature of the necessity from coercion.

Key Terms
القادر
الموجب
الداعية الجازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any external factor determining an agent's action destroys free will and makes the agent a non-agent.
Correction: Sanusi argues that the decisive motive, though external, does not negate agency because it is the motive that makes the act necessary, not coercion.

Checkpoint
What is the opponents' main objection to applying the preponderator to the capable agent?
A) It would lead to an infinite regress.
B) It would make the agent a necessitator (موجب).
C) It contradicts the uniformity of possibility.
D) It is not supported by the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The objection that a preponderator would make the agent a necessitator is answered by noting that the decisive motive, while causing necessity, does not remove agency.

When you face a difficult decision, do you tend to look for external factors to blame if the outcome is unfavorable? What would it mean for you to take ownership of your decisive motive, even when it feels uncomfortable?
?
If the decisive motive makes the act necessary, then are you truly free? How does this lesson challenge or affirm your understanding of your own agency before Allah?

Why Motive is Necessary for Action: The Fourth Proof
Proof that capacity alone is insufficient; motive must intervene.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mere capacity for action is insufficient without a motive, and how the author uses logical implication and empirical example to prove it.

Core Idea
Capacity alone cannot produce action; a motive is necessary.

In this proof, the author demonstrates a critical principle: the agent's power (qudra) is not self-sufficient to bring about an action. He shows that if it were, then motives would be irrelevant—which contradicts our direct experience. This argument distinguishes capacity from the motive that activates it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the author's full argument.
Notice the conditional structure at the beginning.
2
Identify the logical structure
The argument proceeds as a conditional: if capacity were sufficient, then motive would have no effect. The author then proves the consequent false, so the antecedent is false.
This is a modus tollens argument.
3
Analyze the analogy and example
Examine the stone weight analogy and the empirical example of giving a dirham. They illustrate the independence of capacity and the actual effect of motive.
The example from everyday life shows we know by necessity that motive makes a difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة على أن الفعل بدون الداعي محال أن نقول: لو كان مجرد كون القادر قادراً كافياً في حصول الفعل، لامتنع أن يحصل للداعي أثر في الترجيح في شيء من المواضع. وهذا باطل. فذاك باطل. بيان الملازمة: أن الوصف إذا كان مستقلاً باقتضاء الأثر، فإذا انضم إليه شيء آخر، فقد انضم هذا الزائد إلى شيء كان في نفسه مستقلاً باقتضاء الأثر، وإذا كان كذلك، فقد وقع الأثر بذلك المستقل ولا يبقى لهذا الزائد فيه أثر البتة، لأن الشيء الواحد لا يكون واجباً لذاته ولغيره معاً. ومثاله: أنه لما كان ثقل الحجر مستقلاً باقتضاء الهوى، لا جرم لم يبق لكون الحجر أسود أو أبيض أثر في اقتضاء ذلك النزول. فكذا ههنا، لو كان مجرد كونه قادراً مستقلاً باقتضاء الفعل لامتنع أن يحصل للداعية المنضمة إلى القادرية أثر في اقتضاء الفعل. فيثبت بما ذكرنا أن القادرية لو كانت مستقلة باقتضاء الفعل لامتنع أن يكون للداعية أثر في اقتضاء الفعل. وبيان أن الداعية قد يكون لها أثر في الترجيح: أن ذلك معلوم بالضرورة، فإنا نعلم بالضرورة أنا قد ندفع الدرهم إلى الفقير لعلمنا بفقره، ونعلم بالضرورة أنه لولا هذا الاعتقاد لما دفعنا ذلك الدرهم إليه. فيثبت أنه لو كانت القادرية مستقلة باقتضاء الترجيح لامتنع أن يكون للداعية أثر في الترجيح، وثبت أن هذا اللازم باطل، فوجب أن يكون الملزوم باطلاً.
The fourth proof: That action without a motive is impossible. We say: If the mere fact that the capable is capable were sufficient for the occurrence of the action, it would be impossible for the motive to have any effect in preponderating in any situation. This is false, so that is false. Explanation of the implication: If a description is independently sufficient for producing an effect, then when something else is joined to it, this additional thing is joined to something that was in itself independently sufficient for producing the effect. If so, then the effect occurs by that independent thing, and there remains no effect whatsoever for this additional thing, because the single thing cannot be necessary by itself and by another at the same time. Its example: Since the weight of a stone is independently sufficient for producing downward motion, there remains no effect for the stone being black or white in producing that descent. Similarly here, if mere capacity were independently sufficient for producing the action, it would be impossible for the motive added to capacity to have any effect in producing the action. Thus, from what we have mentioned, it is established that if capacity were independently sufficient for producing the action, it would be impossible for the motive to have any effect in producing the action. And the proof that the motive may have an effect in preponderating: that is known by necessity. For we know by necessity that we give a dirham to the poor because of our knowledge of his poverty, and we know by necessity that without this belief we would not have given that dirham to him. So it is established that if capacity were independently sufficient for producing preponderating, it would be impossible for the motive to have any effect in preponderating. And it is established that this consequent is false, so the antecedent must be false.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof (al-hujja al-rabi'a) that action without a motive is impossible. The argument begins with a conditional: if the mere capacity (qudra) of the agent were sufficient for action, then the motive (da'iya) would have no effect in tilting the scale (tarjih) towards action. Since we know that motives do have an effect, the antecedent is false. The author then explains the implication (mulazama): if a description is independently sufficient for an effect, then adding another factor contributes nothing, because the effect is already fully realized. He gives the analogy of a stone's weight causing it to fall—the stone's colour has no effect on its falling. Similarly, if capacity were independently sufficient, the motive would be superfluous. But we know by necessity that motives affect our choices: we give a dirham to a poor person because we know of his poverty, and without that belief we would not give. Therefore, capacity is not independently sufficient; it requires a motive to produce action. Thus, the consequent being false, the antecedent is false.

Key Terms
القادرية (qudariyya, capacity)
الداعي (da'iya, motive)
الترجيح (tarjih, preponderating)
المستقل (mustaqill, independent)
الملازمة (mulazama, implication)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof shows that capacity has no role at all in causing action.
Correction: The proof only denies that capacity is independently sufficient; capacity still plays a necessary role, but it requires a motive to produce action.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to illustrate that if capacity were independently sufficient, the motive would have no effect?
A) The weight of a stone causing it to fall, regardless of its colour.
B) A person choosing between two cups.
C) God's choice among expiation options.
D) A person returning home upon remembering an important matter.

Key Takeaway
Capacity requires a motive to produce action; it is not independently sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of worship, do you ever stop to reflect on whether your motive is genuinely present, or are you merely going through the motions because you are capable? Write down one specific action you did today where you were aware of your motive, and one where you were not.
?
This lesson shows that capacity alone cannot produce action; a motive must intervene. In your life, what is the most powerful motive that drives you to act righteously? Is it fear of punishment, hope of reward, or love of Allah? Be honest: which one truly moves you to do good when you have no external pressure?
?
Consider a sin you committed despite knowing it was wrong. At that moment, your capacity to avoid it was present, but what motive was absent? What could you have done to strengthen that motive before the moment of decision? Write a concrete plan to fortify that motive for the next time you face a similar temptation.

Fifth Proof: Necessity of a Preponderating Factor
The author provides the fifth proof using three everyday examples to show the necessity of a preponderating factor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof that action requires a preponderating factor (murajjih) when motives are balanced, as demonstrated through everyday examples.

Core Idea
Action pauses when motives balance; only a preponderating factor moves the will.

This lesson presents the fifth proof from al-Matalib al-Aliyah, which uses common human experiences to demonstrate a rational necessity: when two motives are equal, action cannot proceed without a preponderating factor that tips the scale. The author provides three relatable examples—walking, choosing food, and selecting expiations—to show that this principle is self-evident from human experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and the translation to grasp the structure of the proof. Notice how the author builds the argument.
The proof is divided into examples and a conclusion.
2
Identify the three examples
Identify each example: the man walking, the choice between cups/loaves, and the expiation options. Notice that each shows hesitation when motives are equal, and action follows preponderance.
3
Understand the logical conclusion
The author concludes that the dependence of preponderance on a preponderating factor is known necessarily (daruri) from human experience. Ask yourself why he calls it necessary knowledge.
This connects to the broader kalam principle that actions require a preponderating factor.
4
Reflect on the larger argument
Consider how this proof supports not only human actions but also the necessity of a preponderating factor in divine actions, a key point in kalam.
The common misunderstanding is that it only applies to humans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة: إن الواحد منا إذا خرج من بيته وذهب إلى زيارة صديق له، فإذا خطر بباله في أثناء الطريق أنه حصل له مهم في بيته يقتضي رجوعه إلى البيت، فإذا تقابلت هاتان الداعيتان وتساوتا، ولم يحصل لأحد الجانبين رجحان على الآخر، فإن ذلك الإنسان يبقى في ذلك المكان ولا يمكنه أن يتحرك إلى أحد الجانبين، إلا إذا حصل في خياله رجحان لأحد الجانبين على الآخر فإنه عند ذلك الخيال يتحرك إلى ذلك الجانب. وإذا كان هذا المعنى مما يجده كل أحد من نفسه، علمنا أنه ما لم يحصل المرجح في الخيال والعقل فإنه يمتنع حصول الرجحان. وأيضاً: إذا خيّر الإنسان بين شرب أحد القدحين، وأكل أحد الرغيفين، فإنه ما لم يتأمل في أنه يأخذ هذا أو ذاك ويأكل هذا أو ذاك، فإنه لا يصدر عنه أحدهما، ولولا أنه لا بد من الترجيح، لما حصل ذلك القدر من التوقف. وأيضاً: فإن الله تعالى لما خير المكلف في باب الكفارات بين الخصال الثلاثة فالمكلف لا يقدم على واحد منها بعينه إلا إذا تفكر وتأمل في أن أيها أسهل عليه، وأيها أصعب عليه، ولولا أن الرجحان يتوقف على حصول المرجح من بعض الوجوه، لما كان الأمر كذلك. فيثبت أن العلم بتوقف هذا الرجحان على المرجح علم ضروري.
The fifth proof: That when one of us leaves his house and goes to visit a friend of his, then if it occurs to his mind on the way that he has an important matter in his house that requires his return home, then if these two motives confront each other and are equal, and no preponderance occurs for either side over the other, that person remains in that place and cannot move to either side unless a preponderance for one side occurs in his imagination. Then, at that imagination, he moves to that side. And if this meaning is something found by everyone from himself, we know that unless the preponderating factor occurs in imagination and intellect, the preponderance is impossible. Also: If a person is given a choice between drinking one of two cups and eating one of two loaves, then unless he deliberates about taking this or that and eating this or that, he does not perform either. And were it not that preponderance is necessary, that amount of hesitation would not have occurred. Also: God Almighty, when He gave the obligated person a choice in the matter of expiations among the three types, the obligated person does not proceed to one of them in particular unless he thinks and deliberates about which is easier for him and which is harder. And were it not that preponderance depends on the occurrence of a preponderating factor in some respects, it would not be so. So it is established that knowledge of the dependence of this preponderance on the preponderating factor is necessary knowledge.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof (al-hujja al-khamisa) that action requires a preponderating factor (murajjih) when motives conflict. The author provides three empirical examples to show that hesitation occurs when motives are balanced, and action only follows when a preponderance (tarjih) occurs in the mind. The first example: a person walking to visit a friend but remembering an important matter at home. If the two motives are equal, he remains stuck until one side becomes preponderant in his imagination, at which point he acts. The second example: a person choosing between two cups of drink or two loaves of bread—he hesitates until he deliberates. The third example: in Islamic law, a person choosing among three expiation options (kaffarat) does not act until he reflects on which is easier. These examples demonstrate that preponderance depends on a preponderating factor, and this knowledge is necessary (daruri), i.e., self-evident from human experience. Thus, the proof concludes that action is impossible without a preponderating factor.

Key Terms
الداعيتان (da'iyatan, two motives)
التساوي (tasawi, equality)
المرجح (murajjih, preponderating factor)
الرجحان (rujhan, preponderance)
التخيير (takhyeer, choice)
الكفارات (kaffarat, expiations)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof only applies to human actions, not to divine actions.
Correction: The proof establishes a rational principle that action requires a preponderating factor regardless of agent, thus it applies to both human and divine actions.

Checkpoint
Which of the following examples does the author NOT use in the fifth proof?
A) A person leaving home and then deciding to return.
B) Choosing between two cups of drink and two loaves of bread.
C) A stone falling due to its weight.
D) Choosing among three expiation options.

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof establishes that action requires a preponderating factor when motives are balanced, a principle known through necessary human experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you were truly torn between two conflicting desires—one pleasing to Allah and one tempting you otherwise. What was the preponderating factor that eventually moved you? Did you recognize it as such at the moment, or only in hindsight? How does this lesson challenge you to consciously seek preponderating factors from your faith when you are in doubt?
?
The author says this knowledge is 'necessary' and found in every person. Yet we often act impulsively without recognizing the motives behind our choices. Have you ever blamed your circumstances or others for your actions, ignoring the inner preponderating factor? How does this proof place the responsibility for your actions back on your own deliberation and will?
?
If every deliberate action requires a preponderating factor, then what does it mean when you fail to act on what you know is right? Is it because the preponderating factor for obedience is weak? What can you do to strengthen that factor before the moment of choice arrives?

The Fifth Proof: Necessity of a Preponderating Factor
Razi presents the fifth proof that actions require a preponderating motive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's empirical proof that voluntary actions require a preponderating factor (motive) in the mind, and why this is considered necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
Every voluntary action requires a preponderating motive—without it, action is impossible.

Razi presents a compelling introspective proof that every choice we make depends on a factor that tips the balance in our mind. Using everyday examples—a person torn between continuing a trip or returning home, or choosing between two identical cups—he shows that without a preponderating motive, we remain stuck. This proof establishes a foundational principle in causation: the necessity of a preponderating factor (مرجح) for any voluntary action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the three examples Razi gives.
The passage uses a first-person introspective style.
2
Identify the core argument
The first example (leaving home, meeting friend, recalling urgent task) is the main proof. Understand how it shows that equal motives lead to inaction.
Focus on the psychological moment of hesitation.
3
See the supporting examples
The second and third examples (choosing cups/loaves, expiation options) reinforce the point with different contexts. Notice how hesitation indicates the need for a preponderating factor.
These examples show the principle applies universally.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Razi concludes that the dependence of preponderance on a preponderating factor is necessary knowledge—self-evident to everyone. Why is this important for understanding causality?
This conclusion grounds the proof in shared human experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن الواحد منا إذا خرج من بيته وذهب إلى زيارة صديق له ، فإذا خطر بباله في أثناء الطريق أنه حصل له مهم في بيته يقتضي رجوعه إلى البيت ، فإذا تقابلت هاتان الداعيتان وتساوتا ، ولم يحصل لأحد الجانبين رجحان على الآخر ، فإن ذلك الإنسان يبقى في ذلك المكان ولا يمكنه أن يتحرك إلى أحد الجانبين ، إلا إذا حصل في خياله رجحان لأحد الجانبين على الآخر فإنه عند ذلك الخيال يتحرك إلى ذلك الجانب . وإذا كان هذا المعنى مما يجده كل أحد من نفسه ، علمنا أنه ما لم يحصل المرجح في الخيال والعقل فإنه يمتنع حصول الرجحان .

وأيضاً : إذا خيّر الإنسان بين شرب أحد القدحين ، وأكل أحد الرغيفين ، فإنه ما لم يتأمل في أنه يأخذ هذا ، أو ذاك ويأكل هذا أو ذاك ، فإنه لا يصدر عنه أحدهما ، ولولا أنه لا بد من الترجيح ، لما حصل ذلك القدر من التوقف .

وأيضاً : فإن الله تعالى لما خير المكلف في باب الكفارات بين الخصال الثلاثة فالمكلف ، لا يقدم على واحد منها بعينه إلا إذا تفكر وتأمل في أن أيها أسهل عليه ، وأيها أصعب عليه ، ولولا أن الرجحان يتوقف على حصول المرجح من بعض الوجوه ، لما كان الأمر كذلك فيثبت أن العلم بتوقف هذا الرجحان على المرجح علم ضروري.
The Fifth Proof: Indeed, when one of us leaves his house and goes to visit a friend of his, and if it occurs to his mind along the way that he has an important matter in his house that requires his return home, then when these two motives confront each other and are equal, and no preponderance for one side over the other occurs, then that person remains in that place and cannot move toward either side unless a preponderance for one side over the other occurs in his imagination; for at that imagination he moves toward that side. And if this meaning is something that everyone finds in himself, we know that unless the preponderating factor occurs in imagination and intellect, the occurrence of preponderance is impossible. Also: if a person is given a choice between drinking one of two cups and eating one of two loaves, then unless he deliberates whether he takes this or that and eats this or that, neither of them proceeds from him. And were it not that preponderance is necessary, that amount of hesitation would not occur. Also: God Almighty, when He gave the obligated person a choice in the matter of expiations among the three characteristics, the obligated person does not proceed to a particular one of them unless he reflects and considers which is easier for him and which is more difficult for him. And were it not that preponderance depends on the occurrence of a preponderating factor in some respects, the matter would not be so. Thus it is established that knowledge of the dependence of this preponderance on a preponderating factor is necessary knowledge.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof that voluntary actions require a motivating factor (داعي) and a preponderating element. The argument is empirical and introspective. Razi begins with a common experience: a person leaving home to visit a friend suddenly remembers an urgent task requiring return. Two motives (continue to friend vs. return home) confront each other. When they are equal, the person remains stuck, unable to move. Only when a preponderance (رجحان) occurs in the imagination—a stronger inclination—does he move. This is a psychological observation that everyone can verify in themselves. The conclusion is that unless a preponderating factor (مرجح) appears in the faculty of imagination and reason, no action emerges. Razi then provides two supporting examples: (1) a person choosing between two cups and two loaves will hesitate until deliberation yields a decisive preference; (2) in Islamic law, a person choosing among three expiatory options will only pick one after considering which is easier or harder. In both cases, preponderance depends on some factor. Therefore, the knowledge that preponderance depends on a preponderating factor is necessary knowledge (علم ضروري), i.e., self-evident. This proof refutes the position that a capable agent can act without a motive.

Key Terms
داعي (motive)
ترجيح (preponderance)
مرجح (preponderating factor)
علم ضروري (necessary knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument proves that all actions are determined by external forces, eliminating free will.
Correction: Razi's proof only shows that a motive or reason is necessary for choice, not that the motive compels action against one's will. It is a description of psychological causation, not a denial of agency.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth proof, what happens when two motives are equal and no preponderance occurs?
A) The person chooses both actions.
B) The person remains unable to move.
C) The person chooses randomly.
D) The person chooses the easier action.

Key Takeaway
Razi's fifth proof demonstrates through introspection that no voluntary action occurs without a preponderating motive, making this a foundational principle of causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent moment when you stood frozen, unable to choose between two options. Can you identify the preponderating factor that finally moved you? How does this lesson expose the hidden motives behind your everyday decisions? Challenge yourself to notice such moments this week and ask: 'What is the murajjiḥ here?'
?
You may have believed that some of your choices are arbitrary or 'just because.' But Razi insists that every voluntary act has a preponderating factor. Search your heart: Are you willing to admit that even your 'spontaneous' decisions have hidden reasons? Will you commit to examining the real motives behind your actions, especially those you prefer to see as free without accountability?
?
If the preponderating factor is necessary for action, then your choices are not random—they reflect what you truly consider better or more important. What does that say about the priorities you reveal in your daily choices? Are you content with what your decisions show about your values? If not, what preponderating factor do you need to inject into your mind to change?

The Sixth Proof: First Consequence – God's Praiseworthiness
Razi details the first consequence of denying motive necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying the necessity of a motive for acts leads to the absurd conclusion that God would not be deserving of praise for His beneficial actions.

Core Idea
Without a motive, divine actions become arbitrary and praiseworthiness collapses.

Razi begins his sixth proof by showing the first of three impossible consequences that follow if acts could occur without a motive. He argues that if God's beneficial acts might not be motivated by benevolence, then we could not be certain He deserves praise for them, which is clearly false. This lesson unpacks that argument and its strengthening by the principle of original absence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then its literal translation. Notice how Razi sets up the reductio ad absurdum by listing three consequences before detailing the first.
Pay attention to the phrase 'All these consequences are false, so the antecedent is also false.'
2
Identify the first consequence
Focus on the part starting with 'أما الأول'. Razi explains that if an act can occur without a motive, we cannot be sure God created beneficial things for a motive of benevolence. Therefore, He would not necessarily deserve praise.
The strengthening argument using 'الأصل' reinforces the point by defaulting to absence of motive.
3
Reflect on the logical structure
Ask yourself: Why is this consequence considered false? Razi assumes it is self-evident that God deserves praise. If the premise leads to a contradiction, the premise must be rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : إنه لم يتوقف الفعل على الداعي ، لزم أن لا يكون الله تعالى مستحقاً للحمد والثناء على شيء من أفعاله أصلا ، ويلزم أن لا تدل خلق المعجزات على صدق الداعي أصلا، ويلزم أيضاً : أن لا يدل صدور الكلام من المتكلم على معنى من المعاني أصلاً . وكل هذه اللوازم باطلة فذلك الملزوم أيضاً باطل

أما الأول فلأنه إذا جاز أن يصدر الفعل عن القادر ، لا لداعي ، لامتنع أن يقال : إنه تعالى ، وإن خلق الأشياء النافعة إلا أنه خلقها ، لا لداعية أصلاً ، وإذا كان كذلك فحينئذ ما خلقها لداعية الإحسان ، لأن داعية الإحسان داعية بكيفية وعند انتفاء أصل الداعي، لا تكون الداعية المكيفة حاصلة . وإذا ثبت احتمال أنه تعالى ما خلق هذه الأشياء لداعية الإحسان وجب أن لا يستحق على شيء من أفعاله الحمد والثناء . ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل

•

ومما يقوي هذا الكلام : أن الأصل في كل أمر بقاؤه على ما كان والأصل عدم خلق الأشياء لداعية الإحسان . فإذا حصل الخلق ، وكان التقدير أن حصول الخلق لا يتوقف على حصول الداعي، فيلزم أن تبقى حصول تلك الداعية على العدم الأصلي . وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه يلزم المطلوب . وأما الثاني فلأنه إذا جاز صدور الفعل عن القادر ، لالداعي ، جاز من الله تعالى أن يخلق المعجزات ، لا لشيء من الدواعي اصلاً ، وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه يخرج المعجز عن أن يدل على أنه تعالى إنما خلقه لغرض التصديق ، فحينئذ يخرج المعجز عن كونه دليلاً من الصدق . وأما الثالث فلأنه إذا جاز صدور الفعل عن القادر ، لا لداعي ، فلعل المتكلم بهذه الألفاظ وهذه الكلمات فعلها ، وأدخلها في الوجود ، لا لغرض الإفهام والتعريف ، ومع بقاء هذا الاحتمال فإنه يخرج هذا الكلام عن كونه مفيداً ، فيثبت أن القول بجواز صدور الفعل عن
The Sixth Proof: If the act were not dependent on the motive, it would necessarily follow that God Almighty is not deserving of praise and commendation for any of His acts at all, and it would necessarily follow that the creation of miracles does not indicate the truthfulness of the caller at all, and it would also necessarily follow that the occurrence of speech from a speaker does not indicate any meaning at all. And all these consequences are false, so the antecedent is also false.

As for the first: because if it is permissible for an act to proceed from the capable agent without a motive, then it would be impossible to say that He Almighty, although He created beneficial things, created them without any motive at all. And if that is the case, then He did not create them for a motive of benevolence, because the motive of benevolence is a motive with a certain quality, and when the principle motive is absent, the qualified motive does not occur. And if it is established that it is possible that He Almighty did not create these things for a motive of benevolence, then it must be that He is not deserving of praise and commendation for any of His acts. And it is known that this is false.

•

And what strengthens this speech is that the original principle in every matter is its remaining as it was, and the original principle is the absence of creation of things for a motive of benevolence. So if creation occurs, and the estimation is that the occurrence of creation does not depend on the occurrence of a motive, then it follows that the occurrence of that motive remains on the original non-existence. And on this estimation, the required conclusion follows. As for the second: because if it is permissible for an act to proceed from the capable agent without a motive, then it would be permissible for God Almighty to create miracles without any motive at all. And on this estimation, the miracle would cease to indicate that He Almighty only created it for the purpose of confirmation; thus the miracle would cease to be a proof of truthfulness. As for the third: because if it is permissible for an act to proceed from the capable agent without a motive, then perhaps the speaker of these words and phrases performed them and brought them into existence without the purpose of making understood and defining; and with the survival of this possibility, this speech would cease to be beneficial. Thus it is established that the statement that it is permissible for an act to proceed from...
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof, which uses reductio ad absurdum. Razi argues that if an act could occur without a motive (داعي), three impossible consequences would follow. First, God would not deserve praise (حمد وثناء) for His beneficial acts, because we cannot be sure they were done from benevolence (إحسان). But we know God is praiseworthy, so the premise is false. Razi strengthens this by noting the default assumption (الأصل) is that things remain as they were—absence of motive. Since creation occurred without a necessary motive, the motive of benevolence remains absent, leading to the conclusion that God is not praiseworthy—an obvious falsehood. Second, miracles (معجزات) would lose their evidential value for prophethood because they could occur without any purpose of confirming a prophet. Third, speech (كلام) would not convey meaning because the speaker might produce words without any intention of communication. All three consequences are untenable, so the original claim (that acts do not require a motive) is false. This proof defends the Ash'ari principle that acts must be motivated by a purpose (but not necessarily benefit for God).

Key Terms
داعي (motive)
إحسان (benevolence)
حمد (praise)
شكر? no, only حمد
معجز (miracle)
صدق (truthfulness)
كلام (speech)
إفهام (making understood)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof limits God's power or suggests God is forced to act.
Correction: Razi is not limiting God's power; he is arguing that arbitrary actions cannot be rationally attributed to a wise agent. The proof shows that without motives, actions lose their rational significance, which is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
What is the first consequence that Razi argues would follow if acts do not require a motive?
A) God would not be powerful.
B) God would not be deserving of praise.
C) Miracles would not occur.
D) Prophethood would be impossible.

Key Takeaway
If acts could occur without a motive, God's praiseworthiness would be undermined, proving that the denial of motive necessity is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial, do you truly believe God has a wise motive for it, or does your heart whisper that it might be arbitrary? How does Razi's proof expose the danger of thinking God acts without purpose? Commit today to search for His wisdom in every difficulty.
?
You praise God daily with al-Ḥamdulillāh. But is your praise based on the certainty that every blessing is intentionally given from His benevolence? Examine a recent blessing in your life: if you believed it came without a motive, would your praise still be genuine? Let your next ḥamd be a conscious affirmation of His purposeful goodness.
?
Have you ever doubted that your prayers or good deeds carry meaning before God? Razi argues that without motives, even speech loses meaning. How does this truth compel you to ensure that your worship is not just habit but arises from a genuine motive of seeking His pleasure? Resolve to renew your intention before every act of obedience.

The Sixth Proof: Second Consequence – Miracles as Evidence
Razi presents the second consequence: miracles lose evidential value.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying the necessity of a motive for acts would render miracles useless as proof of a prophet's truthfulness.

Core Idea
Without motive, miracles lose their power to confirm prophethood.

Razi now turns to the second impossible consequence: if acts can occur without a motive, then God might create miracles without any purpose. But miracles are supposed to be signs confirming a prophet's truthfulness; if they could be arbitrary, they would cease to be evidence. This lesson examines Razi's concise yet powerful argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the second consequence
Read the Arabic starting from 'وأما الثاني' and its translation. Notice how Razi applies the same reasoning: permissibility of act without motive leads to miracles lacking purpose.
The key term here is 'معجز' (miracle) and 'صدق' (truthfulness).
2
Understand the logical link
Razi argues that if God can perform miracles without a motive, then the miracle might not be intended to confirm a prophet. Therefore, its occurrence does not reliably indicate truthfulness.
This undermines the evidential value of miracles for prophethood.
3
Connect to the overall proof
This consequence is considered clearly false because Muslims agree miracles are signs from God to confirm prophets. Thus, the premise that acts do not need motives must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

?
This teaching shows that even God's acts are deeply purposeful. Where in your life have you acted without real purpose, assuming that your actions still carry meaning? How can you align your daily choices with the conviction that actions require motives, just as this lesson argues?

The Sixth Proof: Third Consequence – Speech Becomes Meaningless
Razi presents the third consequence: speech loses meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying the necessity of a motive for acts would make speech arbitrary and unable to convey intended meaning.

Core Idea
Without motive, speech loses its power to communicate meaning.

Razi concludes his reductio ad absurdum with a third consequence: if acts can occur without a motive, then speech itself might be produced without any intention of communication. This would render speech meaningless and undermine all language. Although the passage is incomplete, Razi's point is clear: such a consequence is impossible, so the premise must be false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the third consequence
Read the Arabic starting from 'وأما الثالث' and its translation. Note that the Arabic ends mid-sentence, but the idea is complete enough.
The key term here is 'كلام' (speech) and 'إفهام' (making understood).
2
Analyze the argument
Razi argues that if acts can occur without a motive, then a speaker might utter words without any purpose of conveying meaning. If that possibility remains, speech loses its beneficial (useful) character.
The word 'مفيداً' means beneficial or meaningful.
3
Reflect on the absurdity
Consider the implication: if speech could be meaningless, then all communication would be unreliable. This is clearly false, so the original claim is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if that is the case, then He did not create them for a motive of benevolence, because the motive of benevolence is a motive with a certain quality, and when the principle motive is absent, the qualified motive does not occur. And if it is established that it is possible that He Almighty did not create these things for a motive of benevolence, then it must be that He is not deserving of praise and commendation for any of His acts. And it is known that this is false.

•

And what strengthens this speech is that the original principle in every matter is its remaining as it was, and the original principle is the absence of creation of things for a motive of benevolence. So if creation occurs, and the estimation is that the occurrence of creation does not depend on the occurrence of a motive, then it follows that the occurrence of that motive remains on the original non-existence. And on this estimation, the required conclusion follows. As for the second: because if it is permissible for an act to proceed from the capable agent without a motive, then it would be permissible for God Almighty to create miracles without any motive at all. And on this estimation, the miracle would cease to indicate that He Almighty only created it for the purpose of confirmation; thus the miracle would cease to be a proof of truthfulness. As for the third: because if it is permissible for an act to proceed from the capable agent without a motive, then perhaps the speaker of these words and phrases performed them and brought them into existence without the purpose of making understood and defining; and with the survival of this possibility, this speech would cease to be beneficial. Thus it is established that the statement that it is permissible for an act to proceed from...
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof, which uses reductio ad absurdum. Razi argues that if an act could occur without a motive (داعي), three impossible consequences would follow. First, God would not deserve praise (حمد وثناء) for His beneficial acts, because we cannot be sure they were done from benevolence (إحسان). But we know God is praiseworthy, so the premise is false. Razi strengthens this by noting the default assumption (الأصل) is that things remain as they were—absence of motive. Since creation occurred without a necessary motive, the motive of benevolence remains absent, leading to the conclusion that God is not praiseworthy—an obvious falsehood. Second, miracles (معجزات) would lose their evidential value for prophethood because they could occur without any purpose of confirming a prophet. Third, speech (كلام) would not convey meaning because the speaker might produce words without any intention of communication. All three consequences are untenable, so the original claim (that acts do not require a motive) is false. This proof defends the Ash'ari principle that acts must be motivated by a purpose (but not necessarily benefit for God).

Key Terms
داعي (motive)
كلام (speech)
إفهام (making understood)
مفيد (beneficial/meaningful)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof denies that speech can be meaningless or that God's speech is without purpose.
Correction: Razi is not denying that meaningless speech exists; he is arguing that if a speaker is rational and acts without motive, speech could become arbitrary, which contradicts the evident purposefulness of communication.

Checkpoint
What is the third consequence that Razi lists if acts do not require a motive?
A) Speech would not be heard.
B) Speech would not convey meaning.
C) Speech would be impossible.
D) Speech would be whispered.

Key Takeaway
Denying motive necessity undermines the very foundation of meaningful communication through speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you spoke without any purpose—just filling silence or out of habit? How did that affect your communication? What would it mean for your speech to always be for the purpose of making something understood (إفهام)?
?
If you believe that God's speech is always meaningful and purposeful, how should that transform the way you speak to others? Are your words filled with intentional benefit, or are they often empty?
?
Consider a recent conversation where your words might have been meaningless or misunderstood. What motive was missing? How can you ensure that your future speech is always tied to a genuine purpose of إفهام (communicating clearly) and تعريف (defining truth)?

First Refutation: Knowledge Does Not Change Power
Al-Razi's first argument that knowledge does not change the nature of power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Razi argues that knowledge does not nullify the capability of power to produce actions, thereby refuting the opponent's distinction between ignorant and knowledgeable agents.

Core Idea
Knowledge adds information, not impotence: power remains power even in the knowledgeable.

The opponent claims that only an ignorant agent can act without a motive; a knowledgeable agent requires a motive. Al-Razi begins his refutation by examining the relationship between knowledge and power. If power is inherently capable of producing action, adding knowledge does not strip that capability. This lesson unpacks that first argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's claim and Al-Razi's first response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Al-Razi directly responds to the opponent's argument.
The opponent's argument sets up the problem; Al-Razi's first response is the core of this lesson.
2
Understand the reasoning: knowledge does not alter power
Al-Razi argues that knowledge does not change the essence of power. If power is capable of action, that capability remains when knowledge is added.
This is a logical point: adding an attribute does not negate the essential property of another attribute.
3
Realize the implication: the distinction collapses
Since the knowledgeable agent retains the capability to act without a motive, the opponent's distinction between ignorant and knowledgeable agents is invalid.
The conclusion directly refutes the opponent's claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الكلام المعتمد للخصم في هذا الباب أن يقول : صدور الفعل عن القادر من غير الداعي، إنما يصح في حق الجاهل أما في حق العالم ، فإنه لا يصدر الفعل عنه إلا للداعي. هذا هو الجواب الذي عليه تعويل القوم

ونحن نقول : هذا باطل من وجهين :

الأول : إن العلم إذا انضم إلى القدرة ، فإن العلم لا يقلب حقيقة القدرة ولا يبطل ماهيتها ، فإذا كان مجرد القادرية صالحاً لأن يكون مصدراً للفعل ، وجب أن تبقى هذه الصلاحية أيضاً مع العلم وإذا كان كذلك فهذا يقتضي صحة صدور الفعل عن القادر العالم ، لا للداعي ، فيثبت أن الفرق الذي ذكروه باطل

والثاني : إنهم جوزوا الترجيح من غير مرجح في حق العالم ، ألا ترى أنهم جوزوا ترجيح أحد القدحين على الآخر . وترجيح أحد الرغيفين على الآخر ، لا لمرجح مع أن الفاعل في هذه الصور عالم . وأيضاً : جوزوا من الله تعالى إحداث العالم في وقت معين دون ما قبله وما بعده ، لا لمرجح . وأيضاً : جوزوا من الله تعالى تخصيص كل جوهر فرد بمحيز معين، دون سائر الأحياز ، لا لمرجح ، فبطل قولهم : إن صدور الفعل عن العالم من غير المرجح باطل وهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب . والله أعلم
And know that the main argument of the opponent in this chapter is that he says: The occurrence of an act from a capable agent without a motive is only valid with respect to the ignorant; as for the knowledgeable, an act does not occur from him except for a motive. This is the answer upon which the people rely.

And we say: This is false in two respects:

First: When knowledge is added to power, knowledge does not alter the reality of power nor does it nullify its essence. So if mere capability is suitable to be a source of action, then this suitability must remain also with knowledge. And if that is the case, then this necessitates the validity of the occurrence of an act from a capable knowledgeable agent without a motive, thus establishing that the distinction they mentioned is false.

Second: They permitted preponderance without a preponderator with respect to the knowledgeable. Do you not see that they permitted preferring one of two cups over the other, and preferring one of two loaves over the other, without a preponderator, while the agent in these cases is knowledgeable. Also, they permitted from God the origination of the world at a specific time and not before or after, without a preponderator. Also, they permitted from God the specification of each individual atom to a particular place, to the exclusion of other places, without a preponderator. Thus their statement that the occurrence of an act from the knowledgeable without a preponderator is false is nullified. And this is the completion of the discourse in this chapter. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its refutation. The opponent (al-khaṣm) argues that an action without a motive (dā'ī) is only possible for an ignorant (jāhil) agent, not for a knowledgeable ('ālim) agent. The author refutes this with two points. First, knowledge does not change the nature of power (qudra); power remains capable of action even with knowledge, so a knowledgeable agent can still act without a motive. Second, the opponent's own school (Ash'ari theology) allows preference (tarjīḥ) without a preponderator (murajjiḥ) in cases where the agent is knowledgeable, such as choosing between two equal cups or loaves, and also in divine actions like the creation of the world at a particular time and specifying particles to specific places. This contradicts their claim that a knowledgeable agent cannot act without a motive. Thus, the objection is nullified.

Key Terms
العلم
القدرة
القادرية
الصلاحية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge changes the nature of power, making it require a motive.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that knowledge does not alter the essence of power; capability remains unchanged.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of Al-Razi's first refutation?
A) Knowledge eliminates the need for power.
B) Knowledge does not change the essence of power.
C) The ignorant are more powerful than the knowledgeable.
D) Motives are irrelevant for action.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's first refutation shows that knowledge does not negate the inherent capability of power to produce action without a motive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have claimed, 'I know what I should do, but I just can't.' According to al-Razi, your knowledge does not strip you of your power. What if the real barrier is not your capability but your choice? Will you admit that you have the power and simply refuse to use it?
?
When you attribute your inaction to 'knowing too much' or 'being aware of complications,' are you not imitating the opponent's error? Al-Razi says knowledge does not nullify power. So what are you really afraid of?
?
If knowledge added to power does not change its suitability for action, then your awareness of sin does not make you powerless against it. Why then do you excuse yourself by saying 'I couldn't help it'? Is it possible you are hiding behind knowledge to evade responsibility?

Second Refutation: Opponents Contradict Themselves
Al-Razi's second argument: examples from opponents' system show they allow preference without a preponderator

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses examples from the opponent's own doctrines to show they already allow preference without a preponderator, contradicting their objection.

Core Idea
If the opponent allows preference without a preponderator in everyday and divine acts, their objection is inconsistent.

After establishing that knowledge does not remove capability, Al-Razi presents a second argument: the opponents themselves accept that knowledgeable agents can act without a motive in cases like choosing between two equal cups, and even in divine acts like creating the world at a specific time. This reveals a contradiction in their position. This lesson focuses on that second refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the examples Al-Razi provides
The passage lists several examples: choosing between two cups, choosing between two loaves, God's creation of the world at a specific time, and God's placement of atoms in specific places.
All these involve a knowledgeable agent acting without a preponderator.
2
Understand the contradiction
Al-Razi points out that in these examples, the opponents themselves accept that the agent (human or divine) can choose without a preponderator, even though the agent is knowledgeable. This contradicts their claim that a knowledgeable agent cannot act without a motive.
The contradiction shows their objection is inconsistent.
3
See the overall impact
By using the opponent's own accepted cases, Al-Razi demonstrates that their objection is based on a false assumption.
This argument is powerful because it turns the opponent's own theology against them.

Refutation of the Claim that Action Requires a Motive
Refutes the opponent's claim that a knowledgeable agent cannot act without a motive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational arguments proving that a knowledgeable capable agent can act without a motive, refuting the opponent's distinction between ignorant and knowledgeable agents.

Core Idea
Knowledge does not nullify capability; acting without a motive is always possible.

In this passage, the author directly tackles the opponent's assertion that only an ignorant agent can act without a motive. He advances two compelling logical arguments: first, knowledge does not alter the essence of capability; second, the opponents themselves admit to preference without a preponderant in various cases. This lesson unpacks these arguments, revealing the inconsistency in the opponent's position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the opponent's claim and the author's immediate rejection.
The passage is a refutation, so the structure is claim → counter-arguments.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Find the sentence where the opponent states that a knowledgeable agent cannot act without a motive. Understand that this is the position being refuted.
The opponent's claim is the basis for the entire discussion.
3
Examine the first argument
The author argues that knowledge does not change the reality of capability. Since capability alone suffices to produce an act, adding knowledge should not remove that sufficiency.
This is a direct logical rebuttal.
4
Examine the second argument
The author points out that the opponents themselves allow 'preference without a preponderant' in several cases where the agent is knowledgeable, such as choosing between identical cups or God's creation at a specific time.
This shows inconsistency in the opponent's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الكلام المعتمد للخصم في هذا الباب أن يقول: صدور الفعل عن القادر من غير الداعي، إنما يصح في حق الجاهل، وأما في حق العالم فإنه لا يصدر الفعل عنه إلا للداعي. هذا هو الجواب الذي عليه تعويل القوم. ونحن نقول: هذا باطل من وجهين: الأول: إن العلم إذا انضم إلى القدرة، فإن العلم لا يقلب حقيقة القدرة ولا يبطل ماهيتها، فإذا كان مجرد القادرية صالحاً لأن يكون مصدراً للفعل، وجب أن تبقى هذه الصلاحية أيضاً مع العلم. وإذا كان كذلك فهذا يقتضي صحة صدور الفعل عن القادر العالم، لا للداعي، فيثبت أن الفرق الذي ذكروه باطل. والثاني: إنهم جوزوا الترجيح من غير مرجح في حق العالم، ألا ترى أنهم جوزوا ترجيح أحد القدحين على الآخر، وترجيح أحد الرغيفين على الآخر، لا لمرجح مع أن الفاعل في هذه الصور عالم. وأيضاً: جوزوا من الله تعالى إحداث العالم في وقت معين دون ما قبله وما بعده، لا لمرجح. وأيضاً: جوزوا من الله تعالى تخصيص كل جوهر فرد بمحيز معين، دون سائر الأحياز، لا لمرجح، فبطل قولهم: إن صدور الفعل عن العالم من غير المرجح باطل. وهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب. والله أعلم
Know that the relied-upon argument of the opponent in this chapter is to say: The occurrence of an act from the capable (al-qadir) without a motive (al-daʿi) is only valid in the case of the ignorant. As for the case of the knowledgeable, the act does not occur from him except with a motive. This is the answer upon which the people rely. And we say: This is false in two respects: First: When knowledge is joined to capability, knowledge does not alter the reality of capability nor nullify its essence. So if mere capability is fit to be a source of the act, then it is necessary that this fitness remains also with knowledge. And if it is so, then this necessitates the validity of the occurrence of the act from the capable, knowledgeable agent without a motive, thus proving that the distinction they mentioned is false. Second: They have allowed preference without a preponderant (tarjīḥ bilā murjīḥ) in the case of the knowledgeable. Do you not see that they have allowed preferring one of two cups over the other, and preferring one of two loaves over the other, without a preponderant, while the agent in these cases is knowledgeable? Also: They have allowed from God the bringing about of the world at a specific time rather than before it or after it, without a preponderant. Also: They have allowed from God the specification of each indivisible substance (jawhar fard) to a particular location, to the exclusion of other locations, without a preponderant. Thus their statement is falsified: that the occurrence of the act from the knowledgeable without a preponderant is false. This completes the discussion in this chapter. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's argument regarding the necessity of a motive (daʿi) for action by a knowledgeable agent. The opponent claims that an act can occur from a capable agent without a motive only if the agent is ignorant; a knowledgeable agent always acts for a motive. The author declares this false for two reasons. First, he argues that knowledge does not change the essence of capability; if capability alone is sufficient to produce an act, then the addition of knowledge should not remove that sufficiency. Therefore, a knowledgeable capable agent can also act without a motive. Second, he points out that the opponents themselves allow preference without a preponderant (tarjīḥ bilā murjīḥ) in various cases where the agent is knowledgeable, such as choosing one of two identical cups or loaves without reason, and in theological matters, God's creation of the world at a specific time rather than another, and His assigning each atom to its location. Since they permit unjustified preference, their denial of action without a motive is inconsistent. Thus the opponent's distinction between ignorant and knowledgeable agents is falsified.

Key Terms
القادر (al-qadir)
الداعي (al-daʿi)
العلم (al-ʿilm)
القدرة (al-qudra)
الترجيح بلا مرجح (tarjīḥ bilā murjīḥ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A knowledgeable agent cannot act without a motive; knowledge removes the ability to act without reason.
Correction: Knowledge does not remove the inherent capability to act; it only adds awareness. The capability remains sufficient to produce an act, so acting without a motive is possible even for a knowledgeable agent.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument used to refute the opponent's claim that a knowledgeable agent cannot act without a motive?

B) Knowledge does not nullify capability, so capability remains fit to produce an act.
C) The opponent admits that ignorant agents can act without a motive, so the same applies to knowledgeable agents.
D) God's actions are always without a motive, so human actions can also be without a motive.

Key Takeaway
Acting without a motive is rationally possible for a knowledgeable agent because capability is not negated by knowledge, and the opponents' own allowance of preference without a preponderant undermines their claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever use your knowledge of good and evil as an excuse for inaction, telling yourself that you 'can't help it'? This lesson proves that capability alone is enough to act. What sin have you been justifying with 'I knew but couldn't stop'? Will you repent today, knowing that you have no excuse?
?
The opponents allowed God to act without a preponderant in creation, yet you try to rationalize Allah's decrees according to your own sense of fairness. When a trial befalls you, do you truly accept that Allah's actions need no cause beyond His will? Or do you secretly resent His choice? Ask yourself: can you submit to His preference without a motive?
?
If capability + knowledge does not force a motive, then your knowledge of Islam does not compel you to practice it—you still have free choice. Right now, are you using your knowledge as a shield for your laziness? The next prayer is coming. You have the power to pray and the knowledge it's obligatory. What's stopping you?

The Proof that Motivation Necessitates Obligation
Razi presents the first proof that motivation necessitates obligation, starting from the state of equality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that a sufficient motivation makes an action obligatory by necessity.

Core Idea
When motivation tips the scales, the opposite side becomes impossible, making the action necessary.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi refutes a claim that motivation only makes action more likely but not obligatory. He presents a crisp logical proof based on the categories of equal, preponderated, and preponderant. Before motivation, action and inaction were equal and thus action was impossible. When motivation occurs, one side becomes preponderant and the other preponderated. Since the preponderated is worse than equal, the preponderated side becomes even more impossible, forcing the other side to become necessary. This is the heart of the first proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the false claim and Razi's response
Notice that Razi first states the opposing view clearly before declaring it false. Then he announces proofs.
2
Understand the initial state of equality
The proof begins with the premise that before motivation, action and inaction are exactly equal (tasāwī). Razi asserts that at this state, action is impossible.
This is a key premise: equality leads to impossibility of occurrence.
3
Follow the shift to preponderance
When motivation (dā'ī) arises, it makes one side preponderant (rājiḥ) and the other side preponderated (marjūḥ). Compare the preponderated state to the equal state.
4
Apply the logical consequence
Since the equal state made action impossible, the preponderated state (weaker) makes it even more impossible. Thus the preponderated side becomes impossible, and the other side becomes necessary by the law of excluded middle.
This is the core of the proof.
5
Confirm the conclusion
Razi concludes that when motivation occurs, the action becomes obligated. Reflect on why this proof is considered decisive.
This directly refutes the claim that motivation does not necessitate obligation.
