Two Views of the Mutakallimun on the Determinant
Presents two sayings of the mutakallimun on the determinant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opposing positions within kalam regarding whether a capable agent requires a determinant for action.

Core Idea
The mutakallimun debated whether preference without a determinant is possible or impossible.

In this passage, the author presents two major responses from the mutakallimun to the objection that an effect must have a determinant. The first group accepts the necessity of a determinant but denies that it necessitates the act. The second group claims that preference without any determinant is possible for a capable agent, citing examples of free choice between identical options. Let's examine each view carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the two groups
Read the Arabic passage once, looking for the two distinct sayings. Notice how each is introduced.
2
Analyze the first group's claim
The first group concedes that preference without a determinant is impossible, but they argue that the determinant only makes the act more likely (awlawiyya), not necessary. Understand this nuance.
They want to avoid the conclusion that the agent is a necessitating cause.
3
Analyze the second group's claim and examples
The second group denies that preference without a determinant is impossible. They give examples like choosing between two identical cups. Understand their argument: if a determinant were necessary, the agent would be equivalent to a necessitating cause, which is absurd.
The examples are meant to show that choice can occur without a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن المتكلمين قولهم في هذا المقام قولان : منهم من سلم أن الرجحان بدون المرجح محال . إلا أنهم زعموا : أن عند حصول المرجح يصير الفعل أولى بالوقوع ، إلا أن تلك الأولوية ، لا تنتهي إلى حد الوجوب . ومنهم من قال : إن الرجحان بدون المرجح في حق القادر غير ممتنع ، وضربوا لذلك أمثلة : منها المخير بين شرب القدحين ، ومنها المخير بين أكل الرغيفين ، ومنها الهارب من السبع إذا وصل إلى مشعب الطريقين ، فإنه يختار أحدهما دون الآخر ، لا لمرجح . قالوا : ولا بد هنا من الاعتراف بإمكان أن يصدر عن القادر أحد مقدوريه، دون الآخر لا لمرجح ، إذ لو وقفنا به على انضمام المرجح إليه وقد ثبت أن عند انضمام المرجح إليه يصير واجب الوقوع فحينئذ يلزم أن لا يبقى فرق بين الموجب وبين القادر ، لكن العلم بهذا الفرق ضروري ، فوجب الاعتراف بأن القادر يمكنه ترجيح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر من غير مرجح . واعلم أن هذين القولين لا مزيد عليهما في الجواب عن ذلك السؤال . لأنه إن ثبت أن القادر لا يصدر عنه الأثر المعين إلا عند انضمام المخصص إليه . وثبت أن عند انضمام المخصص إليه يجب الفعل ، فحينئذ يتم السؤال ، ولا يبقى عنه جواب البتة، فيثبت أنه لا يمكن دفعه إلا بواسطة النزاع في إحدى المقدمتين ، إما أن يقال إن صدور الفعل من القادر لا يتوقف على انضمام المخصص إليه ، وهو قول من يقول : صدور الفعل عن القادر لا يتوقف على انضمام الداعي إليه. وإما أن يقال : إنه وإن توقف
And know that the mutakallimun have two sayings in this place: some of them conceded that preference without a determinant is impossible, except that they claimed that when the determinant occurs, the act becomes more likely to occur, but that likelihood does not reach the level of necessity. And others said that preference without a determinant on the part of the capable agent (al-qadir) is not impossible, and they gave examples for this: among them is one who is given a choice between drinking two cups, and among them is one who is given a choice between eating two loaves, and among them is one fleeing from a predator when he reaches a fork in two roads, for he chooses one of them without a determinant. They said: There is no escape here from acknowledging the possibility that from the capable agent one of his two potential actions may issue without a determinant. For if we make it dependent on the addition of a determinant, and it is established that when the determinant is added it becomes necessary of occurrence, then it would follow that no difference remains between the necessitating cause (al-mujib) and the capable agent. But knowledge of this difference is necessary, so it is necessary to acknowledge that the capable agent can prefer one of his two potential actions over the other without a determinant. And know that these two sayings are not sufficient as an answer to that question. Because if it is established that the capable agent does not issue the specific effect except upon the addition of the specifier (al-mukhassis), and it is established that upon the addition of the specifier the act becomes necessary, then the question is complete and there remains no answer at all. Thus it is established that it cannot be repelled except by disputing one of the two premises: either by saying that the issuance of the act from the capable agent does not depend on the addition of the specifier—and this is the saying of those who say that the issuance of the act from the capable agent does not depend on the addition of the motive (al-da‘i)—or by saying that even if it does depend...
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents two responses from the mutakallimun to the objection that an effect requires a determinant. The first group accepts that preference without a determinant is impossible, but argues that when the determinant is present, the act becomes only more likely (awlawiyya) but not necessary, thus avoiding the conclusion that the act is necessitated. The second group denies that preference without a determinant is impossible for a capable agent, giving examples of choice between two identical options (e.g., two cups, two loaves, a fork in the road) to show that the agent can choose without a cause. They argue that if the determinant were necessary, the agent would be equivalent to a necessitating cause, contradicting the obvious distinction. The author then refutes both views, stating that they do not adequately answer the question. If it is granted that the agent only acts when the specifier is present, and that with the specifier the act becomes necessary, then the objection stands. The only way to refute the objection is to deny one of the two premises: either that the agent's act depends on a specifier, or that with the specifier the act becomes necessary. The text ends by introducing the first possibility of denial.

Key Terms
مرجح (murajjih)
قادر (qadir)
مقدور (maqdur)
موجب (mujib)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that choice between identical options proves that no determinant is needed at all.
Correction: The author presents this as a claim of the second group, but he will later argue that neither view adequately answers the objection.

Checkpoint
According to the first saying, what is the effect of the determinant on the act?
A) It makes the act necessary.
B) It makes the act more likely, but not necessary.
C) It has no effect.
D) It makes the act impossible.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun are divided on whether a determinant is required for an agent's action, with some arguing it only increases likelihood and others arguing it is unnecessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a choice between two seemingly equal options, do you ever feel that your decision is truly free, or do you sense an unseen force tipping the scales? What does your answer reveal about your trust in your own will—and in God's will?
?
If God's actions required a determinant, would He still be worthy of worship as the All-Powerful? How does your view of His absolute freedom affect your gratitude and reliance on Him?
?
Consider a moment when you chose obedience over disobedience despite no external compulsion. Did you credit yourself for that choice, or did you see God's guidance in it? What would change if you acknowledged that even that preference could occur without a determinant—and that God alone is the ultimate source?

Refutation of the Two Views and the Way Out
Refutes both sayings and introduces necessary denials.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author finds both sayings inadequate and what premises must be denied to refute the objection.

Core Idea
Neither view resolves the problem; the only escape is to deny a key premise.

After presenting the two sayings, the author delivers a sharp critique. He argues that if we accept that the agent only acts with a specifier and that the specifier makes the act necessary, then the objection stands unchallenged. The only way out is to deny either that the act depends on a specifier or that the specifier necessitates the act. This lesson unpacks that critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's refutation
Read the passage from 'And know that these two sayings' to the end. Notice how the author states that neither saying suffices as an answer.
2
Understand the logical structure
The author sets up a conditional: if (a) the agent only acts with a specifier and (b) with the specifier the act becomes necessary, then the objection is complete and unanswerable. Identify these two premises.
The objection is that an effect requires a determinant.
3
Identify the two possible denials
The author says the only way to repel the objection is to dispute one of the two premises: either deny that the act depends on a specifier, or deny that with the specifier the act becomes necessary. Note that he introduces the first possibility but does not elaborate.
This sets up the next stage of the debate.

واعلم أن المتكلمين قولهم في هذا المقام قولان : منهم من سلم أن الرجحان بدون المرجح محال . إلا أنهم زعموا : أن عند حصول المرجح يصير الفعل أولى بالوقوع ، إلا أن تلك الأولوية ، لا تنتهي إلى حد الوجوب . ومنهم من قال : إن الرجحان بدون المرجح في حق القادر غير ممتنع ، وضربوا لذلك أمثلة : منها المخير بين شرب القدحين ، ومنها المخير بين أكل الرغيفين ، ومنها الهارب من السبع إذا وصل إلى مشعب الطريقين ، فإنه يختار أحدهما دون الآخر ، لا لمرجح . قالوا : ولا بد هنا من الاعتراف بإمكان أن يصدر عن القادر أحد مقدوريه، دون الآخر لا لمرجح ، إذ لو وقفنا به على انضمام المرجح إليه وقد ثبت أن عند انضمام المرجح إليه يصير واجب الوقوع فحينئذ يلزم أن لا يبقى فرق بين الموجب وبين القادر ، لكن العلم بهذا الفرق ضروري ، فوجب الاعتراف بأن القادر يمكنه ترجيح أحد مقدوريه على الآخر من غير مرجح . واعلم أن هذين القولين لا مزيد عليهما في الجواب عن ذلك السؤال . لأنه إن ثبت أن القادر لا يصدر عنه الأثر المعين إلا عند انضمام المخصص إليه . وثبت أن عند انضمام المخصص إليه يجب الفعل ، فحينئذ يتم السؤال ، ولا يبقى عنه جواب البتة، فيثبت أنه لا يمكن دفعه إلا بواسطة النزاع في إحدى المقدمتين ، إما أن يقال إن صدور الفعل من القادر لا يتوقف على انضمام المخصص إليه ، وهو قول من يقول : صدور الفعل عن القادر لا يتوقف على انضمام الداعي إليه. وإما أن يقال : إنه وإن توقف
And know that the mutakallimun have two sayings in this place: some of them conceded that preference without a determinant is impossible, except that they claimed that when the determinant occurs, the act becomes more likely to occur, but that likelihood does not reach the level of necessity. And others said that preference without a determinant on the part of the capable agent (al-qadir) is not impossible, and they gave examples for this: among them is one who is given a choice between drinking two cups, and among them is one who is given a choice between eating two loaves, and among them is one fleeing from a predator when he reaches a fork in two roads, for he chooses one of them without a determinant. They said: There is no escape here from acknowledging the possibility that from the capable agent one of his two potential actions may issue without a determinant. For if we make it dependent on the addition of a determinant, and it is established that when the determinant is added it becomes necessary of occurrence, then it would follow that no difference remains between the necessitating cause (al-mujib) and the capable agent. But knowledge of this difference is necessary, so it is necessary to acknowledge that the capable agent can prefer one of his two potential actions over the other without a determinant. And know that these two sayings are not sufficient as an answer to that question. Because if it is established that the capable agent does not issue the specific effect except upon the addition of the specifier (al-mukhassis), and it is established that upon the addition of the specifier the act becomes necessary, then the question is complete and there remains no answer at all. Thus it is established that it cannot be repelled except by disputing one of the two premises: either by saying that the issuance of the act from the capable agent does not depend on the addition of the specifier—and this is the saying of those who say that the issuance of the act from the capable agent does not depend on the addition of the motive (al-da‘i)—or by saying that even if it does depend...
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents two responses from the mutakallimun to the objection that an effect requires a determinant. The first group accepts that preference without a determinant is impossible, but argues that when the determinant is present, the act becomes only more likely (awlawiyya) but not necessary, thus avoiding the conclusion that the act is necessitated. The second group denies that preference without a determinant is impossible for a capable agent, giving examples of choice between two identical options (e.g., two cups, two loaves, a fork in the road) to show that the agent can choose without a cause. They argue that if the determinant were necessary, the agent would be equivalent to a necessitating cause, contradicting the obvious distinction. The author then refutes both views, stating that they do not adequately answer the question. If it is granted that the agent only acts when the specifier is present, and that with the specifier the act becomes necessary, then the objection stands. The only way to refute the objection is to deny one of the two premises: either that the agent's act depends on a specifier, or that with the specifier the act becomes necessary. The text ends by introducing the first possibility of denial.

Key Terms
مخصص (mukhassis)
داعي (da‘i)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the two sayings are sufficient answers to the objection.
Correction: The author explicitly states that they are not sufficient, because accepting the premises leaves the objection unanswered.

Checkpoint
What does the author say is required to escape the objection?
A) Accepting both sayings together.
B) Denying one of the two premises about the specifier and necessity.
C) Adding more examples.
D) Rejecting the distinction between agent and necessitating cause.

Key Takeaway
The author argues that the only way to refute the objection is to deny either the agent's dependence on a specifier or the necessitation caused by the specifier.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author argues that the two sayings fail because they accept premises that lead to an unanswerable question. In your own life, are there problems you repeatedly face because you are unwilling to question a deeply held premise or assumption? What would it cost you to challenge that premise?
?
The only escape here requires denying a premise—something foundational. Is there a belief about yourself or about God that you hold so tightly that it has become a prison? Are you prepared to re-examine it, even if it feels like it defines you?
?
This passage shows that sometimes the most honest move is to admit that a question has no answer given the starting points. Are there questions about your faith or your life that you have been forcing an answer to, when perhaps the true resolution lies in changing what you assume from the outset? Write down one such question and the premise you might need to reconsider.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
Objection and First Answer: World's Possibility in Pre-Eternity
Objection about the world's possibility in pre-eternity and the mutakallimun's first answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's first argument that God is a voluntary agent, based on the impossibility of an eternal world and the necessary causal connection.

Core Idea
God's pre-eternal existence does not force an eternal world; He is a voluntary agent.

This lesson presents a philosophical objection against the mutakallimun's proof that God is not a necessary agent, followed by their first response. The objection tries to force a dilemma: either the world was possible in pre-eternity or not. The mutakallimun answer by showing that the combination of two impossibilities—that an effect can lack its cause and that the world is pre-eternal—proves that the cause must be voluntary.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector presents two alternatives about the world's possibility in pre-eternity. Read carefully to see the dilemma.
Notice the phrase 'فإن قيل' introducing the objection.
2
Understand the dilemma
If the world was possible in pre-eternity, then it must be eternal. If it was impossible, then perhaps the pre-eternal cause did not necessitate it. Identify each horn.
The second alternative leads to the mutakallimun's response.
3
Examine the first answer
The mutakallimun combine two impossibilities: the effect's non-existence after the cause (impossible) and the world's pre-eternity (impossible). From this they deduce the cause was not necessary.
They argue that if the cause were necessary and existed in pre-eternity, the effect would have to exist in pre-eternity, but that is impossible.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since the cause is pre-eternal but the effect is temporal, the cause must be a voluntary agent (فاعل بالاختيار) rather than a necessary agent.
This preserves God's pre-eternity while affirming the world's temporality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: العالم إما أن يكون ممكن الوجود في الأزل، وإما أن لا يكون ممكن الوجود في الأزل. فإن كان ممكن الوجود في الأزل فحينئذ يلزم قدم العالم، وليس لكم أن تقولوا: إنه محال أن يكون العالم قديماً، لأن هذا التقدير هو تقدير أن كون العالم أزلياً، ليس بممتنع. وإن كان محال الوجود في الأزل. فنقول: على هذا التقدير، لم لا يجوز أن يقال: العلة القديمة لوجود العالم، وإن كانت موجودة في الأزل، لا يلزم منها كون العالم أزلياً، لأنه كما أن حال المؤثر معتبر في حصول الأثر، فكذلك حال القابل معتبر، وأولى الأحوال بالاعتبار كونه ممكناً في نفسه، فإذا كان الإمكان ثابتاً في الأزل، لم يلزم من حصول العلة الموجبة في الأزل حصول الأثر في الأزل؟ أجاب المتكلمون عنه من وجهين: الأول: أن نقول: تخلف المعلول عن العلة محال، وكون العالم قديماً أيضاً محال. وهذان المعنيان لما اجتمعا علمنا أن العلة الموجبة للعالم ما كانت موجوداً في الأزل. وقد كان المؤثر الأزلي موجوداً في الأزل، فعلمنا أن ذلك المؤثر ما كان موجباً بالذات. بل كان فاعلاً بالاختيار.
If it is said: The world is either possible of existence in pre-eternity, or not possible of existence in pre-eternity. If it is possible of existence in pre-eternity, then the pre-eternity of the world follows, and you cannot say that it is impossible for the world to be pre-eternal, because this supposition is a supposition that the world being pre-eternal is not impossible. And if it is impossible of existence in pre-eternity, then we say: On this supposition, why is it not permissible to say: The pre-eternal cause of the existence of the world, even if it existed in pre-eternity, does not necessitate the world being pre-eternal, because just as the state of the effecter is considered in the attainment of the effect, so also the state of the recipient is considered, and the state most worthy of consideration is its being possible in itself. So if possibility is established in pre-eternity, does it not follow from the existence of the necessitating cause in pre-eternity the existence of the effect in pre-eternity? The mutakallimun answered it in two ways: The first: That we say: The effect's non-existence subsequent to the cause is impossible, and the world being pre-eternal is also impossible. When these two meanings are combined, we know that the necessitating cause of the world was not existing in pre-eternity. And the pre-eternal effecter existed in pre-eternity, so we know that that effecter was not necessary by essence, but was a voluntary agent.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This is an objection (فإن قيل) against the proof that God is not a necessary agent. The objector proposes two alternatives: either the world is possible in pre-eternity (الأزل) or not. If it is possible, then why couldn't the necessary cause produce it in pre-eternity, leading to an eternal world? The mutakallimun answer that the effect's non-existence after the cause is impossible, so if the cause were necessary and present in pre-eternity, the effect would necessarily exist in pre-eternity. But the world is temporal, so the cause cannot have been necessary. Instead, the cause must be voluntary (فاعل بالاختيار), meaning it could choose to create at a later time. The first answer states that since both the impossibility of the effect's non-existence after the cause and the impossibility of the world's pre-eternity are true, we deduce that the cause was not existing in pre-eternity as a necessary cause, but as a voluntary agent. This preserves God's pre-eternity while affirming the world's temporality.

Key Terms
الأزل
ممكن الوجود
محال الوجود
علة موجبة
فاعل بالاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun deny that God's causality is eternal.
Correction: They affirm God's pre-eternal existence but deny that His causality necessitates an eternal effect. He is a voluntary agent who creates in time.

Checkpoint
The mutakallimun's first answer to the objection relies on the combination of which two impossibilities?
A) Impossible for effect to follow cause and impossible for God to exist.
B) Impossible for effect to follow cause and impossible for world to be pre-eternal.
C) Impossible for cause to precede effect and impossible for world to be temporal.
D) Impossible for God to be eternal and impossible for world to be temporal.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun's first answer shows that because an eternal world is impossible, the pre-eternal cause cannot be a necessary cause, hence it must be a voluntary agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God was not compelled to create you, but chose to do so out of His free will, how does that change the way you view your own existence and your obligations to Him? What would you say to Him tonight in prayer about this?
?
The objector tried to corner the mutakallimun with a logical dilemma, but God's action transcends human logic. Are there areas in your life where you try to box God into your expectations or demands, forgetting that He is the free, voluntary Lord? Will you repent of that today?
?
The mutakallimun's answer rests on combining two impossibilities. What impossible demands do you make of God in your heart—demands that He should act according to your timing or your understanding? How can you surrender those to His perfect will?
